HEADLINE: The World;
Call It Aid or a Bribe, It's the Price of Peace

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
AT the height of the cold war, Hans Morgenthau, the political theorist and champion of Realpolitik, identified the three tools of the statesman as logic, bribes and threats.
 Those may have been the power tools of his time, but no more. And the consequences are enormous. The United States has been left with immense power, but it doesn't quite know how to use it. Again and again, it seems unable to focus its authority and lead others toward a solution for the problems of the day. 
 Logic is not an easy tool to use in foreign policy now, because a shared ideology no longer unites the United States and its allies against a common adversary. Even a realist like former Secretary of State Henry A. Kissinger is at a loss to describe the world in a comprehensive way. He told a conference in Washington recently: "It's probably not possible to have one overarching concept."
 Threats of military intervention are less meaningful, too, because Americans have no stomach to sacrifice lives for what they think are lost causes rather than vital interests. And because the Pentagon resists mounting any military operation except with massive force, the price is usually too high.
 The 1991 gulf war aside (price tag: $61 billion), other figures prove the point. Saddam Hussein cost the United States and its Persian Gulf allies $500 million in a military buildup simply by moving some of his remaining troops and tanks to southern Iraq last fall. The Pentagon estimates that a NATO operation to protect a withdrawal of the 40,000 United Nations peacekeepers from Bosnia and Croatia would cost more than $1.5 billion, with the United States footing one-third to one-half of the bill.

 
 
Easy Bribes
 That leaves bribes as the easiest and most cost-effective way to change behavior and make peace. The irony is that at a time when peace is a bargain and a little money goes quite a long way, there is a global disinclination to spend. Budget constraints and the absence of a clearly identifiable threat make it harder to argue that investing in peace -- grants, loans and credits -- is an investment in security at a fraction of the price of war. Secretary of State Warren Christopher made that point in briefing President Clinton on this year's foreign aid budget request. His slide show included graphs that pointed out that it cost $215 million in 1993 to help pay for peacekeepers, elections and economic development in Cambodia, compared to $940 million for one week of war in Vietnam in 1968.

 
 
Bad Investments
 Of course, investing money in a place in hopes of peace doesn't always work. The United States spent $390 million in development investments in Somalia over three decades, only to be faced with almost $2 billion in costs for a 16-month emergency operation.
 Still, countries and movements that have taken risks for peace want the answer to a basic question: If peace is so much cheaper than war, where's the payoff?
 "There are neither the threats nor the ideology to mobilize people," said Stanley Hoffmann, chairman of the Center for European Studies at Harvard University. "So there are some bribes, but with very little money. That means there is no way you can assure a foreign leader, 'I have it in my pocket.' It limits your room for maneuver considerably."
 Take the case of Jordan. Had King Hussein followed Egypt's lead and made peace with Israel in 1979, he would not be in the humiliating position he finds himself in today. He is keenly aware that the United States, as part of the deal struck at Camp David, has since spent well over $75 billion in aid on Egypt and Israel; and even in today's slash-and-burn climate, their annual $5.1 billion in American aid -- nearly 40 percent of the entire aid budget -- is considered politically untouchable by the Congress (at least for now).
 So the King didn't seem to be asking for much in exchange for a peace treaty, just $488 million to pay off Jordan's debt from the United States. President Clinton promised he would, and delivered almost half last year. ("I'm not going to offer something that I can't deliver on," he told the King.) But Mr. Clinton did not push Congress to approve the remaining $275 million needed, and Congress approved only $50 million. Now the Administration may have to cut most other countries' aid by 20 percent if the President's promise to the Jordanians is to be fulfilled.
 Yasir Arafat is having similar money woes. The United States spent $100 million in 1994 and will spend another $100 million this year, hardly enough to build the housing, public service installations and administration that are sorely needed in Palestinian-run areas. Still, Washington has done better than many of its Arab allies. As punishment for Palestinian support for Iraq in the gulf war, Kuwait has paid Mr. Arafat only $10 million for his fledgling government. The United Arab Emirates has paid only $5 million and Qatar nothing. The result is that the World Bank-administered fund to cover Mr. Arafat's operating expenses is flat broke.

 
 
Who Cares?
 So what, say many of the Republicans who dominate Congress, where resistance to underwriting peace in the absence of a threat of war runs deep. Case in point: the recent decision in the House of Representatives to slash $110 million from the $160 million the Administration pledged for building housing for Russian soldiers in exchange for Moscow's troop withdrawal from the Baltics last year. Since $40 million has already been spent, it would end up costing the United States more than the remaining $10 million to break the contracts, pay the lawyers and come home.
 "I would invite you to come back to my district in Cincinnati some time, and perhaps justify paying for housing for former Soviet officers," Representative Steve Chabot, a freshman Republican from Ohio, snapped at Assistant Secretary Richard C. Holbrooke when he criticized the move during a recent hearing.
 Mr. Holbrooke replied that he would be happy to talk to the people of Cincinnati, wondering aloud why the Congressman could not understand that, after spending hundreds of billions of dollars during the cold war, $110 million was a small investment in peace.
 "We say that about everything, do we not?" Mr. Chabot replied. " 'It is only $110 million, you know.' "
 Granted, there are the exceptions. North Korea behaved badly by developing nuclear weapons; it was paid off with an American-brokered agreement creating an international consortium to give its government $4 billion in energy aid and light-water reactors in exchange for freezing and dismantling its nuclear weapons program. The agreement is in jeopardy not for lack of money, but because of North Korea's refusal to allow South Korea to build the reactors.
 But in most cases, the United States finds itself frustrated in trying to change the behavior of intractable leaders or reward those who take risks for peace when there is no possibility of a payoff.
 "For most of the postwar period the United States felt it had an obligation to create a system, and then to insure that it survives," said Charles William Maynes, the editor of Foreign Policy magazine, who predicted a decade ago that the ability to use logic, bribes and threats would diminish. "There was a sense of stewardship, that we had an obligation to protect the system, not just exploit it. Increasingly, we are exploiting it, and there is no steward left."

HEADLINE: IDEAS & TRENDS;
Still Looking to the Persian Gulf

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS 

 BODY:
ANYONE tempted to think it will be easy to keep restricting Iran's and Iraq's access to the world's oil markets might consider these facts:
 The Middle East holds two-thirds of the world's oil reserves. At the rate the world is increasing its use of oil, it will need 10 million more barrels a day by the year 2000. Some analysts and economists say the Middle East is the only place where that extra 10 million barrels a day can be brought into production fast enough. 
 And if American companies don't help develop it, others are certain to try.
 That means the United States is in for a difficult balancing act in what little remains of the 20th century: it will be more reliant than ever on Middle Eastern oil as it tries to pick and choose which of the region's countries should be allowed to produce and sell it.

 
 
Watch Prices Rise
 So the outlook seems to be for a gradual rise in prices and a lot of political arguments about the impact of oil deals. That is the real meaning of what happened this month when Conoco backed off from its deal to help Iran develop a new oil field.
 Past Administrations, of course, have grappled with similar quandaries as they sought to balance support for Israel with a need to keep relations open with Arab oil suppliers. Twenty years ago, though, there was a difference: a much more unified Arab world could threaten to withhold its products from American gasoline pumps.
 Now, in the aftermath of the Persian Gulf war, with Arab unity shattered and with oil prices long depressed, a new oil embargo is no longer a threat. What appears more likely is a creeping price rise as oil companies and Arab lands jockey for the financial resources with which to develop new oil fields.
 "There is no other place but the Middle East to add significant production," said John H. Licht-blau, the chairman of the Petroleum Industry Research Foundation, a consulting group.
The tens of billions of dollars that will be needed to bring proven fields into production can no longer be generated by the Middle East producers themselves. All of them, including Saudi Arabia, have been traumatized by the 1980's collapse of oil prices followed by the staggering cost of the gulf war itself, and they need what revenue remains to meet their own drives to modernize and satisfy social needs at home.
 How else could higher demand be met? Newly discovered fields in Colombia offer one hope, and Russia another.
 Western oil companies have shown particular interest in Russia's huge reserves, but efforts to make deals that will bring them into production have become mired in political infighting, even as Russian exports from existing fields decline. So while Western oil companies are in Russia for the long haul, it is uncertain when that country's exports will begin to recover.
 Meanwhile, even though oil companies have shown a surprising ability to squeeze more oil out of maturing fields (the North Sea is an example), American domestic production has been declining, as the output from fields in Alaska's North Slope declines and many other fields mature. Despite some new investment and exploration, particularly in the Gulf of Mexico, there is little hope the decline can be reversed.
 That leaves the Middle East. Eventually, some analysts believe, the major American oil companies will have to be allowed to help countries that have been branded sponsors of terrorism, such as Iran, Iraq and Libya, bring more fields into production.

 
 
Indulgences
 The alternative, they argue, would be tighter supplies and significantly higher prices -- and the political vulnerability such conditions bring. The industrial world still needs a steady flow of cheap oil for its economic well-being, not to mention indulgences like sport utility vehicles that deliver about 16 miles to the gallon.
 In fact, the dependency of the United States on oil imports has been growing. Last year it averaged about 8.9 million barrels a day or 45 percent, compared with about 30 percent in 1984. Even at moderate oil prices the United States spent about $45 billion last year to pay for imported oil, making it a major contributor to the trade deficit.
 There is some breathing room. Iraq, which can produce 4 million barrels a day, could put off a squeeze if the international community finds its behavior acceptable enough to re-admit it to the world market. Saudi Arabia also has spare capacity, which it is now holding back to support the drive by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries to raise oil prices by limiting production.
 None of this means that spiking the Conoco deal with Iran will have any immediate impact on oil supplies or prices, or, for that matter, on Iran's ability to expand production. Iran has already turned to a French oil company to pick up the deal that Conoco was shut out of, and American companies still have the option of turning increasingly to Saudi Arabia and Kuwait for supplies.
 In other words, while oil prices remain low and supplies plentiful, each side can pursue its politics with little economic risk. Even a bill being sought by Senator Alfonse D'Amato, the New York Republican, that would prevent American companies from buying oil from Iran, would initially change little. Those companies can now buy Iranian oil but cannot sell it directly in the United States. It seems reasonable to assume, therefore, that Iran would find other companies to sell that oil to the foreigners.
 By the end of the decade, however, the picture could be different. If the predicted squeeze develops, there will probably be more pressure to find a way to allow deals like the one Conoco wanted.
 As prices rise, the need to find some accommodation may seem more compelling.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "Leaning More on Middle Eastern Oil" Since 1973, U.S. dependence on oil from the Middle East has risen. Analysts expect that oil from the Persian Gulf area, which makes up the bulk of U.S. imports from the Middle East, will be 26% of all U.S. oil imports in the year 2000. Graph shows U.S. imports of oil from the Middle East from 1973 to 2000. (Source: Energy Information Administration)

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Mexico Lives by Virtual Law

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY

 BODY:
A KEY to understanding the social and political turmoil going on in Mexico these days is a terse phrase that President Ernesto Zedillo works into almost every one of his speeches. "Rules, clear rules" are what he asks for, telling the Mexican people that he wants to play by the rules in a country where the rules have long been ignored.
 The spectacular arrest several weeks ago of the elder brother of former President Carlos Salinas de Gortari on charges of planning a high-profile murder has become the symbol of Mr. Zedillo's vow to place no one above the law. His hard-line approach gained him immediate popularity in these tough economic times. 
 But is Mexican society and culture really ready for an end to impunity? Every nation has its scofflaws and criminals, but few have leaders who concede the way Mr. Zedillo has that the law has become a selective concept. Anyone who has lived in Mexico quickly learns that it has a kind of virtual legality.
 For instance, most streets in Mexico are lined with topes, or bone-jarring speed bumps. Most of those same streets also have traffic lights, stop signs and posted speed limits.
 But millions of Mexicans drivers know that hardly anyone pays attention to legal traffic controls. So they as a society have chosen to put up with the incessant stop-and-go traffic, damaged automobile front ends and rammed rear ends caused by the omnipresent speed bumps. To make it even more unreal, in some intersections the bumps are merely painted on the asphalt to give the impression they are there.

 
 
Timid Enforcer
 "The Mexican Government has become weaker and weaker, almost to the point of being shy, about imposing the rule of law," said Juan Zamora Pierce, president of the Mexican Bar Association. "Consequently, Mexicans realize that there is a rule of law, but that if you don't like it and if you don't obey it, nothing's going to happen."
 The bookshelves in Mr. Zamora Pierce's office are lined with law books filled with carefully written statutes based on Mexico's 1917 Constitution, considered one of the most enlightened in the world. In labor law, for example, Mexicans enjoy constitutionally guaranteed rights to dignified work and just salaries that even workers in the United States do not have. The problem is that the laws are almost never enforced -- one source of the fierce debate over whether the United States should sign the North American Free Trade Agreement with Mexico and Canada in 1993.
 The Constitution itself, while revered, is less than sacrosanct. It has been amended more than 400 times. If the United States Constitution had been tinkered with at the same rate, it would have more than 1,000 amendments instead of 27.

 
 
No More Mr. Nice Guy
 When he went on TV two weeks ago to give Mexicans a pep talk, Mr. Zedillo sounded like a Spanish-speaking Elliot Ness. "Nothing and no one will weaken my resolve to lead the construction of the true rule of law that the Mexican people deserve," he said.
 But which laws are to be applied? Santiago Creel, a lawyer who served as one of Mexico's first citizen watchdogs in last August's presidential election, says that Mr. Zedillo is playing a very dangerous game. He is pledging to enforce laws produced by Mexico's authoritarian Governments at a moment in which the country is trying to turn decisively toward democracy.
 Social needs often decide which laws get enforced and how. Huge and chaotic, Mexico City has laws preventing protesters from blocking streets or roads. But for much of the last decade the Government has allowed the poor to let off some steam by staging big demonstrations. Hardly a day goes by when one group or another hasn't backed up traffic for miles.
 It still takes a tip to get the garbage collected, or the telephone repaired. Less than half the homes in Mexico City pay their water bills. For a long time it was considered a sport to cheat on personal income taxes and from 1929 to 1988 the Government brought only two income tax evasion cases.
 Part of the problem is that those entrusted with enforcing the law are widely believed to be involved in breaking it, which Mr. Zedillo himself had to acknowledge recently when three state police officers stopped a jeep driven by his 19-year-old son Ernesto and, not knowing who he was, tried to rob him. Presidential bodyguards quickly stepped in. No wonder candidates here never promise to put more cops on the streets.
 Mexicans who have lived in the United States and then returned say they are amazed at how rigidly Americans follow the law. "They are like sheep," said a taxi driver, speeding through a red light. A merchant ridiculed Americans penchant for orderliness. "Where do they live? Disneyland?" he wanted to know.
 A middle-class couple in Mexico City neatly demonstrates how differently the law is viewed from both sides of the Rio Grande. He is a dentist, Mexican born, she is an artist, born in Texas. Recently they needed to fill out one of Mexico's innumerable legal forms, another false symbol of a state ruled by law. She tried first, standing on line for hours at a Government office until she ran out of patience. He took over, slipped the clerk 50 pesos, and was back home in 20 minutes.
 Mr. Zedillo has swept clean the Supreme Court and tried a legal route for ending the rebellion in Chiapas. But as he holds up a legal ideal, Mexico seems to have slipped into a pit of lawlessness. Two presidents, three federal prosecutors and three special investigators have, during the past two years, been unable to solve the murders of a cardinal, a president-to-be and a powerful politician.
 One of the investigators, Mario Ruiz Massieu, stands accused of falsifying files to remove the name of Raul Salinas in connection with the murder of Jose Francisco Ruiz Massieu, former secretary general of the ruling party and brother of Mario Ruiz Massieu.
 "Mexico has a solid legal system that is not working properly," Mr. Zedillo said last week in his office. "And that poses a problem." But a problem, it might now be asked, for whom?

GRAPHIC: Photo: The rule of law is not yet a full reality in Mexico, its President says. Security police outside a Government-run pawnshop in Mexico City. (Jesus Diaz for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: AMERICANS IN IRAQ GIVEN 8-YEAR TERM

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 25

 BODY:
An Iraqi court today sentenced two Americans to eight years in prison after convicting them on charges of entering the country illegally, the White House said. The Americans were arrested on March 13 near the Kuwaiti border.
The swiftness and severity of their punishment prompted strong condemnation from the Clinton Administration, which had warned only on Friday that Iraq could serve no purpose in holding the men. 
"There is no justification whatsoever for these sentences," the White House said in a terse statement this afternoon.
The Administration has said that the two men, William Barloon, 39, of New Hampton, Iowa, and David Daliberti, 41, of Jacksonville, Fla., civilian employees associated with the McDonnell Douglas Corporation, strayed into Iraq by mistake while visiting friends in the demilitarized zone between Iraq and Kuwait. While the White House has urged that they be released as a humanitarian gesture, Iraq has shown no sign of forgiveness.
Mr. Barloon and Mr. Daliberti were tried, convicted and sentenced today, according to the State Department, which said it had been so informed by the Polish Embassy in Baghdad, which represents American interests there.
The sentencing adds a major point of friction to the antagonistic relationship between Washington and Baghdad, which has become even more strained in recent weeks as the United States persuaded the United Nations to maintain economic sanctions first imposed on Iraq after it invaded Kuwait in 1990.
The timing of the sentencing perplexed French and Russian officials at the United Nations, who have been sympathetic to the Iraqis in their effort to get the sanctions overturned. Indeed, a major review of the sanctions is due in April.
They pointed out that the last time such a review was to take place, in October, the Iraqis began massing troops near the Kuwaiti border.
On Friday, even before the sentencing, American officials at the United Nations said they were determined not to allow the detention of the two men to affect their positions on various resolutions concerning Iraq. That included a resolution to be debated as early as the coming week allowing Iraq to sell up to a billion dollars worth of oil every 90 days to finance its emergency needs for food, medicine and other basic staples.
Only one American has been sentenced to prison in Iraq since the end of the Persian Gulf war of 1991. He was released after serving about six months of an eight-year sentence for illegal entry. But Administration officials expressed renewed concern today that Iraq might prove less flexible in this case, in the hope of trading Mr. Barloon and Mr. Daliberti for promises of more gentle treatment from the United Nations.
In a clear attempt to signal that the United States will not bargain, the State Department said today that it had made clear to Baghdad "that Iraq has nothing to gain through this obvious misguided attempt to take advantage of two Americans."
The State Department did not say which diplomatic channels it was using to communicate with Iraq. But Administration officials said that both the Polish Government and the United Nations had served as intermediaries since Mr. Barloon and Mr. Daliberti were arrested, and that they hoped that both would continue to press for an Iraqi change of heart.
Exactly how Mr. Barloon and Mr. Daliberti came to enter Iraq remains something of a mystery. No one has explained how they could have crossed a 10-foot-deep, 16-foot-wide trench built last year by Kuwait along the 130-mile-long border without realizing where they were.
But the Administration has said repeatedly that they acted by mistake, and the State Department repeated that declaration today. "They committed no offense justifying jail sentences," it said. "Mr. Barloon and Mr. Daliberti should be released immediately."
Polish diplomats who were permitted to visit the two men on Thursday reported that both were in good health. But American officials said today that the report also spoke of poor sanitation and food where the two men are being held.
According to The Associated Press, no American officials were present during the one-day trial. Two Polish diplomats who represent American interests in Iraq attended as observers, and the men were represented by a lawyer appointed by the Iraqi Government, according to people close to the case in Iraq who spoke on condition of anonymity.
The A.P. said news organizations were not informed in advance of the hearing, and the state-run Iraq News Agency carried no reports about it. Trials in Iraq are conducted before a judge but without a jury. The maximum sentence for illegally entering Iraq is 20 years in jail.
Both men were employed by a McDonnell Douglas subsidiary that has a contract with the Kuwaiti Government. Their families live in the United States, and the Administration said it was keeping them informed of the situation.

HEADLINE: California Acting on Affirmative Action

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr. 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, March 25

 BODY:
While the rest of the country mostly talks of doing something about affirmative action programs, opponents and proponents of such plans in California are moving rapidly to action.
Legislative bills have been drawn. Petitions are being readied. Fund-raising appeals have been mailed. Political consultants are being hired. Television advertisements are being filmed. Grass-roots coalitions are forming and holding rallies. 
 As a result, it now seems likely that by the fall of 1996, and maybe well before then, affirmative action will face an up-or-down vote in California, possibly at the legislative level, but more probably in a plebiscite.
And if polls mean anything, a plebiscite might very well make California the first state to outlaw programs that extend special economic and educational privileges on the basis of sex or race.
The latest surveys show that about 2 of every 3 of the state's voters favor a ban.
Sooner or later, Congress and the legislatures in some other states may get around to acting on the issue. In Washington on Friday, there was a hearing on affirmative action programs on Capitol Hill. On Thursday President Clinton reiterated that White House aides are reviewing programs for him.
But in California, action is superseding talk, meaning that once again the country's most populous and politically powerful state is forging to the fore on a major national issue, much as it did on changes on property taxes and term limits.
"There's a tremendous lot of serious stirring out there now," said Tom Wood, a conservative academic from Berkeley who, along with another San Francisco Bay area professor, Glynn Custred, inspired the drive. They gained instant prominence early this year when they proposed that California take the lead in a rollback of affirmative action programs by holding a voter-initiated referendum on the issue.
"We've got fund-raising letters out," Mr. Wood said. "We've got a political organizer on board. We're opening offices. Everything is go."
Opponents of the rollback are equally active, forming coalitions, drawing up strategies and hiring consultants.
"We're preparing for a fight to the death," said Elizabeth Toledo, the head of the California branch of the National Organization for Women. "We've just printed 40,000 fliers to mail out. We've just finished filming a commercial. We're lobbying like crazy. We're forming coalitions everywhere."
On Thursday, one coalition of a dozen organizations representing women, blacks, civil rights activists, unions and churches held a "No Retreat!" rally in downtown Los Angeles. Speakers warned the state's elected officials that a vote against affirmative action would be followed by political retribution.
"Angry white men -- please!" one demonstrator implored.
In the California capital, Sacramento, no fewer than 10 bills against affirmative action now await action by legislative committees in both the Senate and the Assembly. Hearings on several are set for April 4.
Some of the bills propose passage of laws that would forbid state and local officials from extending special employment and education privileges to anyone on the basis of race or sex. Others seek to have the Legislature put affirmative action on the next election ballot for a statewide vote of approval or disapproval.
From their office in Berkeley, Mr. Wood and Mr. Custred are working with several political consultants and a number of conservative groups, mostly Republican, to come up with a strategy to win passage of at least one of those bills.
Initially they plan to lobby the State Senate and Assembly to approve a statewide vote on the issue. But as the Senate leader, Bill Lockyer, put it the other day, that approach is "a real hard uphill climb" because ballot initiatives require approval by two-thirds of the membership of each house.
Of course, by simple majority votes the Senate and Assembly could pass a state law to ban affirmative action programs. But the Wood-Custred forces say they are wary of that approach because the law would be subject to various crippling amendments as it made its way through both houses.
So, if they fail to get the kind of action they want out of the California Legislature, Mr. Wood and Mr. Custred are prepared to gather enough petition signatures to put the affirmative action issue to a vote.
That approach -- they call it the California Civil Rights Initiative -- was actually what they initially had in mind before deciding to attempt instead the cheaper, less labor-intensive legislative approach.
Arnie Steinberg, a Los Angeles political consultant who is working with Mr. Wood and Mr. Custred, outlined the group's thinking this way: "We know it's iffy that we'll get the two-thirds vote we need to push a ballot initiative through both houses but we've got to try.
"It'll cost a million dollars to gather the 700,000 or so valid signatures needed to go the petition route. We can do that. After all, we've got the polls on our side and we can raise money not only in California but all over the country. So if we can pull it off, why not save our money and our organizing power for the campaign leading up to the vote?"
Presidential politics are adding to the confusion and heating up the debate. If the issue is decided by plebiscite, when should the vote occur?
California voters are next scheduled to go to the polls statewide on March 26, 1996, primary day. But some opponents of affirmative action are lobbying to have the issue placed on next year's November ballot.
They reason that it would then probably be a factor in the outcome of the Presidential race, causing particular trouble for the Democratic nominee.

HEADLINE: Countdown To Pataki Budget Threat

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK 

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 25

 BODY:
During almost two months of budget negotiations, Gov. George E. Pataki has sought to gain leverage with the Legislature by threatening to shut down state government if a budget is not enacted by April 1, the start of the fiscal year.
But a week before Mr. Pataki's doomsday deadline, the Republican Governor's warning seems to have created only modest pressure. And a tangle of legal, practical and political problems has led the Governor to reconsider his original plan, leaving it unclear whether he will act on his threat. 
 In an effort to force the first timely budget in 11 years, Mr. Pataki told legislators in January that if a budget was not in place by April 1, he would refuse to approve emergency appropriations to keep the government running, except for functions that "directly affect the health and safety of New Yorkers." Two weeks ago, budget officials raised the specter of widespread temporary layoffs by asking state agencies to determine which employees were essential and which were not.
But in the last week, as the prospects for a timely budget dwindled, the Governor has suggested he might limit his retribution to the more symbolic act of delaying paychecks for himself, the 211 legislators and their 4,462 aides.
That would still be a more strident move than any Governor has taken since 1981, when Hugh L. Carey, faced with a late budget, paid state workers in scrip. But a number of state officials said that Mr. Pataki's move would fall far short of his original threat and could undermine his credibility.
"I think it was unwise to propose it without having thought it through completely," said State Comptroller H. Carl McCall, a Democrat.
Clarence D. Rappleyea Jr., the Republican Assembly minority leader, said Mr. Pataki was "very cognizant of the fact that he made these statements, and so his first instinct is to do it. If he doesn't do it, I suppose there will be some credibility problem."
Mr. Pataki's original strategy borrowed from the theory of nuclear deterrence: threaten a cataclysm so that opponents would not risk allowing it to happen. But in many ways, the plan has backfired. Mr. Pataki's threat has alienated many of the state's 240,000 public employees, who have lived the last two weeks in fear of losing their paychecks. He has unified and mobilized their unions, which are busy devising legal strategies to fight him in court.
His threat has even dampened the already soggy Albany-area economy by prompting many state workers to defer major purchases, although there is evidence that restaurateurs and retailers have profited from the daily pilgrimages of anti-budget demonstrators to the capital.
Mr. Pataki's action has also invited public rebukes from prominent Republican legislators who represent the thousands of state workers in the Capital District, including the Senate majority leader, Joseph L. Bruno, of nearby Rensselaer County, and Senator Michael Hoblock of Albany.
"It's most unfortunate that anybody has to resort to tactics like making threats on innocent bystanders," Mr. Hoblock said. "I don't think it applies pressure on anybody."
Democratic legislators have taken to mocking Mr. Pataki's threat, convinced that a government shutdown would hurt him more than them. "I hope he's dumb enough to do it," said one prominent Assemblyman. "I think it blows up in his face."
In the latest Capitol parlor game, lobbyists and legislators are spinning scenarios about the implications of a government shutdown.
For instance, would drivers whose licenses expired during this period have to pay fines for tickets issued by city police officers?
Would the Department of Taxation and Finance continue to require the payment of income taxes by April 15 while refusing to process refunds?
Would the state create a cash crisis by temporarily laying off thousands of workers who would each be owed three weeks of deferred salary and accrued vacation pay?
"Once they look at the practical consequences of their slogans, the roof falls in on them," Assemblyman Richard L. Brodsky, a Westchester Democrat, said of the Governor's threat.
Mr. Pataki acknowledged in an interview this week that a shutdown would be fraught with complications. "It's not that simple when you actually have to be in position to do what you say you're going to do," he said.
The Governor said he definitely would not submit or sign the bills needed to pay employees of the Legislature and the Executive Chamber. Beyond that, he said, anything is possible.
"We are going to do ultimately not what is politically right but what we believe is in the best interest of the people of the state," he said.
One possibility is for Mr. Pataki to issue pink slips on April 3, the first work day after April 1, knowing that state law requires a 20-day notice to lay off workers. His gamble would be that the budget would be finished before the layoffs took effect.
Assembly Speaker Sheldon Silver, who is fighting with Mr. Pataki over deep spending cuts, said that a budget would be enacted "when it divides the pain evenly."
"Whether or not they get paid is not going to be a factor in when they think the budget is fair," Mr. Silver said.
Several lobbyists agreed with Mr. Silver that the impact of withholding legislative pay would be minimal, and possibly counterproductive.
"I don't think it will have a big impact, except that it's really going to tick off the Legislature," said Gene Russianoff, senior attorney for the New York Public Interest Research Group. "Up here, politics is personal, and they'll dig in."
Others said that even a broader shutdown might be slow to generate pressure on the Legislature, and could provoke anger toward Mr. Pataki.
"Most direct services don't come from the state and so when you say you're going to shut down state government that's a big yawn," said Norman Adler, a lobbyist and political consultant. "All you do is anger state workers, and they'd be hard pressed to blame the Legislature."

HEADLINE: Special Interests' Special Weapon

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT 

 BODY:
They look like grass-roots movements but are actually campaigns manufactured by special interests. They are becoming an increasingly popular way to try to influence Congress -- so popular, in fact, that there is a new name for them in Washington. They are called "Astroturf."
Unlike traditional lobbying efforts, which seek to sway politicians directly through office visits, position papers and campaign contributions, Astroturf efforts seek to influence them indirectly by changing the climate of public opinion, largely through television advertising. At a time when there is growing distrust of anything emanating from official Washington, Astroturf campaigns have the advantage of offering at least a semblance of populism. 
 "Folks in Washington are so discredited that nowadays one needs the embrace of 'the public,' " said Thomas Mann, a Congressional specialist at the Brookings Institution.
Experts attribute the growth of synthetic grass-roots movements to the apparent success of last year's Harry and Louise campaign, in which actors played a yuppie couple worried about President Clinton's health care initiative. The campaign, sponsored by the insurance industry, was widely credited with helping to turn public opinion against the Administration's plan.
"It's the logical conclusion of the health care debate," said Darrell West, a professor of political science at Brown University who studied the effects of the health care ads. "Interest groups learned that the best way to get their message out is by putting an ad on TV. They're trying to frighten members of Congress into believing there's a groundswell of support out there."
To be sure, interest groups' attempts to drum up public support for their positions are not new; advocacy advertising has long been standard in newspapers, and almost all lobbying campaigns include efforts to shape news coverage and, through it, public opinion. What is new, experts say, is the alacrity with which groups now mount television advertising campaigns to influence opinion, often without the public's ever becoming aware of who is doing the influencing.
"The lesson that people drew from Harry and Louise's putative success is that television advertising is a surrogate form of lobbying," said Kathleen Hall Jamieson, dean of the Annenberg School for Communication at the University of Pennsylvania. "You're going to see more of it, and on increasingly narrow issues."
One interest group clearly trying to match the success of Harry and Louise is made up of businesses favoring legislation that would discourage lawsuits by shareholders and consumers. When the House was debating the legislation, several groups backed by corporations launched television ad campaigns in support of the bills. The measures passed the House, although their fate in the Senate is uncertain.
In the last few weeks alone, several other groups have also mounted television campaigns to help stimulate grass-roots drives. They include the Republican National Committee, which ran commercials asking viewers to urge their Democratic senators to support the balanced-budget amendment, and U.S. Term Limits, a nonpartisan advocacy group that has run ads in several districts criticizing Republicans for backing away from the proposal to limit the tenure of Federal lawmakers.
In a particularly unusual campaign, former Representative Michael Huffington, the wealthy Republican businessman defeated by Senator Dianne Feinstein of California last year, is running television ads in her home state and in Washington, exhorting viewers to chastise her by telephone for her vote against the balanced-budget amendment.
The television campaigns are not restricted to national issues. In New York, for instance, a coalition of labor and health care groups has taken to the airwaves to criticize Gov. George E. Pataki's budget-cut proposals, while the state's Republican Party has mounted an ad campaign urging viewers to call Democratic legislators to voice support for the Governor's welfare plan. One union representing state workers, the Civil Service Employees Association, has been running a television ad explictly modeled on Harry and Louise; in it, a couple, Lou and Harriet, argue about Mr. Pataki's budget.
There is no way to quantify the amount of advocacy advertising on the air. Advertising is not an expense that lobbying groups are required to report, and most of the advocacy advertising appears on local stations rather than on the networks. The broadcast networks, in fact, generally do not accept advocacy ads, arguing that debates over public policy should be handled by their news departments, which can give a fair hearing to both sides.
But most people who follow the trends in political advertising -- politicians as well as political scientists -- say the amount of advertising on public policy issues is growing. "Twenty-five years ago national political campaigns were grass-roots efforts," said Carter Eskew, a Democratic political consultant. "Now national campaigns are 60 to 70 percent advertising. It may very well be that's where we're heading with issues, too."
Experts say one reason television advertising is so powerful a tool for shaping public opinion is that viewers become absorbed in the ads without knowing until the end the identity of the sponsor. Even then it is often unclear, because frequently the sponsor has formed a front group with an innocuous name. And no matter what the actual interests at stake, the ads are almost invariably cast in terms of just plain folks.
Last year's Harry and Louise ads, for instance, steered clear of any direct reference to the insurance industry's concerns, instead couching their opposition to the President's health care plan as a matter of consumer choice.
Similarly, this year's ads in favor of tort reform have avoided mention of corporate interests. Rather, they have framed the issue as one of ordinary citizens versus greedy lawyers and opportunistic plaintiffs.
An ad paid for by Citizens for a Sound Economy, for example -- a group that counts among its major donors companies like Cigna and R. J. Reynolds -- invoked the much-cited case of the Albuquerque woman who sued McDonald's for serving her scalding coffee. "Fewer people nowadays are willing to accept responsibility for their own doin's," an affable-looking old man says in the ad. "I tell you, the system is out of whack." At the end, the man urges viewers to call their Representative, who is mentioned by name according to the district in which the ad is appearing, and urge the legislator "to stand up to the lawyers."
Brent Bahler, a spokesman for Citizens for a Sound Economy, said the group had run the ad in 60 Congressional districts, at a cost "in the upper six figures."
"We've heard from people -- and I'm not going to name names -- that their phones were ringing off the hook," he said. "They asked us, 'Please stop the ads.' " Mr. Bahler said his group had complied only in districts where the Representatives agreed in writing to support the three-bill package. "Ten or eleven gave in," he said.
Lawmakers who were named in the group's ads acknowledged that the campaign had produced a significant number of calls. But they questioned whether the ads had really brought a shift in public opinion, or whether viewers even understood the message.
"The minute they started on television, we'd get up to 60 calls a day," said Representative Louise M. Slaughter, a Rochester Democrat. "But one-half to three-quarters of the people thought I was the one doing the ad."
Ms. Slaughter said the ads had not influenced her vote; she voted for two of the three bills but against the most sweeping one. Still, she said, she is concerned about the effect of such advertising campaigns on the political process. "There is a frenzy they whip up," she said. "And there's no countervailing message at all."
Political scientists who have studied advocacy advertising say the audience often takes away a message different from the one intended, or no message at all. Indeed, studies of the health care debate suggest that the Harry and Louise ads had little effect on the public; rather, it was the influence attributed to them by politicians and journalists that made them effective.
"The bottom line is we're not sure what the impact is," Ms. Jamieson, the dean at the Annenberg School, said of advocacy advertising in general. In the case of the Harry and Louise ads, she said, "members of Congress acted as if they were effective, so they were effective."

HEADLINE: White House In an Appeal To Women

BYLINE: By KAREN DE WITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 25

 BODY:
The White House, in an effort to energize female voters, has unfurled a strategy to contrast the fate of women and children under the Republican Congress with that under President Clinton's programs.
Women voted overwhelmingly for Mr. Clinton in the 1992 Presidential election, and Administration officials view them as a natural constituency for his policies. And although his approval rating among women slipped last October, to 42 percent from 54 percent in January 1994, it has risen in the last several weeks to 48 percent. 
 "We think we have not done a very good job of getting our message out," said Alexis Herman, White House director of public liaison. "We did not spend enough time reinforcing the issues that resonate with working women."
At a meeting with a dozen female journalists this week, Ms. Herman and two other Clinton Administration officials, Karen Nussbaum, director of the women's bureau at the Department of Labor, and Nancy-Ann Min, an associate director of the Office of Management and Budget, discussed how Republican budget cuts would affect poor and working women.
Like many Administration officials, the three insisted that the Republican move to balance the budget is taking money from programs like the Women, Infants and Children's program, the school lunch program and the rape crisis telephone line.
In addition, Ms. Min cited an amendment offered recently by Representative Ernest Jim Istook Jr., Republican of Oklahoma, that would gut a measure adopted in 1993 that allows Medicaid to pay for abortions for poor women whose lives are endangered or whose pregnancies are the result of rape or incest. Mr. Istook and his Republican supporters contend that the 1993 law contravenes state laws that prohibit the use of Federal money in such cases. The Istook amendment passed the House Appropriations Committee, 33 to 21.
In the debate over the amendment, Representative Nita M. Lowey, Democrat of Westchester, called the Republican argument a "smokescreen."
"The amendment is about denying poor victims an abortion in certain instances," she said. "It is Draconian, and it is extremist."
The female officials also played down the debate on affirmative action. They said women and minorities, both beneficiaries of such programs, should not be arguing over which group benefited the most.
"Look at the glass ceiling report," Ms. Nussbaum said, referring to the Federal Glass Ceiling Commission, which found that women are still largely absent from the upper reaches of business. "Women are still most likely to be among those who earn minimum wage."
Ms. Herman conceded that affirmative action should be discussed, but said the debate should be "with real data, real facts."
"We can't allow it to become the scapegoat for the real issues in the economy," she said.
These issues, the officials said, were the lack of growth in real wage earnings over the last 20 years.
Ms. Min said that in her talks with female voters, they expressed concern about balancing work and home, becoming the sole income earner if their husbands lost their jobs, paying for child care and health care, and caring for an elderly parent. The officials conceded that the Administration had not capitalized on its actions to aid working women, from proposing an increase in the minimum wage to passage of the Family and Leave Act.
They discounted the assertion that many women had not voted in the election last November, which saw Republicans wrest control of both houses of Congress, because they were disillusioned with Mr. Clinton and Democrats.
"A lot of what we heard from the women who stayed home is that they didn't feel as though the issues that were being talked about and debated were issues that they felt personally involved in," Ms. Herman said.
She added: "We're going to spend more time speaking to the issues that women care about."
The strategy is one that some political observers think will work.
"Women are far less likely to see government as enemy," said Ann Lewis, vice president for public policy for Planned Parenthood. "They see that it has important responsibilities in terms of education, health care and personal safety."
Judith Lichtman, president of the Women's Legal Defense Fund, agreed.
"It's the job of the President to make clear what's at stake -- from school lunches to reproductive choice," she said. "Women would be inclined to support him again to the extent that they did in '92 and greater because we have a lot to lose."

HEADLINE: Farmland Tax Benefits May Widen

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN 

DATELINE: TRENTON, March 21

 BODY:
If you want a tax break, get a horse.
That's the advice that people with a few acres of land may soon be getting if proposed amendments to New Jersey's 1964 farmland assessment law are approved. Among other changes, tax breaks would be given for land used for boarding, training or rehabilitating livestock, including horses. 
 Sponsors of the changes say they are needed to rescue an ailing horse industry and bring uniformity to taxing practices. But opponents say that the farmland assessment program has done more to benefit the wealthy than it has to preserve farmland. Instead of taking a hard look at loopholes in the program, they complain, the Legislature is preparing to vote on a series of measures that would widen them.
The current law was intended to preserve farmland and discourage overdevelopment in the nation's most densely populated state. To qualify for lower assessments and taxes, a property owner needs only five acres devoted to agricultural or horticultural activities that produce $500 a year in income. Since the program began, more than 28,000 properties covering about a quarter of the state's land area have qualified for the tax break, which can be tens of thousands of dollars each year. Although the number of farms decreased to 8,500 in 1994 from 26,900 in 1950, and farmland to 860,000 acres from 1.7 million acres in the same period, the rate of decline slowed abruptly when the assessment program started 30 years ago.
Some of that money has gone to Christine Todd Whitman, a fact that Jim Florio tried to exploit when she was challenging him for the job of governor in 1993. The Florio campaign never suggested that the Whitmans did anything improper, but it did try to stir up resentment -- and headlines -- in a tax-sensitive state by releasing documents showing the tax breaks they earned on their two New Jersey properties, in part by selling firewood to relatives.
Mr. Florio had promised to create a commission to take another look at the law, but he never got the chance. Instead, the Senate Agriculture Committee, headed by Robert W. Singer, a Republican of Lakewood, took up the issue.
After public hearings, the committee released the bill that adds boarding livestock to the list of activities for which farmland assessments can be sought.
Mr. Singer said the change was essential if the state's horse industry is to survive. "We could lose thousands of small horse farms," he said. "Years ago, stud fees were the mainstay of the industry and boarding was secondary, but that has changed."
The amendment has the support of the New Jersey Horse Council, the Standard Breeders Association and the New Jersey Farm Bureau.
"We consider this bill a series of minor adjustments," said Peter J. Furey, the Farm Bureau's administrator. "And the farming community is watching very carefully to see if the changes and how they are administered are consistent with an overall agriculture policy."
The amendments proposed for the bill would make several changes:
*Property owners would be allowed to keep their tax breaks if the person working the farm is unable to make the required $500 because of illness, injury or death.
*The land would be inspected every three years, instead of every year, to make sure it is still being used for farming.
*Categories of property that qualify for lower assessments would be broadened to include the land under buildings like farm markets and housing for migrant laborers.
When New Jersey enacted its farmland assessment program in 1964, it was the second state to do so; Illinois began a farmland program in the 1930's. Today nearly every state has one.
Mr. Furey of the Farm Bureau said that, contrary to common belief, New Jersey farmers pay more in property taxes than farmers in any other state -- even with the break on assessments.
"New Jersey farmers paid $40.35 per acre in taxes in 1991, according to the U.S. Department of Agriculture, while the national average was $5.51," Mr. Furey said. "Farmland in Connecticut produced $26.71 an acre in taxes, while farm acreage in New York produced $20.51 and in Pennsylvania, $17.79."
Supporters of the assessment program and Mr. Singer's bill claim that the program is accomplishing its chief goal, to preserve open space. Mayor Ron Dancer of Plum sted Township, a member of a family well known for racing and training standardbred horses -- trotters and pacers -- argued that the law benefits nonfarm taxpayers by slowing development and the expensive police protection, roads and schools that come with it.
The opponents, including a number of assessors and homeowners, want to review the economics of the entire farmland assessment program. They are dismayed that Mr. Singer's bill, which was introduced in January, appears headed for a floor vote on March 30, several weeks before the release of a Rutgers University study of the program -- which they hope would support their view that it is not cost-effective.
Walter Kozul, the Marlboro Township Tax Assessor and a member of the Legislative Policy Committee of the New Jersey State League of Municipalities, said the Singer bill would just make a bad situation worse.
"It will be opening that can of worms too wide," he said.
Mr. Kozul argued that the Singer bill, particularly the change favoring horse training and boarding, would extend a tax break to a commercial activity, not to farmland preservation.
"This is going to further erode the municipal tax base at a time when the state is pushing greater responsibility for providing services down to the local level," he said.
Beverly Scarano, a member of the Monmouth County Tax Board and president of the New Jersey Association of County Tax Board Commissioners and Administrators, said the Singer amendments "would liberalize the law so much it would lose its original intent, to preserve open space."
"This is a commercial bill and gets totally away from the law," she said. "It's going to shift the tax burden, and if you take it away from one column it has to go somewhere else."
In 1993, two residents in Upper Freehold Township, Richard D. Smith Jr. and Joseph D. Sarafin, charged that there was widespread abuse of the program, noting that 70 percent of the township's 21,000 acres were covered by farmland assessments. A subsequent state investigation found that only 81 of the first 160 properties examined actually qualified for the assessments they received.
Mr. Smith said that qualifying township farmers paid $9.29 per acre in property taxes in 1991, compared with the average $1,118 per acre for nonfarm property. The two men have begun a lobbying campaign to try to stop passage of the Singer bill. "It's an abomination," Mr. Smith said. "They can't think up ways fast enough to get more money to the rich and famous; they're tripping over each other on the way to the trough."
Mr. Sarafin called the program "welfare for the rich," and said if the state wants to preserve open space it should simply extend the program to everyone and be done with it. "The burden is now on so few, and nearly everyone has a lawn that grows grass, so why not make it all farmland?" Mr. Sarafin, a Democrat, said he would like to board a llama on his land to qualify for a tax break "and teach it to spit at Republicans."
The Rutgers study is headed by Adesoji Adelaja, associate director of the Ecopolicy Center at the university's Cook College. He said the State Department of Agriculture asked the center to determine what would happen if any of the elements of the program were changed -- the five-acre acreage minimum; the $500 in farm earnings for the first five acres with $5 for each additional acre and 50 cents an acre for woodland, and the extent of back taxes a property owner would have to pay if the farm activity on the land ceased. At present the "rollback" covers the two prior years.
"We have to come up a model that will show how many farms and acreage we would lose if any element or combination of elements of the program were changed," Mr. Adelaja said. "We also have to determine the impact on municipal revenues."
"If we make a change, we might lose farms but increase tax revenues to the towns," he said. "We would have to balance what is in the best interest of the state."

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Only 88 More to Go

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
So I've been thinking about this yen problem and I've got an idea. The dollar keeps falling against the Japanese yen, right? It started at 360 yen to the dollar after World War II and now it's down to 88 yen to the dollar. And each time the dollar falls we say, surely it can't go any further. But it does. So let's take the suspense out of this. I say we let the dollar go to zero right now. That's right. Let's make $1 be worth no yen.
Think about it. It will solve all our problems. We won't be able to buy anything from Japan, so our $60 billion trade deficit with Tokyo will disappear. Without that deficit, the dollar will again strengthen and be the darling of international markets. So forget this blather from the Treasury about how we don't want the dollar to weaken further against the yen. Our battle cry should be: "Only 88 Yen To Go Before Zero." 
 O.K., O.K., so it's not in the textbooks to do it that way. But Japan's behavior isn't either. I'm so tired of reading stories that say the dollar went down against the yen because of the fall of the peso, the storms in California, the earthquake in Kobe, the poison in the Tokyo subway or the fact that Japanese TV canceled reruns of "I Love Lucy."
It's not so complicated. The dollar is going down against the yen today for the same two basic reasons that it has been falling for years: because we buy too much from Japan and we sell it too little. Too many dollars chasing goods over there; too few yen chasing goods over here. Everything else is commentary.
We have finally started cutting back our spending and reducing the Federal deficit. We must do more. But so must Japan, which continues to resist U.S. imports. Hey, they wouldn't even use free Tylenol we shipped over to aid victims of the Kobe earthquake. They said it might not be right for Japanese bodies. (I'm not making this up.) I guess it's not surprising they don't buy U.S. cars -- even when the dollar becomes so cheap that a Chevy costs the same in Japan as a bicycle built for two.
We are talking about trade barriers that are deeply rooted in Japanese society. It will take more than a cheap dollar to open such a market. It's time for a new strategy.
Trade Representative Mickey Kantor got China to cave into U.S. trade demands on textiles and intellectual property because he was empowered by the Administration with a very specific list of targets and an even more specific list of punishments if Beijing did not comply. When the U.S. has been equally focused with Japan, such as opening its cellular phone market, it has been successful.
But it would take decades to try to open up every Japanese industry, one at a time. We have tried a more broad-brush approach, called the "framework" negotiations. But they have largely failed to make a dent in the trade imbalance because we were not specific enough about what we wanted -- numerical increases in Japanese imports across a broad front -- and what would happen if we didn't get it. Instead, we danced around the issue, getting into endless Talmudic discussions with the Japanese about what constitutes an "indicator" of more open trade.
Any time we got even close to a specific numerical demand, the Japanese shouted that we were "managing trade" -- the economic equivalent of accusing someone of child molestation. So we backed off, even though we know Japan has the most managed economy in the world and the only way in is to be managed in.
We are being played for fools. Japan will only change when we use the full strategic and economic weight of the U.S. to make clear to Tokyo that a failure to open all its markets, with concrete results, will lead to a crisis in the U.S.-Japan strategic relationship -- not just the economic one -- and to specific retaliations against Japanese exports.
We have to decide: Do we want to do Talmud or do we want to do trade? If we want to debate about trade terminology with the Japanese, we will lose. They are the world champs. If we want to get serious about deploying all our assets toward a specific market-opening arrangement with Japan, we have a chance. But we have to be serious, and up to now we have been ambivalent. As the saying goes: How can I move forward when I don't know which way I'm facing? It's time to make up our minds. We've got 88 more yen to figure it out.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: BOXING;
King, at the Helm Again, Plans Smooth Road to Title

BYLINE: By GERALD ESKENAZI 

 BODY:
Mike Tyson's first fight in his comeback attempt will be in late June or early July, against a ranked fighter but probably not a blockbuster name, Don King's boxing director, Al Braverman, said yesterday.
"You don't know how much rust he's accumulated," explained Braverman, a longtime figure in boxing who is also King's matchmaker. "You don't go for a desperate shot to make a lot of money after being away for three years." 
 According to Braverman, King is back as Tyson's promoter, even though half a dozen interests continually visited Tyson in prison, hoping to replace King. Following Tyson's release from a prison outside Indianapolis yesterday, he and King returned together yesterday to the Cleveland suburbs where they are practically neighbors.
King seemed on the ropes following Tyson's rape conviction in 1992. King's pool of fighters had dried up, especially among the heavyweights. But now he is promoting 18 world champions and has 4 of the 10 ranking heavyweights. He is promoting Oliver McCall, the World Boxing Council titleholder, as well as Tony Tucker and Bruce Seldon, who meet on April 8 for the World Boxing Association title stripped from George Foreman for refusing to defend it against Tucker. It is the co-feature of McCall's defense against Larry Holmes.
In addition, King is involved with Frans Botha of South Africa, another ranked heavyweight. Thus, a Botha-Tyson match, or one against the loser of the Tucker-Seldon fight, or Lionel Butler (another King fighter) could be on the menu for Tyson.
Meanwhile, there is the matter of who will train the former champion. Tyson was a devastating, punishing, and frightful fighter when trained by Kevin Rooney on his rise to the title. But the pair fell out soon after King took over, and Tyson was not quite so overpowering after that. But it does not appear that Rooney remains in the picture.
Braverman suggested George Benton as a possible trainer. Benton already is McCall's trainer. He had been Pernell Whitaker's longtime co-trainer with Lou Duva until they disagreed on how to handle Whitaker, the W.B.C. welterweight champion. According to Braverman, Tyson likes Benton and is impressed with the fact that he is a former boxer and has seen many of the fighters that Tyson likes to read about as a student of the sport's history.
It is uncertain where Tyson will perform in his first fight. A municipal official in Buenos Aires has written to King seeking to hold the bout there. Just last week, Cedric Kushner, a former South African who now promotes boxing in the United States, met with Nelson Mandela, and they discussed boxing.
"I wouldn't be surprised if Tyson fought there in one of his comeback bouts," said Kushner. "But Mr. Mandela didn't say anything about Tyson. He did speak fondly about boxing."
But Kushner said he had spoken recently with a deputy minister in South Africa who suggested that the country had too many economic problems to underwrite a fight.
The fact that even after three years in prison, and having seemed to be on the downside when he was sentenced, Tyson emerges as an intriguing, mysterious figure indicates the state of the heavyweights.
Over the next three months, four different heavyweight titles will be at stake. In addition to the April 8 doubleheader, Foreman defends his International Boxing Federation crown on April 22 against Axel Schulz and Riddick Bowe makes his first World Boxing Organization defense on June 17 against Jorge Gonzalez .
Tyson unified the crown in 1987, but Bowe tossed it into chaos again when he symbolically, and actually, tossed his W.B.C. belt into a trash can in 1992.
The champion with the closest line of succession to Tyson actually is the 46-year-old Foreman, who won the I.B.F. and W.B.A. titles last year.
Bowe, as W.B.O. title-holder, gives that fledgling group a status it has not previously enjoyed. Bowe is the one nonchampion out there whose fights will have meaning to the public at large -- a public that, in Tyson's three years away, has increasingly turned to the world of tabloid TV journalism. Thus, Tyson gets out more of a celebrity than he was as world champion.
So it is not surprising that so many would-be promoters and members of various religions , visited Tyson over the years in prison. But it was his old confidant, Don King, who took him home.

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
A Shower for Rocky, Spot and Fluffy

BYLINE: By MONTE WILLIAMS 

 BODY:
"MAKE sure you dry the ears," said Glen Gaylinn, keeping a steady gaze on Rocky, a 170-pound Great Dane, who gamely stood for 40 minutes of sponging, spraying, blow-drying and brushing. "It prevents swimmer's ear." Mr. Gaylinn, who owns and operates the Dog Wash, was talking not to a groomer, but to Rocky's owner, Alice D'Ambros.
Ms. D'Ambros had driven in from Staten Island just so she could bathe Rocky at this self-service bathhouse for dogs, a sort of canine carwash in the lower level of a squat building at 177 McDougal Street, just a Frisbee's throw from the dog run at Washington Square Park. 
 Opened three months ago, the Dog Wash is the latest wrinkle in the city's growing pet service industry, and apparently is becoming the in place for pampered dogs and their human companions.
There are 120,000 licensed dogs in New York City, a fraction of the number actually believed to live here, once estimated at two to three million. Americans spent $93 million in pet grooming and boarding in 1994, according to the Pet Industry Joint Advisory Council. The numbers aren't broken down for New York City, but industry watchers say the market is substantial, and growing.
"There are easily five times as many pet stores that offer grooming than there were when we opened in 1960," said Steve Kuper, dean of the New York School of Dog Grooming in Manhattan. Depending on the breed and size, groomers charge on average $40 to $100.
Mr. Gaylinn's Dog Wash is the answer for people who don't want to heave their Saint Bernard into their bathtub and those who are not willing to pay an exorbitant fee to have Beethoven bathed at a chic pet salon.
"I'm filling a niche," said Mr. Gaylinn. "What groomers charge just for doing a wash is outrageous."
At the Dog Wash, self-service is $14 and staff service $27, regardless of size. (Through the end of April, first-time visitors will be charged $7 for self-service.) Grooming tools are available at no charge, and young groomers will show owners how to demat, brush and trim their pooches for free. Staff grooming is also available at a moderate fee.
Mr. Gaylinn said he can offer rates at a fraction of what most pet salons charge because he is equipped to handle 100 dogs a day. But he admits he hasn't yet seen that many paws pad through his door in a single day. "I knew it would be a struggle at first," he said. "But once people try it, they rave about it to their friends."
Indeed, on a recent Saturday afternoon several barking Brahmans -- as well as two or three mixed breeds -- came in with owners who howled with delight about the shop. "I was thinking about renting the place for a night and having all my friends come and wash their dogs -- a dog-washing party!" said Robert Greene, an interior decorator and the proud owner of Gerard, a long-haired, tawny-hued briard. "There's such a good feeling here."
Dog owners touted everything from the salon's decor (walls festooned with illustrations of skateboarding and guitar-playing pooches) to its cleanliness (air ionizers zap dust particles and ozone generators kill mold and fungus). But the biggest advantage, they agreed, was psychological.
"It's a fun day for him," said Ms. D'Ambros, a personal fitness trainer. "It gets him out with other dogs. Plus, he likes it better when I handle him."
"It's a bonding experience," said David Faulkner, a West Villager, who lovingly lathered Limerick, a mixed breed found on St. Patrick's Day, two years ago.
"It's very interactive," added Mr. Greene, who has paid up to $200 for a shampoo and cut for Gerard.
Mr. Greene knows from interactive. Three years ago, Gerard leapt from the tub during a bath and dashed out of the apartment. Mr. Greene, who had pragmatically joined Gerard in the bath, was forced to give chase. "I ran stark naked down the hall after him," recalled Mr. Greene, a Chelsea resident, who lived on the Upper East Side at the time. "The elevator opened, and there stood the parents of the only other tenant of the building."
At the Dog Wash, Mr. Greene doesn't have to remove a stitch. Owners get plastic aprons and free instruction in bathing technique. Dog and owner enter one of seven stalls, or bath bays, designed by Mr. Gaylinn, equipped with a bench (for the dog), soap and water (already mixed) and clear water (kept between 86 and 90 degrees).
"You don't have to lift the dog into a tub," said Mr. Gaylinn, putting Jet, the shop's resident rambunctious German Shepard, behind the counter. "Most pet stores use tubs. When I designed the bath bays, I put myself in the position of a dog. If you were a dog, would you like to be picked up and thrown in a confining porcelain tub with no grip and have people pour water over you?"
A former commodities broker and lifelong dog lover, Mr. Gaylinn, 32, said he left the "backstabbing and politics" of the financial world two years ago. "I was living in Manhattan, and I thought about where all these dogs were being washed," he recalled. "Then I found out how much pet stores were charging."
Mr. Gaylinn originally planned to open on West Fourth Street over a year ago, but the deal fell through. He moved in with his parents in Connecticut and became a veterinary technician at Strawberry Hill Animal Hospital in Norwalk. He designed bath bays for the hospital (where a couple of pigs, as well as dogs and cats, came in for laundering). He sold those bays to the hospital's chief veterinarian so that he could return to the city.
With money from the sale and a $200,000 loan from friends, family and financial institutions, Mr. Gaylinn opened the Dog Wash shortly before Christmas, but only to friends' dogs. "I had to test the equipment," he explained. Shortly after New Year's, the public was invited in.
Though washing is focus, the shop also sells gourmet dog foods (both vegetarian and meat-based), bones, toys, sweaters, raincoats and other canine accouterment.
What do his friends from the pit-bullish canons of Wall Street think of his cute new venture? "Well, they definitely thought I was crazy at first," he said. "I mean, I have a degree in economics from Amherst. But when they walk in here and look at the store, they think I'm so successful, they want me to invest in their stock schemes."

HEADLINE: USAir Enters An Agreement With Its Pilots

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT 

 BODY:
USAir broke a yearlong deadlock with its pilots yesterday and reached an agreement that could help the financially beleaguered airline drastically cut costs.
USAir, the nation's sixth-largest carrier, did not disclose any details of the agreement, nor did its pilots union, the Air Line Pilots Association. The airline's statement suggested, however, that the agreement included wage and work rule concessions from the pilots in exchange for "financial returns and governance participation," most likely referring to stock in the company and representation on USAir's board. 
 Although the agreement is tentative and conditioned on other labor groups reaching separate deals with the airline, it is nevertheless significant.
USAir, based in Alexandria, Va., has been among the most chronically unprofitable United States airlines, having lost more than $2.5 billion since the late 1980's. It is trying to cut $1 billion in annual operating costs, and wants half of the savings to come from agreements with its labor groups, including unions representing mechanics and flight attendants.
USAir said discussions with those labor groups were continuing. A tape-recorded message for members of USAir's pilots union said only that the two sides had made "substantial progress."
The last publicized offer by pilots called for employees to contribute $500 million a year in savings in return for 20 percent of the company and seats on the board.
Kevin C. Murphy, an airline stock analyst at Morgan Stanley, said yesterday that the pilots and company were probably motivated to end their impasse by the airline's thinning cash reserves.
The airline had about $400 million in cash at the end of 1994, and Mr. Murphy said the company had indicated that its reserves had since fallen to about $250 million.
"The clock is clearly ticking," he said. "An accord must be reached at this level of liquidity, which is a minimal level for operating solvency."
Until details of the agreement are announced, it remains unclear whether investors will be enamored of the terms of the pilot agreement. One important USAir investor, Warren E. Buffett, recently signaled a no-confidence vote for the airline's prospects by saying that he no longer wanted to sit on USAir's board.
USAir was among the first airlines to cut expenses early in an economic downturn that has cost the airline industry more than $13 billion in losses since 1990. But those agreements were temporary and not deep enough, industry analysts have said.
As a result, USAir has been widely perceived as a laggard in the industry, and is now trying to follow other carriers' successful efforts to strike deals with their labor groups.
Over the last two years, for example, United Airlines, Northwest Airlines and Trans World Airlines have granted their employees large equity stakes in return for concessions, both in the form of pay cuts and work rule changes that lead to greater productivity.
As a result of these deals, employees have typically gained a greater say in the strategy and operation of the company through newly acquired seats on their airlines' boards.
If USAir is successful in concluding the agreement with the pilots, it will mark an important success for Seth E. Schofield, USAir's chairman and chief executive, who has spent his entire 28-year industry career at the airline, rising from the rank of baggage handler.
Representatives of the airline's other unions could not be reached for comment yesterday.
