HEADLINE: Labor Board to Seek Injunction Against Baseball Club Owners

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
One week before the scheduled start of the baseball season, the National Labor Relations Board yesterday authorized its general counsel to seek an injunction in Federal court that could send striking players back to work as early as this week.
Daniel Silverman, director of the board's New York office, said he would initiate the effort for an injunction in United States District Court in Manhattan today and would ask the judge, once one is assigned, to handle the case expeditiously. 
 The injunction would force club owners to restore certain elements of the expired collective bargaining agreement, including salary arbitration and competitive bidding for free agents. The players have said they would end their seven-and-a-half-month strike and return to work if such an injunction is obtained.
What is not clear, however, is whether the owners would then vote to lock out the players rather than allow them back under the old work rules. Twenty-one of the 28 owners would have to vote to approve a lockout for it to take effect, and it is possible that those supporting a lockout would not be able to muster the votes needed.
Thus, the latest development at least raised the notion that major league replacement baseball -- which is scheduled to begin Sunday night when the Mets play the Florida Marlins in Miami -- might not get off the ground.
And in a related development, the two sides in the dispute agreed to resume negotiations tonight in Manhattan, meeting for the first time since March 4.
The vote to seek the injunction was 3-2, with the three Democrats on the board voting in favor and the two Republicans against. The board's action created a scenario under which the season could conceivably begin on a delayed basis with the regular players on the field. But the scenario includes a series of "ifs."
If the judge who gets the case today grants the preliminary injunction before the start of the season, and if the players offer to return to work and are not locked out, the two sides could agree to postpone opening day for about three weeks to give the players time for spring training.
In that way, clubs would not have to use the so-called replacement, or strikebreaking, players they have worked furiously, but not always successfully, to meld into temporary teams.
However, the labor board's decision to seek an injunction doesn't guarantee that its effort will be successful. And even if it is, there is no way of knowing if a judge would rule quickly enough to salvage the start of the season for the striking players.
If, for instance, the injunction came a few days into the replacement season, and the regular players were allowed to return, baseball would have to address the issue of what to do with the replacement games already played.
In addition, more than 800 players are currently unsigned and an additional 100 to 200 are free agents not affiliated with any team. And that only adds to the uncertainty of the situation.
Still, the union was clearly happy with yesterday's action, and the owners weren't.
"I'm pleased with the result," said Gene Orza, the union's associate general counsel. "It's not only the result I was hoping for, but it's also the right result. Now I'm just hoping a judge sees it the same way."
Chuck O'Connor, the owners' chief labor lawyer, took the opposite view.
"We are disappointed that the N.L.R.B. took this action," he said by telephone from Washington. "We think it's an unwarranted intrusion into the collective bargaining process. More importantly, we do not believe it is warranted factually or legally. We expect to resist the action of the board as vigorously as possible."
The board had delayed its vote from last Thursday at the request of the mediator in the baseball dispute, Bill Usery Jr., who had hoped to get both sides to resume negotiations, which they didn't do. William B. Gould 4th, the chairman, and Margaret A. Browning and John C. Truesdale voted to approve the request for the injunction from Fred Feinstein, the board's general counsel. Charles I. Cohen and James M. Stephens voted against.
"I, along with the other two members, believe that the case is a strong one under the applicable legal standards," Mr. Gould said by telephone from Washington after the board's two-hour meeting. "That's why we voted to authorize."
Mr. Feinstein, in a statement, said the board believed the injunction "is necessary to level the playing field in order that bargaining may proceed fairly." Baseball players, he added, "are entitled to the protection of labor law" like any other employees.
In 126 previous injunction requests received by the board under Mr. Gould's chairmanship, board members did not meet but instead studied the cases and voted yes or no on a form on whether to seek an injunction. Mr. Gould's decision to meet in this instance obviously reflected the visibility the baseball strike -- the longest work stoppage in the history of professional sports -- has gained.
Last Wednesday, Mr. Usery asked Mr. Gould to give him time to try to push the two sides closer together. Last Friday, however, it became obvious to Mr. Gould that the delay was meaningless because Mr. Usery had not produced a bargaining session.
Mr. Gould, said a person familiar with his action, went to the other board members, told them he could see no evidence of negotiations and therefore felt it appropriate to move quickly.
Even if a judge doesn't grant the injunction, the players may decide to offer to return to work. They would be risking the kind of economic system they would play under -- it is not clear whose rules would take precedence -- but they would also force the owners to decide whether to lock them out. If the players are to remain out of work, they may prefer a lockout to a strike at this juncture, partly for public relations purposes and partly to keep any players from being tempted to break ranks and return to work.
The owners could ask the judge to stay the injunction, but if he declined, they would have to comply with it while appealing to the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit.
The N.L.R.B. has a good track record in its quest for injunctions or in producing settlements of complaints when it plans to seek injunctions. According to board statistics, during fiscal 1995, which began last Oct. 1, the N.L.R.B. has won injunctions or gained settlements of cases 83 percent of the times it has set out to get injunctions. In fiscal 1994, the board was successful 81 percent of the time. In the preceding nine years, the board's success rate was 90 percent.
Mr. Usery had initially set a new round of talks for March 14 or 15 in Florida, but the owners called off the meetings when the board announced it was issuing a complaint on the union's unfair labor practice charge. That complaint led to yesterday's decision to seek the injunction.
As acknowledged by management people, some owners want to start the season with strikebreaking players as a means of inducing the striking players to go back to work without an agreement.
The owners believe the players will eventually return "in droves," one management person has said. The theory is that the players do not want to lose any more salary after having lost their pay for the last 52 days of last season. In previous work stoppages, the players always have held together while dissenting owners have undermined the owners' position and forced them to settle. This time, the owners have exhibited the players' kind of solidarity.
The owners, though, have tried different lead negotiators. They started with Richard Ravitch, who left when his contract expired Dec. 31, then switched to John Harrington of the Boston Red Sox and more recently to Jerry McMorris of the Colorado Rockies. Bud Selig, the acting commissioner, has been involved, too. He, Mr. McMorris and Mr. Harrington were scheduled to be on hand when talks resumed this evening.
As contentious as all strikes and lockouts have been, this dispute has exceeded the others in the depth of the animosity it has created. The latest salvo was fired Saturday by Jerry Reinsdorf of the Chicago White Sox, who the union believes is in control of the clubs' strategy.
Speaking at the White Sox camp in Sarasota, Fla., Mr. Reinsdorf drew a comparison between Donald Fehr, the baseball union leader, and Jim Jones, a cult leader who led his followers to mass suicide.
"I don't want to say something that's going to make headlines, but there is a certain similarity," Mr. Reinsdorf said. "The players are blindly following Don Fehr."
The two sides have been so entrenched in the battle over the game's economic system that even President Clinton failed to budge them when they spent an evening at the White House last month.

HEADLINE: Budget Planners Cast Nets For Surplus Special Funds

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON 

DATELINE: TRENTON, March 23

 BODY:
New Jersey's boarding house rental assistance fund has $20 million in its account but few applicants for the boarding house improvements it supports, so Gov. Christine Todd Whitman will divert half the money to her new urban housing program.
She has also grabbed $50 million from the state's unemployment insurance fund that had been destined for a health insurance program that still has not been put into place. 
 And the Governor is looking hungrily at the $125 million reserve that the Hackensack Meadowlands Development Commission draws on to maintain the giant, inactive landfill there. So far, the commission has managed to hold her off and hang on to most of its money, although $3 million was taken for the state's general fund and a little less than $1 million to help pay police overtime for the World Cup soccer tournament last summer.
All state governments have pots of money, large and small, stashed away in special accounts and funds, but few administrations have been as aggressive as Mrs. Whitman's budget makers in pursuit of surplus dollars to help pay for $1.4 billion in income tax cuts and balance her $16 billion budget.
"It's like looking under the sofa cushions and fishing out quarters," said Carl Golden, the Governor's spokesman.
"Our position is, if the money is not being used for a legitimate purpose, it belongs to the Treasurer," said Robert Friant, spokesman for the Department of Community Affairs, which manages many of the state's special funds.
Not surprisingly, the managers of the funds being studied by the Governor's accountants do not always react so cheerfully.
"We're very concerned about it," said Anthony Scardino Jr., executive director of the Meadowlands commission, which is trying to preserve its $125 million landfill closing fund to improve the area around the landfill, which has already been closed. "But fortunately, $4 million isn't $125 million, and we're grateful for that."
Most of the funds being studied by the administration do not receive money directly from the state budget, but are dependent on their own sources of financing. The Meadowlands fund, for example, was built up by garbage dumping charges while the landfill was open. But each year, most of the funds spend less than they take in, and it is the surplus that state accountants have their eyes on.
Although the funds are earmarked for specific purposes, the Governor has the power, either directly or through political pressure on fund managers, to divert unspent surpluses, usually to a related cause but sometimes simply to fatten the treasury. So far, only the most powerful guardians have been able to forestall the aggressive application of Treasurer Brian W. Clymer's belief that an idle dollar is a wasted dollar.
"This money belongs to the taxpayers," Mr. Clymer said in an interview. "Is it right to leave the people's money sitting around, when it could be put to use? I don't think it is."
As formal debate nears on the Governor's $16 billion budget for next year, much attention has been focused on the few tens of millions to be raised through a flurry of small fees and taxes, from a new tax on the savings from store coupons to the $5 fee for visits to the Division of Motor Vehicles.
Less noticed has been the way Mrs. Whitman's budget makers have been latching on to the unappropriated surpluses of the special funds. A few million here and a few million there will add up to about $1.5 billion by June 1996.
Sometimes administration officials have used their astuteness with a spreadsheet not to raise money, but simply to make a political point. When school districts, for example, complained last month about a plan to cut financing to districts that spend too much on nonclassroom items, the state's Education Commissioner, Leo F. Klagholz, disarmed the complainers by releasing district audits showing that several of them were maintaining large -- in some cases illegally large -- surpluses that were all but hidden from taxpayer scrutiny.
Sometimes money that the administration skims from surplus accounts is spent outright. At other times, the money is just borrowed, or "leveraged," to use one of Mrs. Whitman's favorite phrases, as a down payment on fresh borrowing.
This was the approach used by Harriet E. Derman, a tax lawyer who is now Commissioner of the Department of Community Affairs, when she found that the state's Housing and Mortgage Finance Agency had $200 million in unused bonding capacity and $60 million more than required in its reserve account.
Mrs. Derman developed a plan to lend the money twice, first to builders and then to their buyers, in a way that allowed the state to proclaim a $525 million home building program.
The announcement of the housing program came a few days after the Governor was roundly criticized by New Jersey editorial writers for releasing an urban revitalization program with no more than $200 million in seed money attached. Although the housing program included mostly artfully repackaged state money, the reaction was universally positive as newspapers announced a half-billion-dollar housing spending plan.
Only occasionally are the Governor's accountants turned back, perhaps growling as they go, when a fat fund is put beyond their reach. Last year, the administration tried to take $16 million in what it considered surplus money from the Catastrophic Illness in Children Relief Fund, which helps with the medical expenses of seriously ill children.
"The Senate President, who created the fund, did not agree with the decision to do so," said Renee Trabert, a spokeswoman for Senator Donald T. DiFrancesco. "He said that the fund was off limits and not available to be lapsed into the general fund."

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

SHORT CUTS SUSPECTED ON PIPELINE
The $695 million Iroquois pipeline is the Northeast's most bountiful source of energy in decades, winding 375 miles from the Canadian border across the heart of New York through western Connecticut and into central Long Island, delivering fuel to heat 2.7 million homes.
But four years after the project was completed, Federal prosecutors, landowners and several of the very contractors who built the pipeline are questioning whether a rushed job took short cuts with safety, creating a time bomb somewhere along the pipeline. A1.
 
POLICE ARREST 296 AT A COCKFIGHT
Heavily armed police officers and agents of the American Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals crashed into an old movie theater in the Bronx, seized 296 people and scores of caged and bloody roosters and shut down what had been billed as a national championship cockfight.
 
The raid -- the largest in the 129-year history of the A.S.P.C.A. -- followed two months of undercover investigations that tracked preparations for an event that had kicked off the 1995 cockfighting season in New York City and brought hundreds of spectators and fighting cocks from all over New York State, as well as New Jersey, Pennsylvania, Virginia and Puerto Rico. A1.
 
NEW YORK CITY
 
QUESTIONS ARISE IN A YOUTH'S DEATH
The police said 16-year-old Yong Xin Huang was shot and killed during a struggle with an officer on Friday when the officer's gun accidently discharged. But a friend of Mr. Huang said that the teen-ager did not resist and that the shooting was not accidental. The two starkly different versions emerged as the police and the family struggled to find out what really happened Friday morning in Sheepshead Bay, Brooklyn. One thing both agreed on was that Mr. Huang was carrying a carbon dioxide-powered Grossman Air Pistol, which resembles a real weapon, outside a friend's house. B3.
 
BOY, 7, IS STRUCK AND KILLED IN CAR CRASH
Seven white candles burned at the front stoop of 192 Nagle Avenue in the Inwood section of Manhattan. They marked the spot where Varness Vargas, 7, died when an Oldsmobile station wagon smashed into two parked cars, jumped a curb and struck the boy early Saturday night. The police still have an incomplete picture of the events that ended with the boy's death. B4.
 
REGION
 
A SHOWDOWN ON LOBBYING BILL
A two-year campaign by business and construction trade groups to undercut the lobbying activities of a student-based advocacy organization is heading for a showdown. A bill to change the way Rutgers University students contribute to the organization, the New Jersey Public Interest Research Group, is in position for a vote in the Senate. Its sponsor predicts that the vote there will be as close as the one in the State Assembly, which recently approved the measure. B5.

She has also grabbed $50 million from the state's unemployment insurance fund that had been destined for a health insurance program that still has not been put into place. CHEMICAL FIRE ERUPTS IN WAREHOUSE
A chemical fire spewing hazardous fumes in a warehouse in Hackensack, N.J., forced several dozen people from their homes Saturday night and burned for more than 24 hours before firefighters extinguished it, officials said. The fire began around 7 P.M. in a one-story warehouse at the CWD Transport Company, a trucking concern at 55 Devoe Street. More than 60 firefighters battled the blaze, and 16 suffered minor injuries and smoke inhalation. B4.
 
BODY MAY BE LINKED TO PAROLED KILLER
A badly decomposed body discovered yesterday in Rockland County is believed to be another victim of a paroled murderer who has been charged with killing one woman and raping another since his release. B5.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Italian Monopoly Faces New World

BYLINE: By JOHN TAGLIABUE 

DATELINE: ROME, March 26

 BODY:
In reaching out to the International Business Machines Corporation, Italy's state-owned telecommunications company is a bit like a sheep looking for a shepherd to fight off yapping wolves.
Societa Finanziaria Telefonica per Azioni, known in business circles as Stet, gives few details of the talks it began last month about possible cooperative ventures with I.B.M.. But the fact that there are talks is significant for a company that has long prided itself on its independence, and profited from monopoly status. 
 Independence, however, does not work very well in a global economy, and Stet's monopoly in Italy is eroding as European nations deregulate their telecommunications industries. So Stet is shopping around.
Some of the wolves snapping at its heels are just cubs -- small local companies seeking chunks of Stet's business. Others are more menacing -- European telecommunications giants that have a big technological lead over Stet and have formed strategic alliances with American companies, affording them a head start as Europeans prepare for the full deregulation of the telecommunications market by 1998.
Enter I.B.M. In the midst of a huge corporate turnaround effort, the computer giant retains big private communications networks throughout the world. It has announced its intention of entering global telecommunications, and could conceivably use a partner like Stet.
Enrico Graziani, Stet's 59-year-old general manager, says such a link would give the company a leg up against competitors like British Telecommunications P.L.C. or Deutsche Telekom by melding Stet's telecommunications skills with I.B.M.'s experience in processing electronic data, which will make up the bulk of telecommunications in the future.
Corporate analysts say such an alliance would make sense. "Stet has many irons in the fire, but in some ways not enough, such as international network links for its large corporate customers," said Sean Phelan of Yankee Group Europe, a British-based consulting operation. I.B.M., Mr. Phelan said, could offer Stet global connections, providing engineers and marketing, while Stet could provide specialized skills like transmission and switching.
As with most European state telecommunications companies, Stet has embarked on an extensive overhaul. Last year, five of its operating units, in businesses ranging from phone systems to manufacturing equipment to satellite communications, were combined into Telecom Italia S.p.A., an $18-billion-a-year group that is controlled by Stet.
The companies have thrust themselves into new businesses in Italy and abroad, starting interactive television projects, beginning one of Italy's two cellular phone systems and providing access to the Internet for business customers.
Stet, like other European phone companies, has been discussing tactical alliances with other players, including Siemens of Germany and AT&T in the United States. But all of this may do little good in the long run unless Stet can gain a strategic partner in a worldwide network for transmitting data and voice.
The outcome is crucial for Stet, which is high on the Italian Government's privatization agenda.
The forthcoming deregulation in European telecommunications is similar in scope and aims to the breakup of the Bell System in the United States in the 1980's. Unlike the American big bang, however, the European process is an evolution that will gradually eliminate state monopolies and lift national barriers until Jan. 1, 1998, when most of Europe's telecommunications market is to be opened to competition.
Already, the big players are choosing allies. Last year, France Telecom and Deutsche Telekom announced plans to acquire a 20 percent stake in Sprint, the No. 3 long-distance carrier in the United States, and a little earlier, British Telecom announced a strategic alliance with MCI Communications, which is No. 2 in the United States. With such alliances aiming to create worldwide networks, Stet risked being left on the sidelines.
Analysts attribute the slowness of Stet's search for a partner to a reluctance to play second fiddle. "Stet is one of the last to enter the fray," said Bill Coleman of James Capel & Company of London. "They wanted to get in with one of the big players, but it did not suit them because they would have lacked the ability to control, to influence decisions."
According to Mr. Graziani, Stet's interest in I.B.M. is born of a conviction that the transmission of computerized data will one day dislodge voice as the dominant form of telecommunications. Thus, he said, a partner in the computer field is "an ingredient of major importance."
Analysts say I.B.M.'s greatest appeal for Stet is its plan to set up an I.B.M. Global Network to link its local communications networks in roughly 100 nations to the world's largest high-speed voice and data system.
Brian Ditzer, a spokesman at I.B.M.'s headquarters in Armonk, N.Y., said the company was increasingly looking toward telecommunications partners to enhance the value of the system. Whether Stet fills the bill is presumably one of the issues being explored in the talks.
Both he and Mr. Graziani emphasized that the discussions were at the exploratory stage.
Even so, the possibility of joining forces with the world's biggest computer company is heady stuff for Stet. For years in Italy, the odds that a call would get through were only slightly higher than those of winning at roulette. Big corporate clients complained endlessly about high telephone bills and poor service. Last year, Fiat said that if its headquarters were in New York or San Francisco, not Turin, its phone bill would have been 42 percent less.
Stet's exploitation of its monopoly kept out competition. When British Telecom offered to provide Benetton with networking services well below the Italian rate, Stet blocked the British by undercutting the bid, offering rates unavailable to other customers.
Now, Stet is in the midst of a $5- billion-a-year investment program to upgrade its lines with fiber glass cable, pushing into businesses like interactive television and gaining corporate partners like Siemens to modernize its manufacturing.
As a hedge against competition, it is investing heavily abroad. Stet owns 32 percent of the company that controls Telecom Argentina and is looking to expand in Latin America. It also operates a cellular phone system in Greece, has reached agreements to upgrade Russia's telephone network and is bidding for a 27 percent stake in the Czech Republic's state phone company.
At home, with lower rates leading to more calls and new services swelling its cash flow, Stet's earnings for 1994 totaled $1.25 billion on operating revenue of $21.2 billion. The company says a comparison of last year's results with the previous year's would be misleading because of its revamping, but it points out that it cut its debt in 1994 by $2 billion, to $11.8 billion.
Stet is clearly playing for time. Mr. Graziani says the problem is that the company, like most of the world's old telephone monopolies, is now wedged between an era of competition and its monopolistic past.
Italy's carousel of governments has not helped the telecommunications giant. Legislation is needed to bring Italy into line with European Union rules, and plans for a telecommunications authority, similar to the Federal Communications Commission in the United States, are still on the drawing board.
"We need a strong authority," Mr. Graziani said, "to fix the rules of the game."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Italian telecommunications international control room in Rome. For Stet, Italy's encrusted telephone monopoly, a voice- and data-transmission arrangement with I.B.M. is one route toward future prosperity. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: USAir Pilot Pact May Cut Costs

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT 

 BODY:
Breaking a yearlong deadlock with its pilots, USAir said Saturday that it had reached an agreement that could help it drastically cut costs.
USAir, the nation's sixth-largest carrier, based in Arlington, Va., did not disclose details of the agreement, nor did the pilots union, the Air Line Pilots Association. The airline's statement suggested, however, that the agreement included wage and work-rule concessions from the pilots in exchange for "financial returns and governance participation," most likely referring to stock in the company and representation on USAir's board. 
 Though the agreement is tentative and conditional on USAir reaching deals with its other labor unions, it is still significant. USAir has been among the most chronically unprofitable United States airlines, having lost more than $2.5 billion since the late 1980's. It is trying to cut $1 billion in annual operating costs, and wants half of those savings to come from agreements with its labor groups, including unions representing mechanics and flight attendants.
USAir said that discussions with those other labor groups were continuing.
The last publicized offer by pilots called for employees to contribute $500 million a year in savings in return for 20 percent of the company and seats on the board.
Kevin C. Murphy, an airline stock analyst at Morgan Stanley, said Saturday that the pilots and the company were probably motivated to end their impasse by the airline's thinning cash reserves.
USAir was among the first airlines to cut costs early in the economic downturn.

HEADLINE: Black Conservative Enters Race for G.O.P. Nomination

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr. 

DATELINE: SAN DIEGO, March 26

 BODY:
Alan L. Keyes, a passionately partisan black conservative who held several State Department posts in the Reagan Administration, jumped into the 1996 Presidential contest today, the first of his race to seek the Republican nomination in modern political times.
He vowed to turn the contest into a wide-open debate advocating family values and opposing abortion, an issue many other Republicans would just as soon leave on the back burner. 
 Because of his relative political obscurity, few political experts expect much from Mr. Keyes's candidacy, even if it is based primarily on the incendiary abortion issue. But Mr. Keyes, who is 44 years old and now earns a living as a Baltimore talk-show host, writer and lecturer, nevertheless plunged in with outspoken vigor this morning.
"Somebody has got to stand up and raise the banner," he said -- thundered, actually -- in announcing his candidacy during a meeting of the California Republican Assembly, the conservative wing of the state Republican Party.
"Too many of our political leaders finesse or run away from the questions of principle that will determine the kind of people we are and want to be," he continued. "We can no longer afford to follow leaders for whom the moral challenge this nation faces is an afterthought. The paradigm of all peace and security should be the peace of the unborn child in the womb."
The Assembly is hard-line conservative, as only California Republicans can be hard-line conservative, and Mr. Keyes surely picked a good forum for his announcement. No issue scores with Assembly members like abortion and Mr. Keyes, a devout Roman Catholic, more than once brought the house down with his attacks on what he called "this moral evil."
At times he even won applause from the many Assembly members who were wearing pins in support of Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, Senator Phil Gramm of Texas and Patrick J. Buchanan, the columnist and commentator.
Still, Mr. Keyes, who was brought up in a military family and turned toward conservatism while studying government at Cornell and Harvard, faces major -- probably insurmountable -- hurdles with his candidacy.
Though he is on the political lecture circuit and though he served in the Reagan Administration as an ambassador to the United Nations Economic and Social Council and as an Assistant Secretary of State, he nevertheless is not widely known in the country, is almost never recognized in any national polls and has little campaign financing. Perhaps worst of all, the half dozen other Republican candidates or would-be candidates have already laid claim to most of the positions he holds dearest, though most cannot match his impassioned rhetoric in articulating them.
Add to all those hurdles the fact that Mr. Keyes is black in a party whose 20th-century history has been mainly white.
"He's good for the party and I welcome what he says," said one Assembly member, Steve Mesarch, a high school administrator from Palm Springs. "But, well, Phil Gramm also says it and I'm for him. That's the kind of thing a Keyes candidacy will constantly have to face."
William Kristol, a Republican strategist in Washington, offered an even blunter assessment of Mr. Keyes's political prospects.
"Alan's candidacy is historical and good for the debate but, no, he's not going to get the nomination," said Mr. Kristol, an adviser to Mr. Keyes in 1988 when he ran unsuccessfully in Maryland for the United States Senate. A 1992 Senate candidacy there also failed.
Whatever his 1996 prospects, Mr. Keyes declared his intention today to campaign the length of the country with his message that the cry is not only abortion but also teen-age pregnancy, homosexuality, "godless" schooling and a loss of "those values that made this country what it is."
He asserted that abortion was the "central issue" in American politics as the century draws to a close. He argued that if the nation did not outlaw it, a collapse of "the American way" was inevitable.
"The debate over abortion epitomizes how we define freedom," he said. "Abortion is a moral wrong. We have the opportunity, the duty to stand up and say, 'Nay!' "
Then, in what seemed to be his own blunt assessment of his political prospects, he concluded: "This is not a horse race. This can't be a campaign about winning government power. It has to be a campaign about winning back that power that built this country: faith, responsibility, family values. That is the victory that matters most."
 
 Alan Lee Keyes

HEADLINE: U.S. Vows to Press Hard On Iraq to Free Americans

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 26

 BODY:
The White House sought to step up pressure on Iraq today by pledging to be "very firm" with Iraq and to press diplomatic efforts to win the release of two Americans imprisoned for illegally entering the country.
Two Republican presidential candidates, Senator Richard Lugar of Indiana and Patrick Buchanan, said that the United States should consider using its military to force the release of the two men. 
 "There is no justification for the sentences that were imposed on these two," Leon Panetta, the White House chief of staff, said on the NBC News program "Meet the Press."
"We have made a very firm request that they be released immediately on humanitarian grounds."
Senator Lugar, one of the the Republican Party's leading spokesmen on foreign affairs and a moderate on foreign policy, said outside an Indiana church service today that the United States must "indicate that we are considering full diplomatic possibilities and military options."
"We ought to do so publicly, firmly and quickly," he said.
Mr. Buchanan, said he would start with diplomatic pressure and indicate that "these aren't our final options." Appearing on the CBS News program "Face the Nation," he said he supported giving Iraq the impression that military action would be considered if the two prisoners are not released.
"These two Americans, if they've been put in jail for eight years for walking across the border, they're not being punished; they're being held as hostages," Mr. Buchanan said. "And I don't think we can tolerate hostage-taking."
Although the Republican views have no direct bearing on the Administration's policy, American diplomats said that Iraq would probably be forced to take the talk of military action seriously if someone as moderate as Mr. Lugar suggested such an option.
Mr. Panetta refused to comment on suggestions that military options be considered.
He told reporters after "Meet the Press," "I don't think either the Iraqi Government or anyone else ought to underestimate our resolve in this situation to have these two released as quickly as possible."
But in interviews today, several Administration officials said they hoped to win the release of the men -- William Barloon of New Hampton, Iowa, and David Daliberti of Jacksonville, Fla. -- through the same type of low-key, behind-the-scenes diplomacy that persuaded Iraq to release another American prisoner in 1993. These officials said they feared that if the dispute turned into a major confrontation it would be harder to win the two men's release.
The Clinton Administration said it would continue to ask the United Nations and Poland, which represents American interests in Iraq, to try to persuade President Saddam Hussein of Iraq to release the two Americans. Administration officials added, however, that they would also ask France and Russia to use their good offices on behalf of the two men.
France and Russia are urging the United Nations to lift economic sanctions imposed against Iraq as a result of its invasion of Kuwait. According to one Administration official, France and Russia would be urged to tell Mr. Hussein that he must release the two Americans soon if he is to retain any hope of having the sanctions ended.
All that can result from Mr. Hussein's "having these guys serve out this ridiculously long sentence is to hurt himself," said one Administration official. "Buy contrast, someone might convince them that they will show a brighter face to the world by releasing them soon."
But the official reiterated that the Clinton Administration would not accept any linkage between the prisoner dispute and its efforts to extend economic sanctions against Iraq.
Administration officials asserted that the two Americans entered Iraq by mistake while trying to visit friends in the demilitarized zone between Iraq and Kuwait.
The Administration is also arguing that Iraq should go easy on the prisoners because United Nations troops allowed them to cross into Iraq by mistake at one border crossing while Iraqi customs officials allowed them into Iraq by mistake at another crossing.
"These two men made an innocent mistake," Mr. Panetta said today. "They should be released."
Mr. Daliberti and Mr. Barloon, who has lived in Kuwait for two-and-a-half years, are civilians who had been working on a contract that McDonnell Douglas has to maintain aircraft for Kuwait's Defense Ministry.
On Saturday, after Iraq sentenced them to eight years in prison, Mr. Barloon's wife, Linda, told Reuters news service in Kuwait: "I'm devastated. I just can't believe it."
She told The Associated Press that her three young children "question why their father is being held as a criminal."
Administration officials say they hope the two prisoners will be released long before they serve the full sentences, as was the case with Kenneth Beaty, an American oil exploration specialist who was sentenced to eight years in prison after illegally crossing into Iraq in January 1993.
After intense but quiet diplomacy, Mr. Beaty was released 205 days later. During his confinement, the Clinton Administration repeatedly urged American and other visitors to Iraq to press upon Mr. Hussein the importance of releasing Mr. Beaty.
Administration officials speculate that Mr. Hussein released Mr. Beaty and nine other foreigners in late 1993 to win good will as part of his effort, which was unsuccessful, to have the sanctions lifted.
"Steady low-key diplomacy worked in the Beatty case, and we hope it works here," said one American diplomat, who insisted that the Clinton Administration's opposition to lifting sanctions on Iran would not be affected by the dispute over the prisoners. The United States persuaded the Security Council to vote to extend the sanctions this month, but they are expected to come to a vote again in May.
"We're not going to be moved off our policy toward Iraq because of this situation, as horrible as it is for these two people being held there," one American official said.

HEADLINE: 7,000 G.M. Workers Threaten To Begin a Walkout This Week

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: DETROIT, March 26

 BODY:
About 7,000 hourly workers at the General Motors Corporation's truck engineering operations and its Pontiac East assembly plant in Pontiac, Mich., could go on strike Friday.
The United Auto Workers union issued a five-day letter to G.M., which authorizes members of UAW Local 594 to strike Friday unless a variety of grievances are resolved, a company spokesman said today. 
 G.M. declined to specify what issues were to be resolved, although increased workloads for some employees and the practice of sending work outside the company to be done at lower costs have been among grievances that were expressed during previous negotiations. The union was not immediately available for comment.
G.M. and the union have been negotiating for several weeks, a company spokesman, Charles Licari, said, adding that the auto maker was optimistic that an agreement would be reached before the Friday deadline.
The North American Truck Group of G.M. is responsible for the engineering and production of the auto maker's light trucks, which include pickups and sport utility vehicles.
G.M. has settled two strikes since October, in part by agreeing to hire more workers at each plant.
Separately, G.M. disclosed late last week that it had lost $24 million on a derivative trade that was terminated last year.
The loss, disclosed in the company's 1994 annual report, which was being mailed to shareholders Friday, was on an interest-rate forward contract.
G.M. officials in New York said that no foreign currencies or banks were involved and that the loss would be amortized over 20 years, ending in 2013.
G.M. made $8 million on a derivative trade in 1993. It declined to say if it expected any derivative losses this year and it sought to minimize the 1994 loss.
An interest-rate forward contract is an agreement between a company and another party to exchange fixed and floating interest-rate payments periodically over the life of the agreement. In the transaction, also called a swap, one investor agrees to pay a floating interest rate to the other investor, who contracts to make payments at a fixed interest rate.

HEADLINE: Cholesterol Pill Linked to Lower Hospital Bills

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS, March 26

 BODY:
A $2-a-day pill that lowers cholesterol can also lower the hospital bills of heart disease patients by cutting their risk of heart attacks and other expensive health problems, a study released last week concluded.
According to the study, giving the drug simvastatin to heart patients reduced their hospital admissions by a third during five years of treatment. It also reduced the number of days that they had to spend in the hospital when they were admitted, and reduced the need for bypass surgery and angioplasty, procedures to open up clogged heart arteries. 
 "This is both good medicine and good economics," said Dr. Sanford Schwartz, a physician and economist at the University of Pennsylvania.
The findings are the latest to be presented from the Scandinavian Simvastatin Survival Study, an examination of the health effects of lowering cholesterol. Simvastatin is one of four similar cholesterol-lowering medicines now on the market. It is made by Merck & Company, which paid for the study but not for Dr. Schwartz's economic analysis.
The study was conducted on 4,444 volunteers. Its initial results, presented last November, showed that the treatment reduced the risk of death by 30 percent in patients who had already suffered heart attacks or angina pain.
At last week's meeting of the American College of Cardiology, the same team showed that the treatment could also have a major impact on hospital costs.
But like most medical treatments, this one does not actually save money. Hospital costs were $8 million lower among the 2,221 volunteers who got simvastatin rather than placebo pills, but the medicine itself costs $11 million, Dr. Schwartz said.
Nonetheless, Dr. Schwartz said the treatment was highly "cost-effective" compared with other widely accepted therapies and tests.
For example, it costs between $5,000 and $10,000 for each year of life saved to lower cholesterol, while mammograms to screen for breast cancer cost $30,000 to $35,000 for each year of life saved.
About a fourth of heart patients in the United States get medication to lower their cholesterol. Although the drugs have long been available, doctors have been reluctant to prescribe them routinely because of doubts about their effectiveness.
The latest findings were presented by Dr. Terje Pedersen of Aker University Hospital in Oslo. According to the study, treatment with simvastatin cut the total number of days patients had to spend in the hospital for heart problems by 34 percent. The treatment reduced the need for bypass surgery and angioplasty by 32 percent. Strokes and transient ischemic attacks, which are less serious brain blood flow disruptions, were also reduced by nearly a third by use of the drug.
When the study began, the patients' total cholesterol levels were mildly elevated, from 212 to 309. After taking the drug, their cholesterol fell by 25 percent and their levels of HDL, the form of cholesterol considered beneficial because it protects against heart disease, rose 8 percent.
The study did not examine the common practice of giving drugs to lower cholesterol to people who have high cholesterol but no signs of heart disease. Whether treatment for them saves lives or money has not been proved.

HEADLINE: U.S. Machine Tool Orders Fell in February

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: NEW YORK, March 26

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools fell slightly in February for the second consecutive month, a trade group reported today, substantiating recent evidence that the economy is slowing down.
The Association for Manufacturing Technology said that machine tool orders totaled $372.85 million in February, down 1.1 percent from $377.15 million in January. 
 But compared with February of last year, orders were up 35.3 percent.
The monthly report covers metal cutting and metal forming machine tools, which are used to manufacture goods from household appliances to aircraft engines. Machine tool orders are considered a gauge of future economic activity. A rise is viewed as an indication that businesses feel confident that their sales will grow enough to justify investing in new equipment.
The report followed the Commerce Department's report on Friday that February orders to factories for durable goods had dropped eight-tenths of 1 percent, the first decline in four months. Economists said that was significant evidence that the economy's expansion is beginning to slow.
For the first two months of 1995, machine tool orders totaled $750 million, up 17.95 percent from $635.85 million in the first two months of 1994.

SECTION: Section D; Page 7; Column 1; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1036 words

HEADLINE: INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY;
Don't Mess With PCS Primeco's Mr. Schmitt

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

 BODY:
The gavel may have fallen on the Federal Government's recent auction of new wireless communications licenses, but George F. Schmitt still sounds as though he is locked in a high-stakes bidding war.
Mr. Schmitt is the president and chief executive of PCS Primeco L.P., which aims to be a power player in the emerging business of personal communications services, or P.C.S. Backing PCS Primeco are Nynex, Bell Atlantic, US West and Airtouch Communications, a cellular company spun off last year by Pacific Telesis. 
 Thanks to the financial muscle of his backers, Mr. Schmitt was able to bid $1.1 billion for P.C.S. licenses in 11 markets, including such key franchises as Chicago and Dallas. Only AT&T and an alliance of the Sprint Corporation and three cable television operators bid more.
Now, though, Mr. Schmitt faces the daunting task of building a wireless franchise in each of the 11 markets. That may explain why this 51-year-old cellular-telephone veteran plays the tough guy when equipment manufacturers come to him, peddling everything from hand-held phones to cellular transmission towers.
"If they don't deliver what they promise, they're going to pay," Mr. Schmitt said. "We've been far too tolerant of vendors who don't deliver. It's why U.S. manufacturers have trouble overseas."
Mr. Schmitt needs to play hardball -- and not just with suppliers. The battle to sell personal communications services promises to be one of the most brutal in the history of telecommunications. AT&T, Sprint, GTE, BellSouth and a host of other companies spent a total of $7.7 billion for licenses to offer the service in the top 51 American markets.
The infant business also faces steep hurdles. For one, it must compete with conventional cellular, which has 23.7 million subscribers and is adding customers at an annual rate of 48 percent. For another, P.C.S. companies must persuade local zoning boards to allow them to put up scores of transmission towers before they can sign up a single customer.
As with cellular, P.C.S. signals are transmitted from a tower to a receiver built into the user's hand-held phone. If the user moves beyond the range of one transmission area, his call is handed off to the next tower.
Mr. Schmitt is no stranger to tough slogs. Before joining PCS Primeco in November, he built a cellular network from scratch in Germany as part of a joint venture between Airtouch and Mannesmann A. G., the German telecommunications giant. In just three years, Mr. Schmitt said, the fledgling service grew from zero to 600,000 customers.
During the Federal Communications Commission auction earlier this month, Mr. Schmitt made it clear in comments at industry conferences that he would retaliate against companies that went after markets he needed to fill out his national coverage. "You mess with me in Chicago, you pay," he said, shortly before the bidding ended.
Mr. Schmitt's bosses clearly hope he will bring the same brio to building his network. "You're dealing with a start-up business and a lot of competition," said Lawrence Babbio, the vice chairman of Bell Atlantic, who is also the chairman of the P.C.S. venture, "so you need a tough guy. If you want me to send him up to Capitol Hill, that's a different story."
Mr. Schmitt does not need to be diplomatic so long as he can attract new customers. In some ways, the job is no different from selling cellular. The only difference between the two is that P.C.S. signals are transmitted at a higher frequency and lower power than cellular signals. For now, P.C.S. phones are somewhat cheaper than cellular ones. But industry executives said that digital technology will soon erase that difference.
So Mr. Schmitt has to figure out how to make his service stand out from what analysts predict will be four or five competing wireless companies in every market that offer basically the same service. "The marketing costs are going to be a lot higher than anybody expected," said David Roddy, the chief telecommunications economist at Deloitte & Touche.
Mr. Schmitt's challenge is thornier than that faced by AT&T or the Sprint-cable alliance because he lacks a well-known national brand name. To get one, some observers believe PCS Primeco will end up allying with the MCI Communications Corporation, the only long-distance provider without a presence in P.C.S. MCI has already broached the subject -- in talks it held with Bell Atlantic and Nynex before the auction started.
But Mr. Schmitt insisted that MCI was not in his future. Instead, he said his company would probably devise a new brand name -- say PCS One -- and would market it alongside the name of each partner company in its home territory. In Philadelphia, for example, consumers would purchase the service from PCS One, brought to them by Bell Atlantic.
Whatever name he settles on, Mr. Schmitt figures to do it without any interference from his owners. He said that he rarely spoke to executives at his four parent companies, aside from Mr. Babbio, his direct boss. "I don't intend to have anybody in my back pocket," he said.
Still, with four strong-willed backers, Mr. Schmitt cannot avoid getting pushed around once in a while. Consider the arcane but important issue of how P.C.S. signals are transmitted.
Mr. Schmitt favors G.S.M., a digital transmission method widely used in European cellular systems. By using G.S.M., which stands for Global System for Mobile Communications, in the United States, PCS Primeco could offer customers extra services, like the ability to send and receive faxes via hand-held phones.
The trouble is, Mr. Schmitt's backers prefer a different transmission format, called code-division multiple access. Bell Atlantic and Nynex use C.D.M.A. in their cellular networks and they want to make sure their cellular customers can talk to their P.C.S. customers. That may not be possible with G.S.M. As a result, they are likely to choose C.D.M.A. for their P.C.S. service, regardless of Mr. Schmitt's objections.
As Mr. Babbio points out, when you have just collected $55 million from each of your backers to make a down payment on P.C.S. licenses -- which Mr. Schmitt recently did -- you cannot always get your way.

HEADLINE: Essay;
You Lucky Seven

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Like Lucy in the comic-strip Peanuts, who dispenses wisdom to all comers under the sign "Psychiatric Help -- 5," I offer herewith invaluable political-guru service to the seven Republican Presidential candidates for the price of a single newspaper.
To Phil Gramm: With Pat Buchanan slicing into your hard-right support, you cannot make it by reminding us of your triumph last year over Hillaryhealth, by out-grinching Gingrich, or by singing "Younger Than Doletime." A fresh, substantive appeal is needed. 
 You're an economist. Clintonites, joined by some nervous Republicans, are now saying "no spending cuts to finance tax cuts." This kicks Lord Keynes in the head.
You could explain how major spending reduction, unless accompanied by substantial tax reduction, would dangerously reduce aggregate demand -- perhaps plunging the nation into recession. By becoming the only anti-recession candidate, you can say you told them so when the downturn comes this winter.
To Pat Buchanan and Arlen Specter: How to break out of single-digit, second-tier status? Upstage the field by staging a series of formal, town-hall, C-Spanned Specter-Buchanan debates.
Just the two of you -- diametrically opposed on abortion rights and civil liberty and free trade -- could put on sustained performances that would energize your disparate constituencies, capture media attention, hype your fund-raising and raise the intellectual content of the campaign to a Bryan-Darrow level. And by grimly maintaining civility and mutual respect, you could detoxify the natural party split.
To Lamar Alexander: Ignore all elitist pundit advice to abandon your flannel shirts and outsider shtick. Nobody else in the field is working the cybercornball side of the street and it could turn out to be effective in Iowa and New Hampshire.
Your initial anti-Congress theme -- "Cut their pay and send them home" -- may be boring to the cognoscenti but could be appealing to voters when their hopes for term limits are dashed. As we will see, the problem of Congressional paralysis was not solved in 1994; by February, the term-limits candidate may find supporters resonating with resentment.
To Richard Lugar: To run as the Thinking Person's Candidate would have won press plaudits but no primary votes, but your strategy of double-hawkishness is intriguing.
Your hard line on Saddam -- send back those two American hostages or face real consequences -- dovetails with being a deficit hawk on seemingly untouchable farm subsidies. Nobody else has the foreign-policy hand and Hoosier eye to frame this symmetry, and there may be more interventionist budget-squeezers than pollsters suspect.
To Bob Dole: Drop the defensive deal-making about your age; promise to serve two full terms without one afternoon nap.
You're the legislative doer, so do: Get the Balanced Budget Amendment this fall by winning Hatfield's abstention, and send Clinton a welfare reform bill he'll hate to have to sign. Work secretly with Packwood to match spending cuts with a surprise tax package featuring flatter rates on both income and capital gains, with caps on future mortgage deductions. And stop running rightward against Gramm when your main competition is Wilson.
To Pete Wilson: Remember how Eisenhower wrested the G.O.P. nomination from Senator Taft -- appeal to the Republican lust to win.
Forget the sniping about running so soon after winning re-election as Governor; it's a reminder that you won. Welcome the onslaught about being pro-choice and never waffle; praise pro-life Gramm for candidly telling the preachers he won't become a preacher. Predict Dole's front-runnerhood for months ahead.
Even as you harass illegal immigrants to encourage their departure, show you're for legal immigrants by opposing plans to restrict their welfare. Don't pander to single-issue voters; because everybody knows the White House fears you most, your primary theme should be "go with a winner."
To my fellow right-wingers: What's the rush to decide 15 months before the convention? Send in a few bucks to the two or three candidates you like most. Revel in the system.

HEADLINE: Inflation Adjustment Defeats Real Cuts

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Mr. Kasich's Numbers" (editorial, March 20), concerning the House Budget Committee's proposal to help pay for tax relief, perpetuated the myth that all discretionary spending must automatically increase by the rate of inflation and if it doesn't is classified as a spending cut. We reject this "Inside the Beltway" approach to spending taxpayers' money and are prepared to offer real and honest budget cuts to the American people. 
 Your argument -- that any spending cut must be measured against an inflated baseline -- is the same mantra used by special interests who want to protect their programs and maintain the status quo. This built-in bias toward higher spending is one reason Federal budget deficits persistently total $200 billion and are projected to climb to more than $300 billion. Perversely, such reasoning allows special interests to claim their programs have been cut, when in fact spending has increased, albeit at a slower rate, or to make the reductions seem much greater than they really are, so as to make them appear impossible to accomplish. Measuring Federal spending with inflation adjustments only permits Federal spending to grow -- and forces Americans to finance a larger national debt.
These are the facts: Discretionary spending is now capped at $546 billion. The Budget Committee proposal extends and lowers the caps by $100 billion over five years.
These are real cuts, as they are measured not from an inflation-adjusted "baseline" but from current levels. We took this approach because we reject the notion that spending for every program must be adjusted upward for inflation.
Further, no matter what measuring stick you want to use to assess our spending cuts, it appears you cannot find even a single dollar or one program in the discretionary part of the Federal budget that should be cut.
We assume, therefore, that you do not support our efforts to attack corporate welfare, such as the Advanced Technology Program, which makes taxpayer dollars available to successful Fortune 500 companies. Although you describe our effort to cut spending as "punishing and arbitrary," we wonder if your readers support continued taxpayer financing of the Travel and Tourism Administration, whose promotional activities are nothing more than a subsidy for the nation's multibillion-dollar tourism industry, or the Interstate Commerce Commission, a regulatory agency with virtually nothing to regulate. This is just a small sample of the 144 proposals we suggested could be used to meet the goal of reducing discretionary spending by $100 billion.
The time for such reform is long overdue, and our effort to reduce the discretionary spending caps is only the first step.
 
JOHN R. KASICH
Chairman, House Budget Committee
Washington, March 22, 1995

HEADLINE: Creator of Business Zones Is Under Fire

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Daniel F. Biederman, the mastermind behind the Grand Central, 34th Street and Bryant Park business improvement districts, is about to create another. But this district is in Jersey City, and it has raised questions about the management and financial controls of the districts in New York.
Part of an audit of the Grand Central and 34th Street districts says that money from them was used to help set up the district in Jersey City. The audit is being prepared as Mr. Biederman and other top managers of the New York City improvement districts are about to sign a contract to run the one in Jersey City, too. 
 The audit and the contract have focused attention on the accountability of business improvement districts, which tax their members in exchange for providing services, as governments do, but do not have the same kinds of oversight. Such districts have been growing rapidly in size and numbers across New York City as a way to supplement city services.
"It is important that public-private partnerships be held to the highest standards," said Ruth W. Messinger, the Manhattan Borough President, who is an ex-officio member of the three districts' boards. She said she had not been informed of the accountant's report or the Jersey City venture. "We anxiously await an audit," she said.
Peter L. Malkin, chairman of the Grand Central and 34th Street districts and the owner of several Manhattan office buildings, said it was a mistake to spend the New York money in Jersey City. He and Mr. Biederman said that it totaled only $2,000 and had been repaid by Jersey City officials.
Mr. Malkin said that he was familiar with Mr. Biederman's involvement in Jersey City, but that the venture had not yet been submitted to the full boards of the Grand Central and 34th Street districts.
He said he saw no conflict in Mr. Biederman and his staff working to create a New Jersey district. "We think it is our job to help wherever B.I.D.'s are being formed," he said, adding that the Grand Central and 34th Street districts had become models "to be replicated across the country at a time when government is cutting back."
Another city official said that the audit underscores the expanding interests of Mr. Biederman, who is paid $315,000 as president of the three New York districts.
Mr. Biederman would be paid $45,000 for the venture in Jersey City, although he would return $15,000 of that to the New York improvement districts. Under the conditions of his employment in New York, he can spend 15 percent of his time in Jersey City, but must return part of the salary he earns there.
"How can he devote 15 percent of his time to Jersey City when he is already running three B.I.D.'s?" asked Alan G. Hevesi, the New York City Comptroller, who is also an ex-officio board member of the districts presided over by Mr. Biederman. Mr. Hevesi, too, said he had not been informed of the accountant's report.
The audit, being prepared by KPMG Peat Marwick, was to have been filed with the city by Dec. 31, but it has not yet been turned over. Mr. Biederman attributed the delay to the complexities of the three large business improvement districts, which are not-for-profit corporations financed through property owner assessments, corporate contributions, income from services, like payments from banks to keep homeless people out of automatic teller machine vestibules.
He and Louis J. Mezzina, a partner in Peat Marwick, said the audit had been delivered to the districts' offices on March 15 and would be submitted to city officials this week.
In addition to revealing the ties to Jersey City, the audit also raised questions about the districts' bookkeeping, the appearance of what it called inequitable benefits to some of the members in the district and executive salaries that it said should be "reviewed for reasonableness."
For example, Peat Marwick said that the midtown districts had sometimes made special arrangements to "meet the requirements of a particular building owner."
It pointed to $1.3 million that was spent by the Grand Central Partnership to install floodlights on the Lincoln Building, an office tower owned by Mr. Malkin across 42d Street from Grand Central Terminal. The lights were part of a project to illuminate 42d Street and the south side of Grand Central. More of the district's money was spent on Mr. Malkin's building than on any other.
Mr. Malkin said the expenditure was necessary because more lights were installed on the Lincoln Building, requiring a different type of rack, extensive roof repairs and a new air-conditioner to replace one that was destroyed when the lights were installed.
Mr. Biederman first became involved in the Jersey City project through Thomas Kelly, a real estate executive who is vice president of a company in the Bryant Park business district and who has also been appointed chairman of the new Jersey City district.
Mr. Kelly said that the district was to include several blocks in the Journal Square commercial neighborhood. Unlike districts in New York City, which are financed chiefly through assessments from property owners, this one would receive $1.4 million of the projected $2 million annual budget from public funds.
A contract for Mr. Biederman and his Manhattan colleagues, who Mr. Kelly said had been advising him for several months without pay, is "on our lawyers' desks," Mr. Kelly said. He said the contract stipulated that Mr. Biederman's group would receive more than $220,000 for their year of management responsibility.
But Mr. Biederman said the amount proposed was $210,000. Of that, he said $110,000 would be designated for phone bills and other administrative costs, leaving $100,000 in salary for himself and his Manhattan employees.

HEADLINE: Clinton to Urge A Rights Code For Businesses Dealing Abroad

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 26

 BODY:
In its latest attempt to win back the good will of human rights groups disappointed by President Clinton's decision last year to extend trade benefits to China without tying them to human rights concerns, the White House will announce on Monday a voluntary code of human rights principles for American companies operating abroad.
After protracted argument within the Administration, Mr. Clinton decided last spring to bow to business interests who contended that the Administration's annual review of China's human rights performance was costing the United States billions in business in the world's most dynamic emerging market. 
 Now the Clinton Administration is once again trying to step lightly between two constituencies. Mr. Clinton has probably done more for American businesses operating abroad than any President in postwar history, turning the Commerce Department into a forceful advocate for American companies, pressing foreign governments to send business their way and raising trade issues to the top of the foreign policy agenda. But as the election approaches, Mr. Clinton also wants to protect his left flank after alienating many in the human rights community.
The chief problem is that America's allies, who are also its biggest economic competitors, have no such codes, and companies in those nations stand to sweep in business that the United States passes by. That issue was highlighted earlier this month when the Clinton Administration barred Conoco Inc. from entering a billion-dollar development deal with Iran. Now French oil companies are negotiating with Iran to replace Conoco.
It is unclear how many businesses will abide by the Administration's proposed code. Some executives reached last week said that if the wording is sufficiently weak or vague and appears unlikely to affect their competitiveness, there will probably be widespread adherence to what the Administration calls "a model of behavior."
But already there are signs that the final draft has so many compromises that it will disappoint the human rights groups it was trying to satisfy in the first place.
Details of the code have not been disclosed, but Administration officials acknowledge that many provisions governing the rights of foreign workers have been weakened in deference to the commercial realities of competing with the Japanese and Europeans.
People who have seen parts of the code say its strongest statements refer to bans on child labor and the use of prison laborers, actions that most large companies already say they oppose. The code will have deliberately vague language urging companies to respect the right of workers to organize and stops short of asking them to encourage such activities. The fear, several Administration officials said, is that such activity could be viewed in many nations -- including China -- as an effort to undermine national laws that strictly regulate labor activities.
The principles will be described to corporate executives, members of Congress and human rights groups by the White House on Monday. But the code will make no mention of paying a "fair wage" to workers in developing countries and emerging markets, and it sidesteps the question of how companies should treat workers who are prosecuted for engaging in political dissent in countries like China.
Nonetheless, the code is the biggest effort by the United States in two decades to create a minimum standard of conduct abroad for American companies. The last such effort was the passage of the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act of 1977, which bars American companies from bribing officials of foreign governments. That act has been widely criticized by American executives in recent years, who contend that while it is well intentioned, it often places them at a competitive disadvantage when they are bidding against companies from countries without comparable laws.
"We're back to the same problem again," a senior Administration official said. "This time we're juggling the interests of the business community and human rights, and discovering that it's an impossible balancing act."
In a declaration issued at the time he made the decision to extend China's trade privileges, Mr. Clinton committed himself to increased broadcasts into China by Radio Free Asia and the Voice of America, more support for nongovernmental human rights groups operating in China and "the development with American business leaders of a voluntary set of principles for business activity in China."
Progress on the first two commitments has been minimal. When the Administration began drafting the code of conduct, the State Department argued that developing such a code only for businesses operating in China would further irritate relations between the two countries. So President Clinton's plan quickly evolved into the development of what Secretary of State Warren Christopher described to Congress last month as "the promulgation of a worldwide standard for the conduct of American business, not focused primarily on China, but focused on the world as a whole."
Many private companies already have codes of conduct for their operations abroad, including running-shoe manufacturers like Reebok and Nike, which have advertised their standards and used them as a selling point with American consumers. An Administration official who was deeply involved in drafting the new guidelines said that the White House reviewed several of the corporate codes and also looked at standards published by the Hong Kong Chamber of Commerce, whose members have extensive manufacturing operations in China.
But in the course of consultation with the Administration last year, several executives warned the White House that its early drafts of the code would place a burden on American companies that was not shared by Japanese and European companies -- especially if the code appeared to create a standard for some kind of minimum wage.
"I give them credit, because no other Administration has even attempted to do something like this," said Mike Jendrzejczyk, the Washington director of Human Rights Watch/Asia, which was formerly known as Asia Watch. "But they waited too long, and the human rights situation in China has deteriorated" since President Clinton decided to extend what in the trade community is called "most favored nation" status to Beijing, he said.
"If the President had pulled C.E.O.'s into the Oval Office last May when he issued the M.F.N. decision," Mr. Jendrzejczyk said, "he might have generated broader business support. That was the time of maximum political advantage," because the Administration had just given the business community something it desperately sought.
