SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1637 words

HEADLINE: The Swing Vote Has Dim View Of Both Parties

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE 

DATELINE: MORENO VALLEY, Calif., March 26

 BODY:
Fifteen years ago this bedroom community of stucco houses and strip malls was little more than scrub land. Today it is ground zero in the battle for the American electorate.
The judgments of independent-minded voters like those that largely make up this middle-class enclave 75 miles east on the freeways from Los Angeles could determine whether Republicans or Democrats win control of the White House and Congress next year. The concentration of voters with no deep political roots or traditions explains why pollsters from both parties have been converging here. They are trying to figure out what the people want. 
 But dozens of random interviews with Moreno Valley residents suggest that the answer is not so simple as pronouncements from President Clinton about cutting taxes for the middle class or the passage of the House Republicans' Contract With America.
In spurning President George Bush in 1992, these voters helped fuel Mr. Clinton's victory. But now they are politically tortured. They complained that they were caught in a helpless predicament of declining wages, rising taxes and worsening crime. They wondered why politicians had not come to the rescue.
Jerry Nichols, the 40-year-old owner of a small computer supply company, said he voted for George Bush in 1988 and the independent candidate Ross Perot in 1992. He sees no reason to turn to the Republican or Democratic fold.
"Unless something is done to take care of the economy in the next two years there's going to be no middle class," Mr. Nichols said. "There will be just rich and poor. If Bill Clinton was helping me any more, I'd probably be bankrupt."
Indeed, the news in Moreno Valley -- representative of the fast-growing sprawl of seemingly endless suburbs east of Los Angeles called the "inland empire" -- is not heartening for either party. All but the most hard-core Democrats in this community of 130,000 expressed dissatisfaction with Mr. Clinton, to the point of downright hostility, over what many described as his flip-flopping on issues and his failure to deliver on promises for the middle class.
But Republicans do not seem to have made much headway either. Many residents said they were taking a wait-and-see attitude about the promised achievements of Newt Gingrich, the House Speaker. Some, not so hopeful, said they feared that Mr. Gingrich and his lieutenants had embarked on a course that was too extreme. Democratic officials say they will be poised to seize on those fears come election time.
"I don't think there's any future without these independent swing voters, and that's true for both Democrats and Republicans," said Stanley Greenberg, the President's pollster. "The parties are eyeing them and they are eyeing the parties. They're very much open."
For Mr. Clinton to win re-election, his advisers say he must again win California, the state with the most electoral votes, 54. And to win California, he must hang on to a substantial proportion of the voters in places like Moreno Valley.
In the interviews, most people who backed Mr. Perot in 1992 said they would probably do so again if he runs. Their point was that even with the upheaval in Washington and the Republicans' talk of reform, not much has changed: Politicians still cannot be trusted, and they still seem to be neglecting the middle class.
Most people, even many Republicans who said they always voted, responded with blank stares when asked about the Contract With America. And most were unaware of the Senate's passage last week of the line-item budget veto or House approval of its plan for an overhaul of the welfare system.
"I think the contract is going to be a tremendous boon for Republicans," said Tom Brooks, a 44-year-old Republican who builds steel-frame houses.
"What is it?" asked his wife, Karen, 45. "I'm ignorant."
"It's a deal with foreign countries," Mr. Brooks replied. Told that was not the case, he tried again, unsuccessfully: "Isn't it this trade thing with Mexico?"
Down the street, Cindy Polujancewicz, who described herself as a registered Democrat but said she had voted for Mr. Bush in 1992, said: "Contract? What do you mean?" But she had strong feelings about Mr. Gingrich. "He's a loudmouth and too aggressive," she said.
Asked what the parties should do to win her over, Ms. Polujancewicz, 35, who is married to a struggling tool dealer, said, "Be more concerned with your average person -- and don't pay attention just to the rich."
Over the last decade, Moreno Valley grew dramatically as people sought peace and quiet and escape from the high costs of Los Angeles. But the half-acre plots and well-manicured cactus gardens can be deceptive. In the aftermath of the economic downturn spurred by drastic military cutbacks in the area, the developments here are sprinkled with foreclosed homes. Some people who moved here have lost their jobs, cannot sell their houses -- whose values have plummeted -- and now have to contend with the gangs that migrated here from Los Angeles.
"This was a $259,000 home," said Mike Pagan, 51, a retired police officer whose house has been on the market for more than a year. "Now I'm selling it for $184,000. But it's not going to sell."
Mr. Pagan said that Mr. Gingrich's leadership was refreshing and that he would probably vote with the Republicans next time. But he said he was also watching carefully that the Republicans do not go too far. "We should dump it," Mr. Pagan said of the welfare system. "I don't believe we should have girls with babies running around picking up money for free. But that doesn't mean we should hurt people."
But Charles Johnson, 39, an ice cream store manager who explained that business was so bad that he had not gotten his usual bonus for two years, said neither party was addressing his needs. "I just did my taxes yesterday," he said. "I made less and paid more in taxes. We're too rich for food stamps but too poor to live in a good neighborhood."
Mr. Johnson said he typically voted for Democrats, but may back an independent in 1996. "I just don't see where anything he's done has done anything for me," Mr. Johnson said of Mr. Clinton.
Rob Oliver, who works for a securities company and describes himself as an independent-libertarian, said he might favor Democrats if he believed the party delivered on tax cuts for the middle class.
Would he vote for Mr. Clinton in that case? "There's no way I'd consider voting for Bill Clinton," replied the 36-year-old Mr. Oliver, adding that he had lost confidence that the President had any leadership skills.
Such talk obviously disturbs Democratic officials, who see a rise in Republican registrations among newcomers, although the two parties are about evenly split. A majority of voters in Moreno Valley, which is overwhelmingly white, backed Mr. Bush in 1988. But four years later, 41 percent favored Mr. Clinton, 34 percent voted for Mr. Bush and 23 percent turned to Mr. Perot.
"We have to do better," said Bob Mulholland, an adviser to the state Democratic Party. Noting that support for Democrats seems to have dwindled since 1992, he added, "It's absolutely key that we get at least 40 percent."
The Democrats should not count on Diane and George Mackay, who voted for Mr. Bush in 1988, Ross Perot in 1992 and have lost confidence in both the Democratic and Republican parties. Ms. Mackay does not work, and Mr. Mackay, after taking a buyout from his job in computers, has been unable to find a new one.
Mr. Mackay tried to play down his family's financial distress. "We're surviving," he said.
"By our fingernails," Ms. Mackay shot back.
Asked what she thought Mr. Gingrich and the Republicans had accomplished, Ms. Mackay, 48, shrugged. "Not much," she said. "I don't see any changes." And on some issues, like the passage of a law here to stiffen sentences for three-time offenders, Ms. Mackay said Republicans were going too far. "Are they going to take a whole state and make a prison out of it?" she asked.
The oratory from Washington about returning power to the states does not appear to have registered with Mr. Mackay. "I blame Democrats and Republicans for so much government," he said, as his 2-year-old grandson, Christopher, who lives there with the Mackays' daughter, watched cartoons on television. "I don't need their health care."
Asked what it would take for him to vote for Mr. Clinton in 1996, Mr. Mackay said that would never happen. "I'd be surprised if they ran Clinton again," he said.
But while Mr. Mackay may be a lost cause, Democrats should be worried about Nikki Heider, a reservations clerk for Amtrak who is worried that she may be laid off this week. Ms. Heider, a 39-year-old registered Democrat, said she voted for Mr. Clinton but was having second thoughts. "I'm disappointed in him and I'm upset that he's taking Amtrak funding away," she said. "I'm still a Democrat, but I'm listening to the Republicans more now."
Her husband, Dennis Heider, an engineering consultant, considers himself a right-leaning independent. Asked if there were any Democrats he would now vote for, he shook his head and remarked, "Truman died, right?"
Most voters interviewed said they had not given much thought to the Presidential field in 1996. Most had not heard of the Republican prospects. Even Gov. Pete Wilson's announcement last week of a committee to explore a possible candidacy was largely met with hostility. Almost every voter interviewed -- even Republicans who backed Mr. Wilson for re-election last year -- said they did not think he could win the Republican nomination.
Asked whom he would vote for, Mr. Pagan, the former police officer, offered a fairly typical response: "It won't be for Pete Wilson. That's for darn sure. He's not a guy you can trust, whether he thinks he's conservative or not."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Rob Oliver is among voters in Moreno Valley, Calif., who are critical of the political leadership. HEADLINE: Making Good Psychiatric Care Cost-Effective;
Costly Dental Cuts

 BODY:
To the Editor:
We applaud "The Albany Budget Games Begin" (editorial, March 15) and would like to supplement your observations by focusing on the proposed elimination of adult dental Medicaid services. This shortsighted proposal is economically unsound and destructive to the health of the neediest segment of our population. 
 Supposedly saving $17.1 million by not allowing for preventative dental care while retaining payment for only the more costly emergency treatment places health care economic theory back 20 years.
Should we not insist that caries be prevented and if necessary treated before the decay results in the need for more costly emergency root canal therapy or extraction? If New York State were to reimburse under Medicaid only for emergency dental treatment, it would require Medicaid to expend conservatively $35 million more than the proposed budget has projected.
To make the situation worse, it is more than likely that more than 900 private-practice dentists who primarily serve the indigent Medicaid population, including children, will be forced to close their offices and terminate the jobs of some 3,000 assistants, clerical aides and hygienists. What will be the economic impact of lost income-tax revenues and increased unemployment expenses?
Add to this the tens of millions of dollars in increased reimbursement by New York State Medicaid when even children covered under the program seek treatment at more expensive hospital clinics. Human suffering, unnecessary government spending and lost revenue are not rational solutions to our budget problems.
 
VINCENT V. LA BRUNA, D.D.S.
President, First District
Dental Society of New York
New York, March 17, 1995

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

SmithKline Beecham Clears A Hurdle on Tagamet Sales
An F.D.A. advisory committee recommended that a less potent form of the anti-ulcer drug Tagamet be sold for treating heartburn without a prescription. 
 The committee insisted, however, that the drug include warnings that it might interact with some prescription medicines. The ruling follows two unsuccessful attempts by SmithKline Beecham to make its drug, which lost patent protection last May, available over the counter. D4.
 
Value Health to Buy Diagnostek
Value Health has agreed to buy Diagnostek, a prescription benefit manager, for stock valued at $480 million, or $18.85 a share. D4.
Dow Rises in Late Trading
 
The Dow surged in the last half hour of trading, closing up 18.67 points at 4,157.34. That is the eighth record close in the last 12 sessions. Treasury securities also rallied. In currency markets, the dollar fell to 1.4050 marks, but rose to 89.40 yen. D12.
 
American Express Plans Buyback
American Express stock rose $1.25 to an eight-year high after the company said it would buy back as much as 8 percent of its stock. D7.

Home Sales Fell 5% During February
Sales of existing single-family houses fell 5 percent to 3.43 million units in February, as higher interest rates and fears about the slowing economy depressed the housing market. Many analysts expect the Government to report on Wednesday that new-home sales also deteriorated in February. D11.
 
Bid for Turner Stock Dropped
Tele-Communications said it was no longer interested in buying Time Warner's 19.4 percent stake in Turner Broadcasting. D9.
 
USAir Pilots Agree to Furloughs
The pilots' union at USAir has agreed to furloughs, according to people close to the negotiations. The union agreed to furloughs of up to 300 pilots, or 6 percent. D2.
 
Baseball Owners Face Injunction
A Federal judge set a hearing for Friday on a request by the N.L.R.B. for an injunction against baseball owners. B15.
 
Suit Filed Over Bally Name
Bally Entertainment filed suit to strip its former gaming-machine subsidiary, Bally Gaming, of the Bally name, contending that the company's problems with gambling regulators had damaged the trademark. D8.
 
I.B.M Announces Digital Ventures
I.B.M. announced projects to assist companies and institutions to transform text, art, films and music into digital form that can be transmitted over computer networks. D3.
 
Companies Plan Internet Service
MCI plans to become a national Internet service provider, offering residential and business access to the Internet. Also, Pacific Bell is expected to announce today an alliance that is intended to offer business Internet services in California. D3.
 
Auto Investment Rising in Brazil
Ford, G.M., Volkswagen and Fiat are doubling their investments in Brazilian factories as the country becomes one of the world's big car producers. D6.
 
Boston Ad Agency Wins Volkswagen
Volkswagen of America selected Arnold Fortuna Lawner & Cabot to handle its North American account with billings estimated at $90 million to $110 million. The Boston agency bested three far more familiar rivals to win the account. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. D9.
 
Orange County Getting Nervous
There is a sense in Orange County, Calif., that things may never be the same after December's multibillion-dollar bankruptcy. People are talking about a future of closed libraries, volunteer fire departments, parent aides at schools, rising crime and taxes. A14.

HEADLINE: President Goes on Offensive With Call for Spending Cuts

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 27

 BODY:
President Clinton opened an ambitious new political agenda today by proposing spending cuts to shrink the size of NASA and three other agencies beyond what his budget recommended seven weeks ago.
The further cuts would amount to $13.1 billion over the next five years, far short of what Congressional Republicans are preparing to recommend. The President's proposal comes as he prepares for his most intensive schedule of domestic travel since the midterm elections last year. He and his advisers used the announcement to call renewed attention to the differences between what they portrayed as their common-sense approach to paring spending and a wrongheaded Republican alternative. 
 "You can reinvent Government and cut costs to taxpayers without a mean spirit or a meat ax," Mr. Clinton said during an appearance at the Federal Communications Commission, at which he announced the cuts.
Those blunt words suggested that the President has become emboldened by the success that Democrats have found in casting Republican budget-cutting plans as heartless. And as Mr. Clinton travels to Georgia, Florida, Arkansas, Texas and California during the next two weeks, his aides said today, that message will be a recurrent theme.
For Mr. Clinton, who has been overshadowed by the Republicans' new domination of the Capitol, the back-to-back journeys to the South and to the West Coast may prove an opportunity for political rehabilitation.
With much of his attention focused on the House Republicans' effort to pass the Contract with America into law, Mr. Clinton has not made even a one-day political journey outside of Washington in more than a month. But now that Congress seems likely to leave town for the Easter recess before it can reach agreement on any more important legislation, Mr. Clinton is eager, White House officials say, to return to the hustings.
Beginning in Atlanta, where on Wednesday he will preside over a daylong conference on the region's economy, Mr. Clinton hopes to win more credit for the nation's prosperity while emphasizing that he shares the sentiments of those eager to shift more power to the states.
To reinforce that embrace of Federalism, the President is scheduled to address the Florida Legislature in Tallahassee on Thursday, his first appearance in a statehouse since taking office.
The new cuts in Federal spending that he proposed today would give the states more authority in some respects. For example, Maryland and Virginia would be required to maintain what are now Federal roadways along the Potomac River, and states would be required to collect mine royalties now handled by the Federal Government.
At a White House briefing today, some of Mr. Clinton's top deputies took turns drawing comparisons between the President's proposals and those put forward by Republicans, like the legislation approved by the House last week to give the states broad authority over the social welfare system.
Elaine Kamarck, a senior policy adviser to Vice President Al Gore, said, "We believe there is a right way to cut Government and a wrong way, and we believe we are doing it the right way."
The changes recommended by Mr. Clinton emerged from the latest phase of the Administration's review, which is headed by Mr. Gore and has been greatly accelerated since the Republicans gained control of Congress. Beyond NASA, they would force consolidations in the Interior Department, the Small Business Administration and the Federal Emergency Management Agency.
Together with the $144 billion in savings that Mr. Clinton had already presented, the new proposals would amount to substantial shrinkage over the next five years in departments and agencies like NASA. The combined effect of reductions now planned at the space agency, including some that have been under way for several years, could cut as many as a total of 55,000 jobs from the Government payroll and space-related industries, Administration officials said today.
But the President's new five-year proposal amounts to about $4 billion less than the House has already voted to strip from the current budget alone, and its unveiling today is bound to intensify the rivalry between the White House and Capitol Hill on questions of taxing and spending.
The tax cuts that Mr. Clinton has already proposed are also much smaller than those that the Republicans have embraced, and their benefits would fall mainly to Americans who earned less than $70,000 a year.
To consolidate support around that limited approach, the White House remains intent on calling attention to the scope of the Republicans' budget-cutting plans, arguing that it leaves no doubt that the Republicans want to cut spending for the young and poor in order to cut taxes for the wealthy.
But aides to Mr. Clinton said today that he might well avoid direct attacks on particular Republican plans when he begins his Southern swing on Tuesday afternoon. They said he believed that the 1996 elections were still too far away for such outright partisanship, and the need to work with the Republican majority would make that stance counterproductive.
But they also said that by emphasizing his approach to issues like the economy, crime and education, the President hoped to call attention to what he had already done, while inviting comparisons about the two parties' proposals for the future.
The seven-day journey will also include a stop in Haiti on Friday, when the United States will hand over authority for the peacekeeping mission to the United Nations. The trip ends on Monday, when the President is to return to Washington after a weekend in Arkansas, where he is to make a political appearance. He is to begin a three-day trip to Texas and California on April 7.
Mr. Clinton has rarely spent much uninterrupted time in a single region of the country. Aides said today that he had grown frustrated with itineraries that have him hopscotching from state to state. As a native of the South, they said, Mr. Clinton chose that region for the first of four planned economic conferences reminiscent of the one he convened in Arkansas shortly before taking office.

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Clinton proposed $13.1 billion in new spending cuts yesterday, more than he announced seven weeks ago but less than Congressional Republicans want. He talked at a news conference with Interior Secretary Bruce Babbitt, whose department would be one of four Federal agencies affected. (David Scull/The New York Times)

 President Clinton's plan to consolidate operations at four agencies over five years:

Extend two buyout offers that have already caused more than 1,500 of its 23,000 civil service workers to leave. An additional 500 are expected to follow.
 
Est. Savings: $8 billion
Jobs cut: 2,000
 
INTERIOR DEPARTMENT
 
Propose eliminating the Minerals Management Service and transfer royalty collection duties to states and tribes.
 
Eliminate the office that deals with United States territories.
 
Accelerate the transfer of bureau of Indian Affairs programs to tribes.
 
Transfer management of three Washington-area highways to Maryland and Virginia.
 
Alter procedures for companies paying offshore oil and gas royalties.
 
Est. Savings: $3.8 billion
Jobs cut: 2,000
 
SMALL BUSINESS ADMINISTRATION
 
Drop subsidies the Government pays on loans, imposing fees on lenders and borrowers.
 
Consolidate field offices.
 
Move more programs from headquarters to less costly field offices
 
Est. Savings: $1.2 billion
Jobs cut: 500
 
FEDERAL EMERGENCY MANAGEMENT AGENCY
 
Sign contracts with governors to reduce reporting requirements and make it easier to respond to disasters.
 
Provide incentives to states to establish their own emergency funds.
 
Require states to pay for uninsured public repairs equal to a deductible level established by the Federal Government.

Jobs cut: 305

HEADLINE: Orange County Faces Future With Anxiety

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS 

DATELINE: NEWPORT BEACH, Calif., March 27

 BODY:
Three months after risky investments collapsed and plunged Orange County into bankruptcy, residents here are suffering new anxieties about the future, an unusual turn for people living in one of the most comfortable counties in the nation.
The full pain of the county's fiscal crisis is still to come. But people are already talking about fears of a future with fewer libraries, fire departments run by volunteers, parents filling in for teachers, coaches and janitors, and property values dragged down by the prospect of it all. 
 Even the unthinkable seems increasingly likely here in the nation's conservative, anti-tax heartland: a half-cent increase in the 7.75 percent sales tax collected in the county. After a preliminary vote last week, the five county supervisors are expected on Tuesday to put a proposed sales tax increase on the ballot for a vote in June. The current sales tax includes a state levy of 7.25 cents per $1 and a county levy of 0.50 cents for $1 for transportation.
To the surprise of many, a majority of respondents in a poll conducted this month by The Orange County Register said they favored increasing the sales tax. But the county's ardent anti-tax groups are gearing up for battle.
"We'll be opposing the sales tax tooth and toenail," said Tom Rogers, a rancher who is a former chairman of the county's Republican Party and now leads a private group, Citizens Against Unfair Taxation. Some people have threatened to try for a recall vote on any supervisor who votes to put the tax question on the ballot.
The likelihood of sharp cuts in services and new taxes has reinforced anxieties in recent years over the state's lingering recession and cutbacks in the aerospace industry.
They are affecting the swimming pool salesman who is uncertain about his business for the coming summer and the college president who fears that her salary may be cut. The administrator of a local hospital says she is preparing for the admissions of more people suffering from anxiety and depression.
In one indicator of a more uncertain mood, business is up at Proos Security, which trains and rents out security guards. Hard times breed crime, or at least fears of crime, said the agency's owner, Patricia Johnson.
Mark Baldassere, chairman of the urban and regional planning department at the University of California at Irvine, said: "The impacts of the bankruptcy are still being discovered. Every day a new rock is turned over and something else is discovered, and it's, 'Oh, gosh, we didn't think of that.' "
Last week, the county's chief executive, William J. Popejoy, presented his most detailed outline yet of a recovery plan: a mix of asset sales, privatization, layoffs, cuts in services, new bond issues and the half-cent sales tax increase.
Under Mr. Popejoy's plan, the sharpest and most immediate squeeze will be felt at the bottom, among the county's homeless, its disabled veterans, its mentally ill, its elderly, its abused children and its welfare recipients. Of 1,600 layoffs and staff reductions he proposed among county workers, two-thirds would come in the social services.
But the cuts will affect everyone, with a reduction in services that are rarely noticed until they are gone, said Tobye Lovelace, staff manager at the Orange County Employees Association.
"It's the county that makes sure that when you buy a gallon of gas, you are getting a gallon of gas," Ms. Lovelace said. "They check to make sure the restaurants are clean. They send the building inspectors to assess property."
And in a trickledown of hardship that is just beginning, the county's schools have tightened their belts severely, and cities and towns face their own layoffs, cuts in services and increases in fees.
The lightest burden will fall on the rich, who despite their abhorrence of taxes, comfort themselves, as one shop owner here reported, by saying, "The county's broke, but we're not."
At The Money Man, a gallery where it is possible to buy money in a frame, sales have dropped on the $200 and $300 items like Lincoln and Grant bills. But the luxury offerings, like a $14,000 Lincoln manuscript, are still selling merrily.
"Did you see our yacht parade this year?" asked Shirley Arconti, a saleswoman at The Money Man. "People were saying there would be fewer lights, but it was spectacular. It was one of the best we've had. Of course, here in Newport Beach, we live in our own little shell."
One customer at the shop, Avery W. Willis 3d, an executive with Scott Worldwide, the paper company, said cuts in social services might offer "a benefit in disguise" in persuading the county's growing number of poor people to move.
"That's a crude way to talk about it, but it's real," Mr. Willis said. "Let them go live in New York."
But other shoppers here at the Fashion Island mall spoke of an emerging sense of community and a spirit of volunteerism as people join to shoulder the new burdens, and especially to keep the county's schools afloat.
Whatever the economic and social divisions of the county, the fate of the schools is a broadly shared concern, crucial to both the future of Orange County's children and its property values.
June Sinclair, on a visit to the mall with her 6-year-old son, Jordan, who had just had his first tooth pulled, said that in her home town of Irvine, the Great Harvest Bakery had turned itself over for a day to the El Camino Real Elementary School, letting teachers and parents bake bread for sale to raise money for the school.
The Irvine school district, which had invested $100 million in the failed county fund, has cut its current budget by $3 million and is still struggling to find ways not to shortchange its students.
Mrs. Sinclair, whose husband is an anesthesiologist, said she had initiated an advertising newsletter at the school for local businesses that include real estate agents, travel agencies, accountants and house painters. Any that gain business from the newsletter would make cash donations to the school.
Valery J. Deveraux, a real estate lawyer and mother of two teen-agers, described school meetings at which parents are offering to teach math class, coach sports or perform janitorial services.
But volunteerism can go only so far. Ms. Deveraux said that bankruptcy filings had already begun to rise in the county and that there were fears that possible cuts in court personnel could jam an already overloaded court system.
She said some unincorporated areas, including her own home of Dove Canyon, might join together as new towns, thereby reducing their dependence on the county and contracting out for fire, police and other services.
One sector of the population has already felt the pain of the bankruptcy in all its force. About 150 county workers have lost their jobs so far, with hundreds more to follow.
Until this month, Lena Gniadek, 69, worked in the map unit of the county's registrar of voters. One day without warning, she said, she was handed a packet of severance papers and told to leave.
"I just walked back to my desk and took whatever I could after 13 years, and just left within 20 minutes," she said. "I remember it was raining and blustery and I got into my car and that was the end of my working life, and I was left to try and figure out what my life was all about."

HEADLINE: Mayor Asks Broad Powers Over Fulton Fish Market

BYLINE: By SELWYN RAAB 

 BODY:
The Giuliani administration, unveiling details of plans to rid the Fulton Fish Market of mobsters and corruption, yesterday asked the City Council for broad powers to determine who can do business and who can work at the market.
The proposed legislation would give administration officials the power to license, regulate and investigate all business owners and employees at the market. It would also give City Hall the right to fingerprint all vendors and workers as well as to conduct background investigations if any of them are suspected of being linked to organized crime. 
 But some Council members raised the issue of potential civil rights violations, questioning the administration's need for fingerprinting and background investigations.
Several business owners at the market and community leaders also questioned the need for any change in existing practices at the lower Manhattan market. One fish dealer warned that the proposal by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani would drive legitimate dealers out of the city.
The proposed legislation to revise the city's existing regulatory authority over the market was conceived by the administration and top Council leaders and its provisions were disclosed yesterday at a hearing of the Council's Committee on Economic Development. Despite opposition, aides to Peter F. Vallone, the leader of the Council, said they expected the bill to be approved without significant amendments. After Council approval, Mr. Giuliani would sign the bill into law.
"Business as usual at the Fulton Fish Market cannot continue," Randy Mastro, chief of staff to Mayor Giuliani, told the committee. "We must act."
Other administration officials and supporters of the bill said that Federal and state law enforcement efforts over the last half century, through occasional prosecutions of reputed mobsters and the appointment of a temporary monitor, had failed to uproot the Genovese crime family or to end abuses. The bill's proponents said new tactics must be used in attempts to diminish entrenched mob influence in commercial enterprises in the city like the market, the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center and the Garment Center.
The Fulton Market, the nation's largest wholesale fish market, is mainly on city property on the East River and South Street, extending from Fulton Street north to Peck Slip. About 125 million pounds of fish are sold each year at the market for distribution to retail stores and restaurants in the New York metropolitan area.
Although various city agencies were empowered to regulate the market, the Giuliani administration, which took office in January 1994, said that previous administrations had largely abdicated responsibility to administer it and had allowed unlicensed vendors to operate without supervision.
Mr. Mastro said that mob influence, violence, extortion and fraud were partly responsible for fish and seafood suppliers on the Atlantic and Gulf coasts and Chesapeake Bay from shunning the market in favor of wholesale distribution points in other cities. He said that business at the Fulton Market had declined to less than $1 billion annually from about $3 billion in the early 1980's.
Mr. Mastro said the drop in shipments to the Fulton Market and hidden charges extracted by mob figures contribute to higher consumer prices. While he was unable to pinpoint the extra charges, Mr. Mastro said, "It is a corruption and crime tariff that inflates prices."
Under the proposed law, the Commissioner of the Business Services Department would establish regulations for all businesses operating in the market, and all employees and vendors would be required to wear photo identification cards. The bill would authorize the Commissioner to ban certain companies and employees from operating there.
The Commissioner would have strong regulatory authority over the flow of fish and seafood as it arrives and leaves the market. A particular target of the bill are companies known as unloaders and loaders.
Truckers who transport produce in refrigerated trucks have complained to Federal and city authorities that six unloading companies set prices and decide how perishable supplies are unloaded and taken to wholesalers on South Street.
A Federal judge in 1993 ruled that the six unloading companies were a cartel and imposed fines on them totaling more than $100,000.
A dozen companies known as loaders assign parking spaces to retailers and restaurateurs who purchase supplies in the hours before dawn when the market thrives. Retailers and restaurateurs say that because of intimidation they do not carry their own purchases back to their trucks but pay parking and loading charges set by the companies.
The bill would authorize the Business Services Commissioner to license loading and unloading companies and to investigate their owners and employees. At the hearing, Mr. Mastro said about 100 owners and workers would be subject to fingerprinting and criminal investigations.
Michael Driansky, a wholesale vendor and president of the Committee to Save the Fulton Fish Market, asserted that the bill would encourage dealers to move to New Jersey.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Car Makers Gaining Momentum in Brazil

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: SAO PAULO, Brazil

 BODY:
Aiming to make Brazil a leading world car manufacturer by the end of the decade, the four biggest car makers in Brazil are doubling their investment, and new companies are planning to start assembly lines in this country.
In rapid succession over the last few weeks, Ford announced a $2 billion investment; General Motors, a $2 billion expansion, and Volkswagen, $3 billion. Fiat said it intended to invest $1 billion in Brazil and $600 million in Argentina. Fiat's operation in Brazil is the Italian company's largest and most profitable. 
 "We are making investments far in excess of what we have ever done here," said Gurminder Bedi, president of Ford's Argentina and Brazil divisions. "In developed countries, automobile markets grow by 2 percent a year. Here, it could easily grow by 5 to 7 percent."
After sharply increasing vehicle production since 1990 -- to 1,583,000 last year from 915,000 -- Brazil intends to double production again by the year 2000. Surpassing Italy in 1993 and the former republics of the Soviet Union in 1994, Brazil expects this year to overtake Britain.
Its boom is part of a larger shift of car manufacturing to third world countries with cheaper labor costs. In general, auto production stagnated in the last decade in the world's four largest car-making countries -- the United States, Japan, Germany and France. In the same period, it doubled in India, tripled in Mexico, quadrupled in China and increased eightfold in South Korea.
The Koreans built their industry by nurturing national companies behind high tariff walls that blocked virtually all auto imports. Brazil encouraged multinational companies to set up manufacturing operations behind similar tariff barriers. But since 1990, Brazil has dropped import bans, forcing manufacturers to upgrade their assembly lines to successfully compete with the rest of the world.
As investments pour into Brazil's automobile industry, centered in the industrial belt around Sao Paulo, Brazilian-made cars are expected to win more international acceptance. Last year, about a quarter of the cars made in Brazil were exported.
For the moment, though, car makers are scrambling to keep up with domestic demand. Brazil is enjoying its lowest inflation in a decade -- about 1.5 percent monthly. From a monthly high of 50 percent last June, inflation has been driven down by a combination of a balanced budget and a shift to a new, dollar-linked currency. With consumer confidence soaring, Brazil's economy expanded last year by 5.7 percent, the highest rate in a decade. Growth this year is expected to exceed 5 percent.
"While Mexico has 8.5 inhabitants per vehicle, Brazil has 11.6," said Luiz Adelar Scheuer, a Mercedes-Benz executive who is president of the National Association of Vehicle Manufacturers. Mercedes makes trucks and buses in this country.
Given the strong consumer demand, both Volkswagen and General Motors recently started third production shifts at factories near here. General Motors plans to concentrate in Brazil its worldwide production of the Corsa, a compact car that is enormously popular in this country.
With the economic situation radically changed, a consortium called Autolatina will cease to exist by the end of the month. This Brazilian-Argentine production operation was formed by Ford and Volkswagen during hard times a decade ago. Since late last year, the two car companies have been phasing out Autolatina's operations.
Volkswagen plans to double its production in Brazil to one million vehicles by the end of the 1990's. And Ford intends to make Brazil the worldwide production center for the Fiesta. [On Monday, Alexander J. Trotman, Ford's chairman and chief executive, arrived from Detroit for a two-day visit to study his company's investment strategy in this country and to meet with the Brazilian President, Fernando Henrique Cardoso.]
Joining the parade of international automobile executives coming through Brazil will be Jose Ignacio Lopez de Arriortua, Volkswagen's vice president for international production, who will come here next month to announce the site of a new truck-manufacturing plant. It is to serve as Volkswagen's world production center for trucks.
And for the first time in almost two decades ago, new companies are announcing plans to build passenger cars. Earlier this month, Cho Rae Seung, president of Asia Motors Corporation of South Korea, told Vice President Marco Maciel that his company planned to build a $150 million factory in Brazil next year. January sales show that Asia Motors' Towner van and the Besta van made by its parent, Kia Motors, were the leading import models made by companies that do not make cars in Brazil.
About the same time, Renault's chairman and president, Louis Schweitzer, met in Brasilia with the nation's President. Renault is a popular import, and, according to the local press, the French car maker is planning to open a Brazilian assembly line.
Peugeot's managing director, Yves Barbe, said on a visit here Sunday that his company was studying the feasibility of making cars in Brazil. Toyota, which began producing jeeps in Brazil in 1959, is considering manufacturing passenger cars on an industrial site it has purchased near here.
But manufacturers' interest began to cool last fall, when the Government cut taxes on imported cars, to 20 percent from 32 percent. This fueled a surge in imports that had started five years ago, when the import ban was lifted. Last year, Brazilians imported 193,516 cars -- a twelvefold increase over the 1991 level. Brazil thereby replaced Venezuela as the United States' leading Latin American market for its cars.
With imports accounting for one-fourth of all car sales in December, the manufacturers said they would not invest further without more protection. In February, they reached a deal with the Government. Manufacturers promised to invest to double production by the end of the decade. In turn, the Brazilian President promised "to end this orgy of importing cars." He restored the import tariff to 32 percent, returning to a former schedule that had called for a 20 percent tariff in 2001.
Even with the 32 percent tariff, however, imports are expected to almost double this year, reaching 350,000, roughly the same level as exports.
For now, the car industry does not have to look too far away for markets. Last year, 87 percent of Brazil's car exports went to South America, largely to Argentina. With Latin American sales increasing 10 percent, Brazil has a strong regional market.
Within three years, this country's automobile industry is expected to surpass Canada's, making Brazil the second-largest car-making nation in the Western Hemisphere, after the United States.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Lower costs have helped attract capital to Brazil's auto industry. "We are making investments far in excess of what we have ever done here," said Gurminder Bedi, president of the Ford Motor Company's Argentina and Brazil divisions, leaning on a Brazilian-made Fiesta. (John Maier Jr. for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Germany Privatizing Phone Service in '98

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: BONN, March 27

 BODY:
Germany will open its telephone service market to competition on Jan. 1, 1998, in a move that could lower prices and create better services for consumers, the Government said today.
An unlimited number of private companies will be allowed to apply for licenses, the Post and Telecommunications Minister, Wolfgang Boetsch, said. The German telecommunications market has long been dominated by the state phone monopoly, Deutsche Telekom A.G. Deutsche Telekom, which the Government is privatizing, will begin selling shares next year. 
 The Economics Minister, Gunter Rexrodt, had pushed to open the German market sooner. But other German officials reportedly thought that the privatization of Deutsche Telekom might be damaged by quick exposure to foreign competition.
"The date of 1998 was set at the highest levels of the German Government," Mr. Boetsch said, noting that all European Union countries are supposed to open their telecommunications markets by that date.
Major American and other foreign telecommunications companies have been eager to get into the German market, which was worth $43 billion in 1992, the world's third largest after the United States and Japan.
The first licenses to private companies should be granted early in 1997, Mr. Boetsch said, but he declined to speculate how many would be granted.
United States regulators have considered rules that could stall a bid by Deutsche Telekom and France Telecom to buy 20 percent of the Sprint Corporation because of complaints by other United States carriers about limited access to Germany's market.

HEADLINE: Canadians Cut the Nets Of Spain Ship

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH 

DATELINE: TORONTO, March 27

 BODY:
The Canadian Coast Guard has cut the nets of a Spanish trawler just outside Canada's territorial waters east of Newfoundland in a renewal of the battle with the European Union over North Atlantic fishing rights.
The action was announced late Sunday in Ottawa after a confrontation on the high seas involving five Canadian patrol vessels and a dozen Spanish trawlers. 
 No shots were fired, but Canadians chased a second Spanish trawler they had also tried to board. That trawler suddenly lifted its nets and ran for the protection of a Spanish Navy patrol vessel. The Canadians abandoned the chase because of what officials said was heavy fog.
"They're all out of the zone, so the activity of today has produced the result that we were aiming to get," Prime Minister Jean Chretien told reporters in Ottawa.
The Spaniards were fishing in the breeding grounds of the Grand Banks, an area Canada has unilaterally declared off limits because of a sharply declining fish population. The disappearance of cod and other species has left some 50,000 Canadian east coast fishermen and fish plant workers unemployed.
The phenomenon has affected the United States as well. In December the Commerce Department ordered the emergency closing of more than 6,000 square miles of fishing area off the Massachusetts coast.
Sunday's high seas drama followed the seizure on March 9 of another Spanish trawler, also operating just outside Canada's 200-mile territorial boundary.
That trawler, the Estai, was brought into St. John's, Newfoundland, and its master was arrested. But a week later he and the vessel were released after its owners posted a bond of $355,000.
Canada and the European Union have vowed to try to resolve their dispute diplomatically. But Spanish trawlers have remained in the disputed area, under the protection of a naval patrol vessel, the Vigia.
On Sunday the Canadian Government increased the conflict by authorizing the use for the first time of a large scissors-like device called a warp-cutter, which slashes cables that attach nets to a trawler.
When the Pescamaro Uno refused to be boarded Sunday, the Canadian cutter Sir Wilfred Grenfell moved alongside and deployed the warp-cutter. The trawler lost an estimated $70,000 worth of gear besides the value of its catch. Then the second trawler, the Verdel, pulled its nets and fled.
Canada says the dwindling stocks must be protected by toughly enforced international conservation measures beyond territorial waters. It has accused the Spanish fishermen of using undersized nets and catching species protected by fishing bans and keeping secret sets of books.
The European Union has denied the allegations and accused Canada of piracy. Yet while condemning Canada for cutting the nets of the trawler, ambassadors of the 15 member countries at a meeting today in Brussels refused to invoke sanctions, presumably hoping to prevent further confrontations.
The Europeans and the Canadians accuse each other of overfishing. After trying unsuccessfully to resolve their differences in talks in Brussels and Vancouver, Canada and the European Union shifted the venue of their debate today to the United Nations in New York.
Although public opinion data is not yet in, newspaper editorials and radio talk shows indicate that the public likes the Government's new gunboat diplomacy.
"The minister is saying the right things that attract a large amount of support in the country," says John Wright, senior vice president of the Angus Reid Group Inc., a national polling organization.
But some Canadian voice disquiet. "This kind of behavior," a recent editorial in The Globe and Mail said, "is jarringly out of character for a country whose greatest international role has been as peacemaker and conciliator."

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Fed Governor to Step Down; Clinton Gets a 3d Vacancy

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 27

 BODY:
John P. LaWare, a Federal Reserve Board governor with a reputation for favoring higher interest rates and opposing the Clinton Administration's banking policies, announced his resignation today, to take effect April 30.
His departure gives President Clinton his third chance to fill a vacancy on the powerful board in less than a year. The replacement of any single governor tends to have little effect on interest rate policy. But the Administration's two appointees to the board have shown slightly more willingness than their Republican colleagues to discuss publicly the risks of higher unemployment posed by Federal Reserve efforts to fight inflation by pushing up interest rates. 
 The Fed's top interest rate policy committee, comprising the 7 Federal Reserve Board governors and 5 of the 12 Federal Reserve regional bank presidents, is scheduled to meet again on Tuesday. The Fed announced today that in keeping with tradition for departing officials, Mr. LaWare would not attend the meeting.
Financial analysts said that with the economy showing clear signs of slowing, the Fed was highly unlikely to raise interest rates again at the meeting, with or without Mr. LaWare there. "I think this is an easy meeting for the Fed -- no change and waiting for the data on the second quarter," said Allen Sinai, the chief economist at Lehman Brothers.
Mr. LaWare said in an interview that he had announced his resignation shortly before the meeting partly because a resignation shortly after a meeting might be misinterpreted as a policy difference.
"I passed my 67th birthday last month and I'm ready for a change of pace," he said. "I plan to work on my golf handicap, and beyond that I don't have any specific plans."
When the committee voted not to raise interest rates in December, Mr. LaWare took the uncommon step of dissenting in favor of higher rates. But he has changed his tone over the last several months, becoming one of several governors to comment on early signs of economic slowing this spring.
"There are signs of a slowing down, but it's a slowing down in the rate of growth -- it's not the kind of slowing down that says you're in a nose dive," he said in a wide-ranging interview on March 10.
Mr. LaWare has been known at the Federal Reserve chiefly for his outspoken positions on bank regulation. He heads the board's bank regulatory committee and led the Fed's successful fight last year to stop the Administration's plan to consolidate banking regulatory agencies, a move that would have taken away some of the Fed's considerable powers over large banks.
Before joining the Federal Reserve in 1988, Mr. LaWare was the chairman of the Shawmut National Corporation, which was a big Boston banking company at that time but has since set up a headquarters in Hartford after expanding into Con necticut. Mr. LaWare is the only former banker on the Federal Reserve Board now, and in his letter of resignation to President Clinton today, he urged that another banker be named to replace him.
"The quality of board deliberations and decisions on banking matters is enhanced by the direct participation of a member with personal private-sector experience in banking," Mr. LaWare wrote.
The resignation took the White House by surprise, and Deputy Treasury Secretary Frank N. Newman said that it was premature to speculate whether the Administration would nominate another commercial banker. He also said the Administration could not choose a replacement and win Senate confirmation for the nominee before Mr. LaWare stepped down. "April 30 is virtually impossible," Mr. Newman said.
The Administration is under no obligation to follow Mr. LaWare's advice to appoint another banker, but, after appointing two academicians last year, the White House might find it easier to win confirmation for a banker from the Republican-controlled Senate.
Edward L. Yingling, the chief lobbyist for the American Bankers Association, said, "We will urge that a banker be appointed -- it's very important given the tremendous role that the Fed has in bank regulation."
But Allen J. Fishbein, the general counsel at the Center for Community Change, a Washington-based advocacy and research group that focuses on issues of fair lending, said President Clinton should appoint someone who would look out for the interests of working Americans and borrowers. "This is a Federal Reserve Board that all too well reflects the banking industry's interests, whether or not they come directly out of commercial banking," he said.
There are some limits on finding an eligible banker because of a requirement that no two governors may come from the same Federal Reserve regional district. The New York and West Coast districts are already represented on the board, excluding bankers in those regions from consideration.
Early speculation in banking circles today focused on John G. Medlin Jr., the 61-year-old chairman of the Wachovia Corporation, a big regional bank based in Winston-Salem, N.C. Mr. Medlin has already announced plans to retire this year, and he is said to know both President Clinton and Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman.
Another possible nominee -- although not a banker -- is Alicia H. Munnell, the Assistant Secretary of the Treasury for Economic Policy.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Extend a Rally On Slow Home Sales Data

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities rallied yesterday for a second consecutive session, helped by a report on home sales that confirmed the market's view of a slowing economy.
The advance came despite a weaker dollar. A surge in the dollar helped set off the bond rally on Friday, but yesterday the dollar retreated against the German mark and other European currencies. 
 The market instead focused its attention on a sign indicating an economic slowdown. With each such report, traders and analysts are increasingly confident that the Federal Reserve is closer to achieving its goal of slower economic growth and little inflation. Indeed, few market participants expect that the Federal Open Market Committee, the Fed's policy-making committee, will raise short-term interest rates when its meets today. The Fed has raised interest rates seven times in the last 13 months.
Prices rose yesterday after the National Association of Realtors reporting that sales of existing single-family homes fell 5 percent last month to an annual rate of 3.43 million units -- the lowest level in two years. Economists had expected sales to fall 1.1 percent, to an annual rate of 3.55 million.
The price of the 30-year bond rose 17/32 , to 10324/3 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from its price, fell to 7.31 percent, from 7.36 percent on Friday. The yield is at its lowest level since June. Shorter- maturity bill rates were mixed.
Rates at yesterday's regularly weekly bill auction dropped smartly, with a three-month bill going at an average rate of 5.64 percent, down eight basis points from last week's auction and the lowest since a rate of 5.56 percent on Dec. 27. The Treasury also sold six-month bills at an average rate of 5.80 percent, down 11 basis points from the previous auction and the lowest since Nov. 14, when the rate was 5.72 percent. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Thomas F. Carpenter, chief economist and managing director of ASB Capital Management, a Washington-based company managing $6 billion in bonds, said the market had a good tone yesterday. One of the reasons, he said, was a "a growing sense that the central banks of both Germany and Japan could lower their rates, thus strengthening the dollar and at the same time enhancing the attractiveness of dollar-denominated investment like Treasury securities."
Mr. Carpenter said the market's view of Federal Reserve policy had shifted sharply in recent months. A few months ago, "there was a large group of economists who thought the Fed would raise rates at the March 28 meeting, but an even larger consensus that the Fed would wait until May 23 to raise rates," he said. "Today, virtually no one believes the Fed will raise interest rates tomorrow," he added.
Indeed, there is a growing minority of traders and analysts who see the Fed's next increase happening sometime after the May meeting of the policy-making committee. The Fed committee is not scheduled to meet in April.
Susan M. Herring, an economist at Salomon Brothers, says policy makers most likely will not feel compelled to tighten further at today'smeeting, having increased rates just eight weeks ago. "If the Federal Reserve remains intent on constraining inflation, however, additional tightening likely will be required in the coming months," she said. "To be sure, the Fed's earlier efforts are beginning to bear fruit. Interest-sensitive spending by consumers has faltered, and their creditworthiness is eroding."
Today, the market will turn its attention to the Government's auction of $17.25 billion in new two-year notes. In when-issued trading late yesterday, the new two-year note was being offered at a price to yield 6.62 percent, while the five-year note, which will be auctioned tomorrow, was offered at a price to yield 6.89 percent. Both have shown there is good investor demand, particularly in the two-year note, whose yield has dropped more than 10 basis points since last Wednesday, when the Treasury announced the size of the auctions.
Following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new three- and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: Stocks Set New Marks Amid Questions on Durability of the Rally

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
Stocks hit record levels once again yesterday, even as analysts questioned whether the rally could last.
The Dow Jones industrial average rose 18.67 points, to 4,157.34, the eighth record in the last 12 sessions. But just five stocks accounted for all of the gains: Boeing, J. P. Morgan, American Express, International Business Machines and Caterpillar. The Dow was flat much of the day, surging in the last half-hour of trading. 
 "Today was a decidedly mediocre advance, a rush to blue chips," said Philip Roth, chief technical market analyst at Dean Witter Reynolds.
The broader Standard & Poor's 500 rose 2.23 points, to 503.20, also a record. The smaller-company Nasdaq composite index climbed 3.97 points, to 822.63, for another record.
The stock market seemed to take little notice of an announcement by John P. LaWare, a Federal Reserve governor, that he planned to retire April 30 and would not take part in today's meeting of the Fed's policy-setting committee. Mr. LaWare has been known as an advocate for raising interest rates to fight inflation.
The bond market, which is somewhat more sensitive to Fed developments, continued a morning rally after the National Association of Realtors reported a decline in home resales, another indication of a slowing low-inflation economy, which is favorable for bonds.
Most Fed watchers expect the central bank to do nothing about interest rates today.
Bond prices rose and the yield on the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond fell to 7.31 percent from 7.36 percent, the lowest level since August.
While the Dow advanced yesterday, some analysts continued to see signs of the market slowing, much like the economy. Yesterday's gain "has all the earmarks of an aged advance," Mr. Roth said.
For one thing, he said,, mutual funds have less than 8.5 percent of their net assets in cash or its equivalent. "There is little fuel, in the form of cash in reserve, to push this market up," he said.
The other sources of money for lifting stocks, Mr. Roth said, would be money locked up in long-term bonds and from foreign investors.
But bonds continue to offer attractive interest rates and therefore it is unlikely that much money can be coaxed away from them soon, Mr. Roth said. And foreign investors are unlikely to buy United States assets unless the dollar stabilizes.
The value of the dollar is important to stocks because a fall in the dollar can wipe away any profits enjoyed by foreign investors. The Dow has risen 8.42 percent this year, but fallen 1.69 percent in terms of German marks, and dropped 2.72 percent in terms of Japanese yen.
Yesterday, the dollar was up from historic lows against the Japanese yen, rising to 89.40 yen from 88.95 yen. But it fell to 1.4050 German marks, from 1.4172 marks on Friday.
Among the large gainers yesterday was Oxford Health Plans, an insurance company, which rose 12 5/8, to 109 1/2, after a Salomon Brothers analyst rated its stock a "buy." Security Dynamics Technologies, which makes security systems for computers, rose 10 1/8, to 37 1/2, after the company said first-quarter earnings would exceed that of the fourth quarter,
The UAL Corporation, parent of United Airlines, rose 7 1/8, to 102 7/8, after The Wall Street Journal reported that the airlines industry was showing signs of recovery.
Among the big decliners was C-COR Electronics Inc., an electronics manufacturer, which fell 3 1/4, to 19, after it said fiscal-third-quarter earnings would be lower than Wall Street expected. Shares of Presstek fell 3, to 69, after Barron's questioned whether increased competition might hurt the maker of imaging technology.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was below normal, with 296.3 million shares changing hands, about 10 percent below this year's daily average of 329 million shares.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index rose 1.22 points, to 271.72, with advancing stocks outnumbering declining stocks by 1,361 to 826. The American Stock Exchange market value index rose 0.29 point, to 461.19.
The most active stock, at 8.4 million shares, was Telefonos de Mexico, which rose 1 5/8, to 29 3/8, after the Mexican peso and financial market appeared to improve.

HEADLINE: Resales of Homes Decline to a 2-Year Low

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 27

 BODY:
Sales of previously owned homes in February dropped to the lowest level in two years, as consumer concerns over a slower economy offset a slip in mortgage rates, a real estate trade group reported today.
 "Despite rates starting to come down some, consumers are now focused on a grim outlook in job growth," John A. Tucillo, an economist with the National Association of Realtors, said. 
 The association said that sales of existing single-family homes dropped 5 percent, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 3.43 million, from 3.61 million in January and 3.84 million a year earlier.
It was the lowest number of sales since a similar 3.43 million rate in March 1993. Except for a 1.9 percent gain in December, sales have not advanced since March 1994.
The Government reported earlier that housing starts also fell in February, and many analysts expect the Government to report on Wednesday that new-home sales also deteriorated in February.
Analysts attributed the weakening market in large part to rising mortgage rates. Thirty-year fixed-rate mortgages averaged 8.77 percent in February, the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation said. Although that was down from 9.15 percent in January, it was above the 7.15 percent of a year earlier.
The monthly payment on a $100,000 mortgage at 7 percent is $665.31; the payment on the same loan at 9 percent is $804.63.
In addition to pushing up housing loans, seven short-term interest-rate increases engineered over the last 13 months by the Federal Reserve to fight inflation are leading to slower job growth in some areas.
As a result, the realtors' president, Edmund G. Woods Jr., said, "People simply don't feel good enough about job prospects or job security to buy a home."
The real estate group said that price changes had little effect on housing affordability in February. The median price of a house was $107,100, barely changed from $107,000 a year earlier.
Regionally, the Midwest had the largest loss in sales, down 7.7 percent, to an 840,000 annual rate. The median price was $86,900, up 2.1 percent from $85,100 a year earlier. Sales were off 4.3 percent, to a 1.32 million rate, in the South, where the median price was $92,500, down 2.4 percent from $94,800 in February 1994.
The Northeast posted a 3.6 percent drop in sales, to a 540,000 rate. The median price slipped four-tenths of 1 percent, to $137,100 from $137,700. In the West, sales fell 2.7 percent, to a 730,000 rate, but prices edged up six-tenths of 1 percent, to $144,100 from $143,300.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
The Limp Noodle

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

 BODY:
At least this much can be said about a new human rights initiative of the Clinton Administration: There are people in the Government who understood and argued that it was an embarrassing piece of hypocrisy, saying much, meaning nothing, fooling nobody.
They were overruled by higher authorities in the White House and National Economic Council, who actually still believe that the President can restore his reputation as a human rights leader with funny-house mirrors and without putting pressure on Communist China or its American business lobby. 
 Last year Mr. Clinton reneged on his written word to use higher tariffs on some Chinese goods as incentive to bring smidgens of human rights decency to Communist China.
So Thursday his Administration disclosed his new idea. U.S. business should institute voluntary codes of human rights principles for American corporations operating abroad.
This is supposed to satisfy Americans interested in human rights, get them and their money behind him in the Presidential campaign, and so avoid the price Presidents pay when they break their word.
Presumably Americans, Chinese Communists and tortured political prisoners in China and Tibet are all moronic enough not to notice a few realities that make the proposal just more salt in the wound.
1. It transfers entirely the political and moral responsibility for fighting for human rights in China from Washington to American business, which has already enthusiastically rejected any intention of annoying the Chinese Communists by harboring such thoughts.
2. To insure that Beijing is not irritated merely by being mentioned in context of a human rights proposal, the Administration does not mention any country. The plan is supposed to apply to the whole wide world. Un-codes for un-countries -- so diffuse that un-China need take no offense, or worry about its impact.
If catch-all declarations about human rights had any effect -- so many exist already -- China would be a huge civil liberties entertainment park. The point and purpose of human rights action is to apply specific pressure on specific tyrannies.
3. Even as the limp noodle it is, United States businesses complained that it might somehow annoy the dreaded China. So, in the lengthy code-contemplating process, the noodle was made ever limper.
4 to 100. The plan contains no provisions for economic pressure or penalties against governments and businesses that will continue saying what they have always said about human rights codes without any enforcement: Put it in your ear.
Instead, the Administration "encourages" businesses to adopt codes on fair employment practices, including "avoidance" of child and forced labor. Avoidance!
About political oppression in workplaces in Whereverland, the Administration says only that it should not be condoned, no. There are no unsettling details about how Chinese Communists openly operate within foreign-backed factories to carry out such political terrorism as harassing women workers into abortion.
Commenting on the noodle, Human Rights Watch/Asia said that given the range of abuse in countries where American companies do business, including Mexico, Saudi Arabia, Nigeria and of course China, the "codes" would do no more good than the "hortatory" language already found in personnel manuals. History records no dictatorship frightened by a company manual.
But in Congress a bill has been introduced that would demand reports from U.S. businesses in China on what they have done to deal with specific violations of human rights affecting their workers. The sponsors are Tom Lantos and Nancy Pelosi, California Democrats, and Christopher H. Smith of New Jersey and Gerald Solomon of New York, both Republicans.
Mr. Lantos, long a human rights fighter, said in a written statement that it was "unconscionable" for American business to say that to operate in China they had no choice but to accept gross violations of human rights on their own factory floors. Mr. Lantos said the Clinton plan was "weak and ineffectual."
Over the phone he was more pithy. In seven words he summed up what I have been writing in this column.
"It makes me sick to my stomach," he said.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Moves Lower vs. Mark But Strengthens Against the Yen

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar fell against the German mark yesterday, hurt by profit taking and fading hopes that the German central bank would lower interest rates.
But the dollar strengthened against the Japanese yen after Japan's Finance Minister indicated he might reduce interest rates in an effort to halt the yen's rise. 
 "Traders are watching closely to see if interest rates are going to move," said Paul Farrell, head currency trader at Chase Manhattan Bank in New York. "Much of the trading is based on speculation of what's to come."
In late New York trading, the dollar fell to 1.4050 marks, from 1.4172 marks on Friday. The dollar also fell to 1.1595 Swiss francs from 1.1740. The British pound edged up to $1.5975 from $1.5935.
But the dollar rose to 89.40 yen from 88.95 yen. The dollar moved higher against the yen after Finance Minister Masayoshi Takemura of Japan indicated that rates might be lowered. He did not directly call for lower rates, but he said a cut was possible, depending on the economic conditions in coming months. Lower rates would make yen-denominated investments less attractive.
"There is really a chance that the Japanese will move the rates," Mr. Farrell said.
Japan wants a weaker yen because the currency's rise has made Japanese products more expensive and less competitive abroad.
Also helping the dollar against the yen yesterday was heavy intervention by the Bank of Japan during overnight trading in Tokyo.
"The dollar really started to rally as the Japanese central bank intervened," said Richard Vullo, vice president of the New York branch of Hypo-Bank of Germany.
The Bank of Japan has been regularly buying dollars and selling yen on currency markets in an effort to hold down the value of the yen.
After climbing to a two-week high against the mark on Friday, the dollar fell back yesterday.
The dollar was weighed down as currency dealers became increasingly skeptical that the Bundesbank would lower interest rates when its policy makers meet on Thursday.
And most analysts and dealers expect the Federal Reserve to leave interest rates unchanged when its policy-making comittee meets today. Higher rates in the United States typically make dollar-denominated holdings more attractive.

HEADLINE: Mother Nature Didn't Devastate Fisheries

 BODY:
To the Editor:
You report March 22 that Gov. William F. Weld of Massachusetts will ask President Clinton to declare his state's commercial fisheries a natural disaster. While no one would dispute the magnitude of the crisis, the devastation of Georges Bank, off Cape Cod, is not attributable to the "uncontrollable forces of nature." 
 The demise of once-rich fish populations is the consequence of a management regime rife with conflicts of interest and of the failure of fishery managers to heed warnings on overfishing.
Populations of cod, haddock, flounder and other ground fish in New England waters have been declining since the early 1980's.
Despite overwhelming scientific evidence that excessive fishing pressure was undermining the long-term health of the Georges Bank fishery, the New England Fishery Management Council refused to impose conservation measures. As a result, a fishery that ought to contribute $350 million annually to the region's economy is today virtually shut down.
While we recognize the need to provide assistance to individuals and families who will suffer as a result of the mismanagement, this should not be accomplished under the guise of natural disaster relief. Other avenues are available.
For example, Congress last year provided more than $30 million to bail out the New England fishing industry, and the Commerce Department has embarked on a $2 million pilot program to buy back excess fishing vessels. In this time of fiscal austerity we need to focus on the biggest problem confronting New England's fisheries: an overcapitalized fleet.
 
MICHAEL SUTTON
Vice President, World Wildlife Fund
Washington, March 23, 1995

HEADLINE: 1994 Catastrophes Rock Property Insurers

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, March 27

 BODY:
A string of storms, floods and fires and the Los Angeles earthquake rocked property-casualty insurers last year and cut the industry's profit in half, a trade group said today.
The combined earnings for the insurers fell 47.7 percent, to $10.1 billion, last year from $19.3 billion in 1993, according to the Insurance Information Institute. 
 "Last year was the second-worst year on record for catastrophes," said Sean Mooney, senior vice president and economist at the institute, which released the figures.
The insurance companies paid out $15.7 billion, still less than the $23 billion in 1992 when Hurricane Andrew devastated Florida and parts of Louisiana in the costliest United States natural disaster on record.
The biggest single factor causing last year's decline was the earthquake on Jan. 17, 1994, near Los Angeles, now estimated to cost the industry $11.4 billion, he said.
The tropical storms Beryl, Alberto and Gordon accounted for another $235 million in losses, while above-normal levels of flooding, snow and wind damage pushed the overall losses even higher.
The figures were compiled by the Insurance Services Office and the National Association of Independent Insurers and are consolidated estimates for the entire industry based on the reports of insurers that account for 96 percent of the country's property and casualty insurance business.
The industry's pretax operating income of $10.7 billion in 1994 was 26.5 percent lower than 1993's $14.6 percent. The operating income includes a pretax underwriting loss of $22.4 billion and pretax net investment income of $33.0 billion.
