HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Injunction Hearing Set; Clubs Offer New Plan

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
In the most active day-night doubleheader baseball may see all year, a Federal judge yesterday scheduled a hearing for Friday on a request for an injunction that the players say would end their seven-and-a-half-month strike, and the owners last night presented the players union with a new proposal they believe should end the strike.
"We're very pleased the judge will have an opportunity to render a decision prior to the start of the season if she chooses to," said Daniel Silverman, director of the National Labor Relations Board's New York office, who filed the injunction request yesterday afternoon in United States District Court in Manhattan. 
 Judge Sonia Sotomayor scheduled a hearing for 10 A.M. Friday, giving the two sides time to submit briefs tomorrow and Thursday.
"I won't decide until Thursday if I'm going to have oral arguments or live testimony," she said. Sotomayor instructed the owners' lawyers to submit their papers by 5 P.M. tomorrow, "concentrating on whether the Sunday deadline is significant."
The judge referred to the scheduled start of the season Sunday night. The owners basically made that a deadline, too. Bud Selig, the acting commissioner, repeatedly declined to characterize the new proposal as nonnegotiable, but his answers to questions at a news conference left that impression.
"We have said to them," Selig said at a Manhattan hotel, "with the economics changing on a daily basis for both parties, if this offer isn't accepted by this weekend, it's hard for us to estimate what may happen after that. The economics are changing and getting more negative on a daily basis."
Union representatives did not appear at the news conference, and the only officials who could be reached, Donald Fehr and Gene Orza, declined to comment.
The proposal, however, did not seem to be one the union would leap to accept. It was an improvement over the proposal the owners made at the last bargaining session, March 4 in Scottsdale, Ariz., but it still contained elements the union is virtually certain to reject.
First on the union's rejection list would be the payroll tax -- 50 percent on the portion of payrolls above $44 million, beginning in 1996. The threshold was up from $40.7 million, but the tax rate remained the same, double the 25 percent the players proposed with a payroll threshold of $54 million.
Furthermore, the tax would be in effect for the last five years of a six-year agreement, whereas the union has proposed ending any payroll tax after three years.
The players would continue to see the tax as having the same effect as a salary cap. Eleven clubs had payrolls greater than $44 million in 1994, and the union believes a 50 percent tax would force clubs to reduce their payrolls to the threshold to avoid paying taxes.
The owners made more significant improvement on salary arbitration and free agency. They offered alternative plans -- the same arbitration and free-agency systems that existed under the expired agreement but with some modifications, or unrestricted free agency for players after four years (down from six) and the elimination of arbitration for other eligible players after 1996.
One of the changes under the first alternative would be a three-man arbitration panel for each case in place of the one-arbitrator system that has always been in effect.
When Selig was asked if the proposal was subject to negotiation, he said: "I don't want to get into that. We strained to make this offer. They know they have this offer. They have to accept it by Saturday or Sunday."
The Mets are scheduled to play the Florida Marlins in the opening game Sunday night, and they will do that with substitutes for the striking players barring a development either in court or at the bargaining table that would prompt the owners to delay the start of the season.
The players have said they will return to work if the N.L.R.B. gains an injunction forcing the clubs to restore salary arbitration and competitive bidding for free agents. The owners, who have said they do not want to play this season under the previous work rules, would then have to decide whether to lock out the returning players. They would need the votes of 21 of the 28 teams to do so.
There has also been talk among people on both sides that the players might offer to end their strike even if the the injunction request is denied.
"I doubt if that will be the subject of much discussion," Fehr, the players' labor leader, said of the latter possibility, alluding to meetings of the union's negotiating subcommittee tonight and the executive board tomorrow. "If the injunction doesn't issue, we'll worry about what to do after that."
When the two sides resumed bargaining last night after a 23-day hiatus, no one expected the new round of talks to lead to a dramatic 11th-hour settlement that would render court action unnecessary.
The owners' lawyers indicated no interest in having a ruling by Sotomayor before the start of the season, or any time, for that matter. Frank Casey, representing the clubs, raised points in a 30-minute hearing that appeared to be aimed at muddying the issues before the judge.
He said a grievance filed by the union last Friday contending that all unsigned roster players are free agents eliminates the need for the court to rule on the labor board's charge that the owners have eliminated salary arbitration. He also said the clubs did not understand the board's charge that they had eliminated bidding for free agents.
"We simply do not know what they are talking about; we need discovery," Casey told the judge, referring to information two sides in a court case seek before proceeding with the case.
Casey, in court, and Chuck O'Connor, outside court, emphasized the owners' view that an injunction would not affect the strike.
"Under Federal labor law, what happens in this proceeding has nothing to do with the strike," Casey said.
After the hearing, O'Connor, the chief management labor lawyer, echoed the argument. "The N.L.R.B. should decide the issues first, not the court," he said. "There is nothing this court will do to end the strike. If the union wants to end the strike at some point, it will do so. The court will not impact that directly -- maybe indirectly."
But Casey and O'Connor ignored the union's stated stance about ending the strike. They were trying to make the point that only a negotiated settlement will end the dispute.
But Silverman said the labor board thought it was necessary to eliminate the changes the owners made unilaterally last month on salary arbitration and free agency.
"If the players will go back to work under the terms that existed prior to these changes, the season can progress," he said. "We believe the elimination of these conditions will permit the parties to end the dispute themselves."
In three previous labor disputes, the owners have locked the players out. If the players offered to end the strike and the owners locked them out, it would be the first time the clubs would have taken that step while using replacement players. Court rulings have permitted the use of replacements during a lockout, but it is not clear whether there could be circumstances under which an employer would not be permitted to take that dual action.
Among those who have appeared before her during her two and a half years on the Federal bench, Sotomayor, at 40 years old the youngest of the 38 judges in the district, has a reputation for being thorough and deliberate. She is not likely to be rushed if she feels she needs more time to make the correct decision.
She did not display any particular reaction to having the celebrated baseball dispute land in her courtroom. "I know nothing about this," she said, "except what the common layperson reads in The New York Times."

SECTION: Section A; Page 12; Column 3; Foreign Desk 

LENGTH: 1436 words

HEADLINE: Nigeria Accused of a 2-Year War on Ethnic Group

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH 

DATELINE: AMINIGBOKO, Nigeria, March 24

 BODY:
Two years ago, when a handful of villages were destroyed and residents began fleeing a wave of killings in the oil-producing Niger River Delta country near this small town, the Government and refugees gave starkly different explanations of the violence.
For Nigeria's military authorities, the killings were an example of the kind of inter-ethnic hatred that has long afflicted the overcrowded farming and fishing lands of this region, and occasionally exploded in tribal violence. 
 For the Ogoni people, however, a mostly poor 500,000-member ethnic group that shares its lands near the outlet of the Niger River with the work crews and rigs of foreign oil companies, the violence was clearly the work of soldiers bent on silencing their protests over their tiny share of the revenues generated by some of the continent's richest petroleum fields.
Now, in the first independent accounting of the two-year rash of violence in Nigeria's oil-producing southeast, Human Rights Watch says it has collected the accounts of both victims and participants in attacks on the Ogoni.
In a report published this week that largely corroborates the victims' claims, Human Rights Watch, based in New York, says Nigeria's military has conducted what amounts to a two-year war against the Ogoni that has involved repeated attacks on Ogoni hamlets, shooting of unarmed villagers, gang rapes of women and burning of homes.
In one of the worst of the attacks, in May 1993, according to the report, a Nigerian soldier said his company had been told they were being sent to repel an incursion by troops from neighboring Cameroon.
"When we arrived, they told us to shoot everyone who crossed our path," a Nigerian soldier who took part in the attack on an Ogoni village told Human Rights Watch. "I followed my orders until I realized that the approaching civilians were Nigerians."
The accounts of army-led drives against the Ogoni come at a time of growing calls, in Nigeria and abroad, for sanctions to isolate the dictatorship of Gen. Sani Abacha.
At the heart of the campaign of violence, residents of the delta region say, is a conflict that has pitted the interests of the Ogoni against the Shell Petroleum Development Company of Nigeria. For years the Ogoni have been at the forefront of demands by many of Nigeria's ethnic minorities that the Government do more to protect their environment and share more revenue with oil-producing lands in the southeast.
The Ogoni, in addition to accusing Shell of destroying their lands, say the company has ordered army reprisals or provided support in the form of money and intelligence to the military in its campaign against them.
Shell officials at the company's sprawling operational headquarters for eastern Nigeria in Port Harcourt, the capital of Rivers state, deny both assertions, saying they have voluntarily withdrawn from Ogoni areas.
"I have made it quite clear that we are not prepared to spill one drop of blood, even for a million barrels," said Egbert Imomoh, a Shell general manager. "We are not in a hurry, and will return only when we are welcome."
At the same time, however, officials of Shell Nigeria say the company's conflict with the Ogoni is spreading to other ethnic groups in the region who harbor many of the same complaints against the company. Shell says that in several areas of the Niger Delta its equipment has been sabotaged and its workers have been taken hostage.
Nigeria's military Government has repeatedly denied any involvement in reported atrocities in Ogoniland, ascribing any killings to local conflicts and terrorism by the Ogoni themselves.
Last May, in what Nigerian and international human rights groups describe as a politically inspired frame-up, the authorities arrested Ken Saro-Wiwa, an Ogoni who is one of Nigeria's most distinguished writers, on charges he had killed Ogoni leaders.
Mr. Saro-Wiwa, who has been held in military jails since his arrest and deprived of visits for most of that time, is being tried for the murders before a military-run tribunal in Port Harcourt.
James Ron, a Human Rights Watch researcher who spent three weeks investigating military abuses, said that after the killings of four conservative Ogoni leaders for which Mr. Saro-Wiwa was accused, "soldiers moved in and just began shooting people in their villages, burning their homes and arresting young Ogoni men en masse."
"When the Government began its attacks, it looked at first as if they were looking for the killers, but they conducted themselves as if they were leading a punitive expedition," Mr. Ron said. "If some people were interrogated, many of the detainees were simply mistreated. Young Ogoni men were flogged twice a day, and there was an effort by the soldiers to extract as much money from the detainees as possible. People were told that unless their families came up with large amounts of money they would not be released."
Mr. Ron, who was allowed to enter Ogoniland for one day with a military escort, said he and another colleague had only been able to interview many of the victims in secret after they were brought out of their homeland by trusted messengers.
Since the start of the attacks on the Ogoni, access to their villages has been effectively closed to outsiders by Nigerian security forces. Throughout this region, the police and soldiers maintain heavily armed roadblocks where menacing men in uniform wave automatic weapons as they systematically stop vehicles for searches and interrogations.
Human Rights Watch was allowed to tour the region with military escorts, but during a three-week investigation was able to arrange clandestine meetings with several Ogoni victims, as well as with four soldiers who separately gave their accounts of army-led attacks on Ogoni villages.
Military officials and Shell managers warned of "trouble" should foreign reporters seek to enter Ogoni areas.
Nigeria has increasingly become the focus in recent weeks of international human rights critics. They have reproached General Abacha's 18-month-old Government for wave after wave of arrests of political opponents, union leaders and, most recently, one of the country's former Presidents, Olusegun Obansanjo. In 1979 General Obasanjo became the first Nigerian leader since independence in 1960 to voluntarily surrender power after democratic elections.
The Washington-based lobby TransAfrica warned Nigeria this month that it was preparing to mount a "global campaign of economic and political isolation" to force General Abacha to restore democratic rule.
Petroleum production accounts for 80 percent of Nigeria's Government revenue. Nigeria exports between 60 and 90 percent of its oil, a lightweight, or "sweet," crude considered ideal for gasoline production, to the United States. Shell, which generates about half of this country's output, is by far Nigeria's largest producer.
For years villagers have complained that Shell's operations have poisoned their once-rich croplands and devastated the wetlands of the Niger River Delta, which form the world's largest mangrove swamp. At the same time, residents of these oil-producing zones have complained that together, Shell and the Nigerian Government have done little to develop the area.
"The problems of the oil areas can be summed up as irreversible destruction of the environment," said William Odiete, a chemist at the University of Lagos who has studied oil-related ecological damage in Nigeria. "The inhabitants are becoming more and more impoverished because their farmlands, rivers and vegetation is dying."
Flying over Ogoni territory in a Shell company helicopter, the devastation of the last two years can be seen in the burned and badly tarred fields around damaged oil installations. Unlike other areas of the Delta, automobile traffic is almost nonexistent and many villages seem barren and abandoned.
Even more than environmental damage, though, what most strikes many visitors to this area is its lack of development. While gas flares that from a distance resemble giant bonfires burn off millions of cubic feet of natural gas, most of the rural world here remains without electricity or other modern amenities.
"In Nigeria, companies like Shell are struggling between greed and fear," said Claude Ake, director of the Center for Advanced Social Science, a Nigerian social and environmental research center in Port Harcourt. "Because of their behavior, moderates are losing ground in our communities, and Shell will have to think if one day they will be able to afford to protect each and every one of their workers."

A picture of Nigerian villagers yesterday, with an article about a wave of killings in an oil-producing region of Nigeria, carried an incorrect credit. The picture was by Robert Grossman for The New York Times, not by Reuters.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
2 California Health Care Providers Agree to a $1.8 Billion Merger

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
Two big California health maintenance organizations, Wellpoint Health Networks and Health Systems International have agreed to a $1.8 billion stock swap that would create the biggest stockholder-owned H.M.O. in the lucrative California market and the fifth largest in the country, people close to the deal said yesterday.
The companies said Wellpoint was "in discussions" to buy Health Systems. Health Systems stockholders would receive 1.09 shares of Wellpoint common stock for each Health Systems share and a proportionate share of $1.2 billion in cash. 
 The combined company would have a stock market valuation exceeding $4 billion.
On the New York Stock Exchange, Wellpoint jumped $5, to a 52-week high of $36, on the news yesterday, before falling back to $33.75, up $2.75; Health Systems' class A shares rose $1.375, to $32.375.
The combined company would have about one-sixth of California's 12 million H.M.O. enrollees, Kenneth S. Abramowitz, an analyst with Sanford C. Bernstein, said. With that kind of size, it could drive hard bargains with doctors and hospitals in the hotly competitive California market.
H.M.O.'s also need to grow to make up for lower profit margins. Employers, led by the California Public Employees Retirement Systems, or Calpers, have driven down the premiums the health maintenance organizations get by 3 percent per enrollee, Mr. Abramowitz said.
Wellpoint had put itself up for sale, but it rejected takeover offers from Blue Shield of California, a nonprofit mutual insurance company, and Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts & Company, the leveraged buyout firm, according to people with knowledge of the negotiations.
Wellpoint and Health Systems said their merger plan would "offer distinct advantages in terms of long-term stockholder value, strategic fit, operational synergies and the probability of closing" the deal.
Spokesmen for Blue Shield and Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts declined to comment, and it was not clear if either company would make a new offer or contest the Wellpoint-Health Systems deal.
"We are very very far along in the process" of completing the deal, Leonard D. Schaeffer, chairman and chief executive of Wellpoint, said in a telephone interview. The boards of Health Systems and Blue Cross of California, which owns 80 percent of Wellpoint, met yesterday to go over the fine print. All three companies are based in Woodland Hills, Calif.
Any deal is subject to approval by Gary S. Mendoza, the California commissioner of corporations. He said his department and its Wall Street adviser, Bear, Stearns & Company, were "reviewing the situation."
Wellpoint and Health Systems said they would turn over $1.2 billion in cash and about $2 billion in stock to a California philanthropic foundation -- a move that they hope will placate consumer groups and persuade Mr. Mendoza to approve the deal. Consumer advocates have objected to any privatization of the nonprofit California Blue Cross.
The foundation, which would be one of the nation's five or six biggest philanthropies, would get $1 billion of the $1.2 billion cash from Health Systems and $200 million more from the sale of certain businesses by Blue Cross to Health Systems. Wellpoint's public shareholders would divide $200 million.
Health Systems has 1.25 million California enrollees in health maintenance organizations and 300,000 in seven other states. It recently acquired M. D. Enterprises, a Connecticut H.M.O., for $100 million. Wellpoint has 700,000 H.M.O. enrollees and 2 million people in more loosely organized managed health plans called preferred provider organizations.
Kaiser Permanente, a nonprofit company, is the nation's largest H.M.O., with 4.7 million California enrollees and 6.7 million nationwide. The Wellpoint-Health Systems merger would be the second largest in California. Other big H.M.O.'s in the state include Pacificare with 1.1 million enrollees, FHP International with 900,000 and Foundation Health with 700,000, Mr. Abramowitz said.
Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts offered a fully financed buyout valued in the high $30's a share, people close to the deal said. They said Blue Shield offered $40 and then raised it to $45. But, they added, Blue Shield had not lined up financing. They also said that Blue Shield, as a mutual insurance company owned by policyholders, would need approval from Mr. Mendoza's Department of Corporations to convert to a for-profit company. That could subject Blue Shield to political challenges by consumers, similar to those faced by Blue Cross and Wellpoint.
In the Wellpoint-Health Systems deal, Mr. Schaeffer would be chief executive. Dr. Malik M. Hasan, chairman of Health Systems, would become chairman and a major owner, with about 6 percent of the shares.
Dr. Hasan merged a Colorado-based company, Qual-Med, with Healthnet last year to form Health Systems International. Like Wellpoint, Healthnet was originally part of Blue Cross of California; thus, it has products and computer systems that are compatible with those of Wellpoint.
The Healthnet unit is tangled in litigation with the state Department of Corporations and shareholders who have challenged the terms under which the company transferred ownership to a foundation, the California Wellness Foundation.
In the Wellpoint-Health Systems deal, the Wellness foundation would have nonvoting shares valued at about 25 percent of the new company. The foundation to be set up in the merger would have voting control with 53 percent of the votes and about 43 percent of the economic equity.
The negotiations generated a feast for investment bankers and lawyers. The Wellpoint and Blue Cross boards created committees of outside directors to weigh the offers. Morgan Stanley advised the Wellpoint committee. Goldman, Sachs advised the Blue Cross committee. Merrill Lynch advised the Wellpoint company, and Furman Selz advised Blue Cross.
Late last year, the Foundation Health Corporation and the FHP International Corporation made separate offers to buy Health Systems International, which rejected the offers as too low. Both offers were withdrawn last January.

CORRECTION-DATE: March 29, 1995, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day yesterday about a proposed acquisition of Health Systems International by Wellpoint Health Networks misstated a term of the deal. Under the plan being discussed, the owners of stock in Wellpoint, not Health Systems, would receive $1.2 billion in cash.

HEADLINE: Housing Voucher Test in Maryland Is Scuttled by a Political Firestorm

BYLINE: By KAREN DE WITT 

DATELINE: BALTIMORE

 BODY:
The theory was elegant, the outcome anything but.
The idea was that by scattering one or two poor families in large middle-income areas, they would disappear like salt crystals in a glass of water, quietly integrating themselves into communities where they would find more jobs, better schools and safer streets. 
 Instead, a national program that was intended to spend $234 million over two years to move 6,200 poor families to better neighborhoods unleashed a firestorm of protest here over race and class even before any of these families were moved.
As a result, financing for the second year of the program was canceled and Federal officials were left reeling from what could serve as a case study of how failing to lay the groundwork for a program that involves race and class can undercut an experiment.
"Primarily, we learned not to offer a program like this during an election year, when people are looking for a wedge issue and are not above frightening people with questions of race," said Henry G. Cisneros, Secretary of Housing and Urban Development. "Secondly, we need to put this into perspective for the community so we can get the correct information out there instead of having the picture painted incorrectly by someone else."
The opposition was started by citizens in several blue-collar towns east of Baltimore who feared that their neighborhoods would be inundated by poor blacks from the public housing projects in the city. The residents were quickly joined by local politicians. Eventually Senator Barbara A. Mikulski, a Maryland Democrat who is chairwoman of a subcommittee that oversees HUD, doomed the program. She said she had not responded to political pressure but she also said the program had become too controversial.
The program was begun in Boston, New York, Los Angeles, Chicago and Baltimore, but only in Baltimore were any families, only a handful of them, actually moved. They will be the last.
Margery Turner, deputy assistant secretary for research, evaluation and monitoring at HUD, said it was "a sad, discouraging experience." But she still expresses some hope for the idea. "But it's important not to be diverted by short-term flare-ups," she said. "I have a strong, perhaps naive faith in the ultimate power of information."
The program, called Moving to Opportunity, was one of a number of voucher programs. Currently, 1.3 million families receive a total of $7 billion in housing vouchers and the Clinton Administration hopes to substitute this overall concept for all Federal housing programs eventually. In general the Republicans in Congress, too, favor a voucher approach, but they balked at one that involved intrusions into wealthier neighborhoods.
Moving to Opportunity is one of the sharpest departures from decades of Federal housing policy, which had isolated the poor in pockets of poverty and violence.
The program sought to use vouchers to help poor families move to middle-class neighborhoods. It was modeled on a desegregation program in Chicago, the Gautreaux program, which was ordered by Federal courts two decades ago. During that time, the program has moved several thousand families, and has been credited with raising incomes and drastically lowering school dropout rates among the children.
Moving to Opportunity was not intended to be a desegregation program as such, but Mr. Cisneros said he expected it to combat what he called the "spatial segregation" that has resulted from the concentration of poor blacks, especially those in public housing, in center cities. Though 61 percent of the poor in the United States are white, they are not compressed in what Senator Mikulski calls "zip codes of pathology." Only 12 percent of poor whites live in poor neighborhoods in Maryland compared with 75 percent of the poor blacks.
Some sociologists and urban policy experts say that dispersing some poor to middle-income neighborhoods not only benefits the families, but also spreads the fiscal burden of social programs more equitably between city and suburbs. But others note that voucher programs like Moving to Opportunity cannot be the sole solution to the problems of poverty and that at some point the available housing runs out.
Officials are under some pressure to find alternatives to urban housing projects from a spate of lawsuits against HUD and local housing authorities complaining about racial segregation. The most recent one was filed here in Baltimore in January by the American Civil Liberties Union of Maryland. Court-ordered programs in Chicago, Dallas, Cincinnati, Memphis and Boston have spurred programs that have succeeded in helping low-income families escape ghettoes.
Some have started without much opposition. Karla Irvine, executive director of Housing Opportunity Made Equal, said the 12-year program, has successfully placed 900 families in the Cincinnati metropolitan area.
"People are so spread out that we've had very few racial problems," Ms. Irvine said. "We certainly haven't had any angry crowds like in Baltimore."
Here in Baltimore and in the other cities, the Government hoped to duplicate the achievements of the Gautreaux program and document the results by tracking participants for 10 years.
For Robin Dudley, the benefits of the program are already evident. Living in a modest town house in a Baltimore suburb, Mrs. Dudley, a single parent, said she had already seen changes in her three children, Gerrard, 9, Lachaye, 7, and Latish, 4. They liked school and were eager to explore this new green and quiet neighborhood after the crack-infested neighborhood that kept them indoors in Baltimore.
"I was scared to move, scared that there might be prejudice, but I was ready," said Mrs. Dudley, an unemployed high school graduate.
Admirers of the project say the circumstances here were unique. They note that there had been no incidents in Boston, New York, Chicago and Los Angeles. But critics contend that housing vouchers, by their very nature, are slated to generate controversy.
"It runs counter to the social structure of American neighborhoods, which are stratified along social, economic and education levels that people have worked to attain," said Henry Husock, a voucher opponent and director of case studies in public policy at Harvard University's John F. Kennedy School of Government.
Federal officials say they failed to educate the white working-class communities near Baltimore about the program. The opposition began in blue-collar areas like Essex and Dundalk east of Baltimore.
The residents there believed that the program meant that Baltimore would tear down its public housing projects. People envisioned Baltimore's 18,000 public housing tenants, who are predominantly black, being dumped in their overwhelmingly white backyards. In an area that had already suffered the loss of a steel plant, the idea of black welfare families being subsidized to move into their neighborhoods was untenable.
"When I found about what the program would do, I thought people should know," said Jerry Hersl, a resident of the area who led the crusade against Moving to Opportunity. "The program wasn't to give people in public housing a hand up to move up, but make them equal right off the bat."
The program also ended up, during an election year, being a frequent topic on talk radio.
During one such program, Louis L. De Pazzo, a State Delegate from Baltimore County and at the time a Democratic candidate for the County Council, warned that the county would be flooded with thousands of inner city people who would have "to be taught to take baths and not steal."
E. Farrell Maddox, former chairman of Baltimore County's House delegation, said that while housing vouchers had some value he still opposed them.
"I don't think we should get into social engineering and that's what they do," he said.
Oddly what was lost in all the shouting was the fact that places like Essex and Dundalk are too poor themselves to have been picked as destinations in the program.

HEADLINE: Dr. James Coleman, 68, Dies; Work Helped to Foster Busing

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. McFADDEN 

 BODY:
Dr. James S. Coleman, the renowned University of Chicago sociologist whose controversial studies laid much of the groundwork for and against the use of busing to desegregate schools in the 1960's and 1970's, and later helped shape American education policies by comparing public and private schools, died on Saturday at the University of Chicago Hospital. He was 68.
Dr. Coleman died of prostate cancer, said William Harms, a spokesman for the University of Chicago, where Dr. Coleman had spent much of his career as a teacher, researcher and author. Since 1973, he had been University Professor of Sociology. 
 In an academic and public life that spanned four decades, Dr. Coleman wrote 30 books and hundreds of articles, developed important theories on social change and collective action and widely influenced the course of education in America. But he was also drawn into many heated professional and political controversies.
In a 1966 report to Congress based on an extensive study of the performance of students in public schools, Dr. Coleman concluded that disadvantaged black children learned better in integrated classes, and his findings became a manual for political and court actions and were widely used to support busing to achieve racial balance in public schools.
In some cities, anger over busing and other desegregation efforts led to violent clashes among proponents and opponents and disputes over the policy continued for years, with much credit or blame ascribed to Dr. Coleman. Lost in the controversy were many caveats he had posed, including that black children could learn better only when mixed with a majority of middle-class children.
In 1975, Dr. Coleman apparently reversed himself, finding in another study that busing had been a failure because, he said, it had prompted a massive departure of whites from public schools, and he concluded that this "white flight" had undermined the aim of increasing contact among the races in public schools.
The second study generated enormous uproars among government policy makers, civil rights leaders, experts in education, sociologists and parents. Critics disputed his data, saying that white flight had begun long before busing, and attacked his motives, saying he had abandoned his commitment to desegregation.
But supporters contended that many of the critics were themselves motivated by racial politics, not educational facts, that Dr. Coleman had simply reported his scientific findings and that he had demonstrated courage in changing his mind when the new data dictated that he do so.
"Exceptional ability, fertile imagination and the courage to go against received opinion and to bear sometimes vicious attacks marked his distinguished career," Gary Becker, a professor of economics and sociology at the University of Chicago, said yesterday.
Dr. Becker recalled that some prominent members of the American Sociological Association moved to have Dr. Coleman expelled for his 1975 work. "It was not a fashionable or pleasant scientific finding," he said. "Fortunately for the integrity of the association, the move to expel him failed." Dr. Coleman was elected president of the association in 1991.
In 1981, another study by Dr. Coleman concluded that private and Catholic schools provided a better education than public schools. When another uproar ensued, he drew back from his findings, saying that the data were flawed and that his report did not emphasize the most significant issues. He said the new study did not prove any inherent superiority of private schools, only that they were more likely to stress things like discipline, homework and high standards. Dr. Coleman's study, "The High School and Beyond," is said to be the largest American survey ever conducted on the effects of schooling. It is a continuing research project that examines the lives and careers of 75,000 people who were sophomores and seniors in high schools in 1980.
In his 1990 book, "Foundations of Social Theory," which he considered his most important work in sociology, Dr. Coleman examined the formation and behavior of communities -- "natural" ones, like towns, based on geography and the decisions of ordinary people to live in them, versus "artificial" communities, like corporations or trade unions, in which people are grouped around objectives.
The book explores the use of rational choice by individuals -- long the basis for studies in economics -- as the basis for reviewing the way institutions are run and communities are structured, and shows how individual choices are affected by social norms, peer pressure and other factors.
James Samuel Coleman was born in Bedford, Ind., on May 12, 1926, and grew up there and in Louisville, Ky. He received a bachelor's degree in chemical engineering in 1949 from Purdue University, but switched fields in graduate school and earned a doctorate in sociology from Columbia University in 1955.
He joined the faculty of the University of Chicago in 1956 and taught there until 1959, when he joined the faculty of Johns Hopkins University to found its Department of Social Relations. He was a professor and department chairman at Johns Hopkins until 1973, when he returned to the University of Chicago.
Dr. Coleman is survived by his wife, Zdzislawa Walaszek; four sons, Thomas, of Greenwich, Conn., John, of Madison, Wis., Stephen, of Baltimore, Md., and Daniel, of Chicago, and a granddaughter.

CORRECTION-DATE: April 8, 1995, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An obituary on March 28 about Dr. James S. Coleman, a University of Chicago sociologist, referred imprecisely to his survivors. He had three sons from his first marriage, to Lucille Richey Coleman -- Thomas, of Greenwich, Conn.; John, of Madison, Wis., and Stephen, of Baltimore -- and a fourth son, Daniel, of Chicago, from his marriage to Zdzislawa Walaszek.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
MERGER TO CREATE NEW JAPAN BANK, WORLD'S LARGEST

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN 

DATELINE: TOKYO, March 28

 BODY:
Two of Japan's most powerful financial institutions, the Bank of Tokyo and the Mitsubishi Bank, announced today that they planned a merger creating the world's largest bank. With about $819 billion in assets, the new bank would be about a third larger than the Sumitomo Bank, now the biggest with $604 billion, and more than three times the size of Citicorp, the largest in the United States.
If the deal goes through, the new behemoth, tentatively called the Tokyo Mitsubishi Bank, would combine two of the strongest banks in Japan, where the banking industry has been shaken in recent years by the collapse of the country's stock and real estate markets. 
 Some experts see this deal, whose terms were not disclosed today, as the possible forerunner of other large combinations of Japanese banks, many of which are either looking for fresh capital or a strategic alliance to help them compete.
But analysts said the merger would have little impact on banking and trading markets outside of Japan. Japanese banks, after all, have been the largest in the world for a decade but they are by no means as influential as such big European and American banks as Credit Suisse, Deutsche Bank and J. P. Morgan & Company.
"These are markets with lots of players," said John D. Leonard, a bank stock analyst with Salomon Brothers in London. "One more or less will not make a difference."
The merger could have some impact, however, on consumers and small businesses in California, where Mitsubishi and the Bank of Tokyo each own medium-sized banks based in San Francisco. The Bank of Tokyo owns about 70 percent of the Union Bank, which has $17 billion in assets and 200 branches. Mitsubishi owns all of the Bank of California, which has about $7 billion in assets and 50 branches.
While these California-based banks could be combined as a result of the merger announced today -- strengthening Union's position as the fourth largest in the state -- such a deal would face regulatory scrutiny in the United States. For that reason, some analysts say, one of the California banks could wind up being sold.
In Japan, the proposed merger of Mitsubishi and the Bank of Tokyo radically alters the banking landscape. The union, which had been talked about for years, would link the Bank of Tokyo's strong global presence with Mitsubishi's extensive domestic branch network.
"It's like marrying the two most beautiful people in the world," said Alicia Ogawa, a bank stock analyst at Salomon Brothers Asia Ltd.
Mitsubishi, which was founded in 1880 and is now ranked sixth in the world, is one of several companies that make up the loosely knit Mitsubishi corporate group. The others in the group -- whose name loosely translated means three diamonds, the shape of its corporate symbol -- include Mitsubishi Heavy Industries and Mitsubishi Motors. The Mitsubishi Bank has the reputation of being among the most prestigious and conservative of Japanese banks and is sometimes referred to as the J. P. Morgan of Japan.
The Bank of Tokyo, the world's 24th-largest bank, has traditionally been the most international of Japan's banks. Its precursor, the Yokohama Specie Bank, which also dates back to 1880, was so close to the emperor that it served for a time as the main conduit between an isolated Japan and the outside world. But the Bank of Tokyo's distinctive international role has been reduced in recent years as Mitsubishi and others have increased their foreign presence.
After a trail of "no comments" and denials earlier in the day, the presidents of the two banks sat shoulder to shoulder tonight to declare their intention to merge.
"As bank presidents, we frequently met in various occasions, including drinking sessions, and we exchanged opinions and we found we had sympathies with one another," said Tsuneo Wakai, president of Mitsubishi.
Although they refused to reveal which side initiated the deal -- and declined to describe its financial terms -- they suggested that they both endorsed the idea from the start.
"I had come to think that a new image of the bank was necessary," said Tasuku Takagaki, president of the Bank of Tokyo. "I came to think that if we could work together, we could make an interesting bank."
The Ministry of Finance and the Fair Trade Commission both appeared to welcome the news, suggesting that they would not block the merger, which the two banks say they hope to complete before April 1996. The ministry has recently endorsed rescue mergers that would bring together a strong institution with one of the many weaker banks suffering from a heavy burden of sour debt.
Bad real estate loans have plagued the banking industry in Japan, since the collapse of the "bubble" economy in the early 90's depressed prices and made it impossible for many companies to repay loans for real estate projects. Analysts say that problem loans -- on which interest has not been paid for six months -- amount to nearly $150 billion.
Yet both the Bank of Tokyo and Mitsubishi have a relatively small amount of bad loans, as a percentage of their loans outstanding.
"Both are star performers in terms of writing off bad debt," said Rie Ota, an analyst at Barings Securities (Japan) Ltd.
So the proposed deal is startling in that it will create an exceptionally strong bank to compete against a group of large ailing banks, and it is likely to shake up the banking community in Japan even further.
"This is a superbank," said Yoshinobu Yamada, an analyst at Smith New Court Securities (Japan) Ltd. "It shows that the stronger can be stronger."
Immediately, speculation focused on other potential merger candidates. The share price of the Fuji Bank, a strong institution, rose 300 yen to close at 2,050 yen ($23.06) on the Tokyo Stock Exchange. Another candidate, the Sanwa Bank, saw its share price rise 260 yen, to 1,900 yen ($21.37). A third candidate is the Asahi Bank, the product of the most recent major bank merger, in 1991.
While the terms of the merger were not disclosed. Mr. Yamada suggested that the target share prices in the merger, which is expected to occur with an exchange of stock, would be 2,400 yen to 2,500 yen ($27 to $28.12) for Mitsubishi and 1,500 yen ($16.87) for the Bank of Tokyo. Trading in Mitsubishi and Bank of Tokyo stock was suspended after news about the merger leaked out, but their share prices rose earlier on rumors of the deal, with Mitsubishi rising 160 yen to close at 2,150 yen ($24.18), and the Bank of Tokyo rising 150 yen to end at 1,400 yen ($15.75).
Mr. Yamada said, however, that while the proposed deal might precipitate some mergers it would not begin an explosion of activity. That is partly because there are not that many banks to merge, he noted. Japan has only 150, compared with about 11,000 in the United States.
The Japanese banks, broadly, have been shrinking their overseas activities, returning capital to Japan, in part because of the rising yen and in part because the capital is needed to deal with loan losses at home. This merger is not in itself likely to reverse that trend, analysts said.
"As long as the dollar stays weak, it's hard to convince the Japanese to put money overseas," said Robin Monro-Davis, managing director of IBCA Ltd., a credit-rating agency in London. He said he doubted that the merged bank would use its bulk to buy large commercial or investment banks in the United States or Europe. "It will be a long time before the Japanese banks will make major acquisitions in the States," he said.
Although the Bank of Tokyo had indicated for years that it was looking for a match -- and a good yen deposit base to compensate for its weak presence in Japan -- many analysts said they believed that Mitsubishi stood to gain more from the merger.
Mitsubishi would gain the right, for example, to borrow money by issuing medium-term fixed-rate bonds, a privilege currently available only to certain banks, including the Bank of Tokyo.
"It's a great capital play for Mitsubishi," said Elizabeth L. Daniels, an analyst at Morgan Stanley Japan Ltd.
In a purchase essentially orchestrated by the Government, Mitsubishi recently acquired the ailing Nippon Trust Bank. That will hurt Mitsubishi's profits and drain capital.
Mergers in Japan do not necessarily result in big layoffs. Thus, it is unclear exactly where or whether substantial costs would be cut in Japan.
Japanese banks have not been shy, however, about laying off foreign staff members. A combined Tokyo Mitsubishi Bank would be able to achieve some savings by merging trading and banking operations in various world financial centers. And there is substantial overlap in their two California subsidiaries.
Yet a merger of the California banks is not assured because Union Bank is partly owned by the public. Federal regulators would need to be assured that the terms of any merger would be fair to Union's shareholders. As a result, the Japanese banks may delay any decision on the fate of the California subsidiaries and may instead choose to sell one of them, rather than attempt a combination, analysts said.
"For the folks back in Tokyo, what they decide to do in California is not a page 1 priority," said Thomas F. Theurkauf, a bank stock analyst with Keefe, Bruyette & Woods in New York. "The two banks represent 3 percent of the assets of the new bank."
The biggest issue for Mitsubishi and the Bank of Tokyo will be melding two venerable but different cultures as well as two well-entrenched bureaucracies, analysts said.
"On paper, it looks like a great team-up," said J. Brian Waterhouse at James Capel Pacific Ltd. "But I've strong doubts in terms of how it will work. Each has a strong corporate culture and in any merger of two very large organizations, it never goes as smoothly as planned."

HEADLINE: Major Ethnic Changes Under Way

BYLINE: By DAVID FIRESTONE 

 BODY:
New York City's white population, which made up more than 60 percent of the residents in the city in 1970, will decline to 35 percent by the end of the century, predicts a new forecast by the City Planning Department.
By contrast, the Hispanic population is projected to grow substantially by the year 2000, the study says, surpassing the black population for the first time.
The study, based on projections of birthrates and migration from the 1980's, provides a rough sketch of what the racial mixture is most likely to be. 
 The real numbers may be different if trends in the 1990's vary from those of the 80's, but planners said it was clear that Hispanic people, principally Dominicans and Puerto Ricans, and nonwhite ethnic groups would have a much larger role in politics and economics.
"The city is headed in the direction of more diversity," the director of the population division, Joseph Salvo, said. "We're going to see much more of a balance of the major groups."
The study predicts that the overall population will increase slightly in the 90's, to 7.48 million from 7.32 million. The city is projected to lose 559,000 non-Hispanic white people, but expects to gain 391,000 people with Hispanic backgrounds, 101,000 blacks and 233,000 of other ethnicities, mostly Asian.
One surprising finding, Mr. Salvo said, was diversity in patterns among the major groups. Each of the four has a distinctly different mixture of births, deaths and comings and goings.
Hispanic people, for example, have high birthrates and immigration rates, the study showed. More than two-thirds of the Hispanic increase is forecast to come from births to existing residents, with the remainder a result of immigration. By contrast, more whites are dying than are being born, and more are leaving the city than moving in. Black residents have a moderately high birthrate, but more blacks leave the city than move in.
Asians have a low birthrate, but a very high immigration rate, even higher than the Hispanic rate.
Ms. Salvo said the differences were primarily a result of age disparities. Although whites' median age is 40, the median age of Hispanic residents is 29, meaning a greater proportion is of childbearing age.
The newest Hispanic immigrants tend to be even younger and closer to childbearing age, Mr. Salvo said, noting that the median age of immigrants from the Dominican Republic is about 21.
The median age of Asian immigrants, half of whom are Chinese, is about 27, which explains their relatively lower fertility, Mr. Salvo said. "Six years is a big difference in fertility terms," he added.
The increase in the Hispanic population parallels a national trend in which Hispanic residents now outnumber blacks in Los Angeles, Houston, Phoenix and San Diego. Most Hispanic people in those cities are of Mexican or other Latin American origins. In New York most trace their roots from Puerto Rico or the Dominican Republic.
For the first time Hispanic residents will be a majority in one borough, representing 52.3 percent of the Bronx by the year 2000, the study projects. In Queens the white population will drop, from 48 percent in 1990 to 34 percent in 2000, according to the projections, and all other populations will increase.
Borough President Fernando Ferrer of the Bronx said he believed that his borough was already more than half Hispanic, a trend that he added was long in coming.
"I don't think this will mean anything dramatic for the borough," Mr. Ferrer said. "We're about there now, and we have a Puerto Rican Borough President, a black District Attorney and a white Jewish Surrogate Judge elected countywide. The faces may change. The names may change. But the nature of building support for a consensus government will go on."
Mr. Ferrer noted that those groups did not necessarily act or see themselves as part of a monolithic Hispanic bloc, any more than Jews and Italians behaved in similar ways because they were listed in the category of whites.
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani was questioned about the study at a news briefing yesterday, because one of the seven or eight closest advisers in his inner circle is Hispanic and the others are white. Asked whether his administration reflected the growing diversity of the city, he said such questions promoted division.
"My government's representative," Mr. Giuliani said, "made up of people of merit and quality who work very, very hard. I think I've moved the city to a deeper understanding of how people interact with each other, not based on an excessive concern over differences, but looking for things to unite us."

Here are the 1970 and 1990 demographics and a forecast of the city's population in the year 2000. (Source: New York City Planning Commission)
Map/Diagram: "Major Ethnic Changes Under Way"

HEADLINE: Council Set to Fight Mayor On Service Cuts in Budget

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

 BODY:
Drawing battle lines over Rudolph W. Giuliani's proposed New York City budget, officials of the City Council said yesterday that they would scale back some or all of the Mayor's $185 million in proposed tax cuts if the city failed to receive enough state aid and could not find ways to pay for the cuts without drastically reducing services.
And if the city can afford any tax reductions, the Council officials said they would propose alternatives to the Mayor's plan that would provide a greater benefit for middle- and working-class New Yorkers. 
 Specifically, the officials said yesterday, the Council would reject the Mayor's proposed elimination of sales tax on clothing under $100 and instead propose a package of tax cuts for homeowners, first-time home buyers and married couples, as well as tax credits for college students and working families.
The tax issue is included in a report, to be released today, detailing the Council's concerns and objections to the Mayor's financial blueprint for the coming fiscal year. It reflected the opening barrage in what could become a bitter fight over the Mayor's proposed $30.5 billion budget, which includes a $1.3 billion spending cut, the deepest reduction since the Great Depression.
In the summary of its report, scheduled to be released today in full, the Council also takes the Mayor to task for calling on Gov. George E. Pataki to expand his proposed reductions in Medicaid and welfare, saving the city a total of $1.2 billion.
In recent weeks, as the Governor and State Legislature have haggled over the state's budget, the Council has lobbied in Albany against many of the Mayor's and Governor's cuts in those programs, saying they will cost the city in the long run.
"The Council believes that the financial plan responds to the current political climate and economic circumstances, but in many areas fails to acknowledge the long-term social and economic consequences of these budget choices," the report said.
The Council's report also staked out its opposition to the Mayor's proposed cuts in education, sanitation, cultural programs and youth services. And it recommended abolishing the Human Resources Administration and dividing the Department of Social Services into three new agencies dealing separately with children, adults, and benefits programs for Medicaid and welfare.
The Mayor's office declined to comment on the Council's proposals until officials could review it. "We look forward to studying the Council budget plan," said a spokeswoman, Karen A. Crowe.
Although the summary report made clear that the Council would oppose the Mayor on several fronts, it did not address the additional cuts the city could be forced to make if it fails to receive all of the savings from the state that the Mayor anticipated in the budget plan he unveiled in February.
A Council official, speaking on condition of anonymity, said that the uncertainty in Albany raised questions about the city's ability to afford the tax cuts the Mayor proposed, which total $185 million in the next fiscal year and grow to $539 million in the following year.
Besides objecting to eliminating the clothing sales tax, whose loss would cost the city $98 million in revenues in the next fiscal year, the Council also opposes the elimination of the 12.5 percent surcharge on personal income tax that Mr. Giuliani proposed beginning in fiscal year 1997. The costs of the two tax cuts, the Council said, would total $370 million over the next two years.
In its report, the Council's staff argues that the Mayor's tax cuts over the next four years offered more savings for people who make more than $80,000, while the Council's plan would give greater relief to people who make less than that. The Council also argued that tax relief for home buyers would stimulate economic growth. The Council will not oppose the Mayor's $70 million reduction in property taxes on co-ops and condos.
The Council also clashed with the administration yesterday over the Mayor's plan to sell three of New York City's 11 municipal hospitals.
In more than four hours of often combative testimony, Maria K. Mitchell, the Mayor's special adviser on health policy and the newly appointed chairwoman of the Health and Hospitals Corporation, rejected charges that the Mayor's office had developed its plan without doing adequate research on its impact and without consulting the Council.
"I want to know what is the mind-set of the administration," said Julia Harrison, a Democrat from Queens. "Are we serving an autocratic ruler?"
Una Clarke, a Democrat from Brooklyn, said the administration's plan would dismantle the city's health care system for the poor. The Mayor, she said, was trying "to make it a city of the middle class and not the poor."
Ms. Mitchell countered by saying that the city needed to restructure its health care system. "I can't understand why anybody wants to keep the status quo," she said, "when we have health indicators that show us that we're not doing a good job."

HEADLINE: A Changing California Emerges From Recession

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, March 28

 BODY:
New evidence shows that three years after the rest of the country, California has finally pulled out of its brutal recession, with job growth and incomes rising swiftly. There are even signs that the outflow of residents from the state has slowed sharply and may reverse in coming years.
But California's economy has changed considerably and has been humbled by the extraordinary growth of the late 1980's. 
 A business forecasting group at the University of California at Los Angeles has released a much anticipated survey confirming indications that the state, which accounts for nearly 13 percent of the national economy, is gaining momentum in spite of problems like the peso crisis in Mexico and the aftereffects of the Northridge earthquake.
Perhaps the most impressive aspects of the U.C.L.A. forecast were its projections of growth in personal income and employment. The report said that in both categories California would catch up with and surpass the national average in several years. That would be the first time since 1990 that California's economy, long used to setting the pace for the United States, exceeded the rest of the country.
"In 1994 we were sort of flying blind," said Larry J. Kimbell, head of the U.C.L.A. Business Forecasting Project. "The numbers weren't all that clear, and we had to make a number of revisions in our forecasts, but now we're much surer. The economy is recovering."
But it will clearly be a different economy from the boisterous one that tumbled into the recession five years ago. Housing construction remains weak and housing prices, which had soared to extravagant levels, are still dropping. The biggest change is a shift away from the aerospace industry, which once formed the backbone of the state's economy, toward service industries, particularly entertainment.
Mr. Kimbell said that for the first time, the number of jobs in the entertainment industries, including movies, music and television, had surpassed those in the aerospace industry, with much of that shift focused in Southern California and Los Angeles.
"We're going to be much more of a media town," he said. "The number of jobs in that area before was never up to the image. But the jobs are here now and we're going to see a lot more growth."
The cutbacks at aerospace companies, a product of the end of the cold war and a slowdown in military spending, has eliminated 210,000 high-paying jobs in California that are never expected to be replaced.
By contrast, the motion picture industry alone added 23,000 jobs last year alone, a 17 percent spurt. That pace is expected to continue. The service sector in general will add 470,000 jobs from the beginning of this year through 1997, according to the U.C.L.A. report. Over all, the state will add 880,000 jobs through 1997.
"The growth of service industry jobs remains the driving force in the California expansion," the forecast said.
The renewed growth in California, although vulnerable to an economic slowdown at home or falling demand in its key export markets, in Japan and Mexico, has now generated another positive change: the flight of people from California is reversing.
David Hensley, an economist at Salomon Brothers in New York, and a former forecaster at U.C.L.A., said the stream of Americans who had been migrating out of California has slowed sharply. Many settled in other western states, whose economies were healthier.
Traditionally, California has received a large influx of Americans from other states. That movement reached a recent peak in the fiscal year that ended in June 1989, when there was a net inflow of 232,000 people, state figures show. (These figures exclude legal and illegal immigrants and include estimates based on changes in the number of drivers' licenses issued.)
The recession changed that with a net outflow of 189,000 in the fiscal year that ended last June. Preliminary figures indicate that the outflow will slow to 80,000 or less in the current fiscal year and could turn positive next year.
"California has come into 1995 with a reasonable head of steam," Mr. Hensley said. "There is lots of pent-up demand and we're seeing the benefits of that. It is pulling people back."
He added that the shift in migration will benefit the California economy, spurring spending on everything from autos to housing.
In total, California lost 520,000 jobs from the beginning of the downturn, in July 1990, to the bottom, in October 1993.
The current number of jobs in the aerospace industry, 170,000, is expected to drop further, to 153,000, by the end of 1997. Employment in high-tech industries is projected to remain flat.
The string of disasters that have afflicted the state -- the Los Angeles riots and earthquake, heavy flooding this year, the Orange County bankruptcy and the peso crisis in Mexico -- have exerted some negative pull on the economy, the report said, but it did not quantify the problems.
California has now won back 330,000 of the lost jobs since the slow recovery began last year, but it will not equal the previous peak employment level until next year.
California's nonfarm employment grew a modest 1.1 percent in 1994, but that was the first time in two years that the state posted any job growth. The growth rate in job creation will double this year, according to the U.C.L.A. report.
Still, California's economy faces a number of challenges, Mr. Kimbell said.
If higher American interest rates squelch the expansion, California will likely follow suit. Another wild card is Mexico, California's third-largest export market, after Japan and Canada. The plummeting value of the peso and a government austerity program there have slashed the inflow of California-made goods and agricultural products. A recent report by the Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco noted steep declines in retail sales in border districts.
But the U.C.L.A. group foresees the Mexican economy recovering by 1997, helping lift California's prospects that year.
The weak American dollar will help the state's exports to Japan, Mr. Kimbell said, but not substantially until 1997. And even then it is not clear that the troubled Japanese economy will still be expanding.

HEADLINE: Students Turn to Internet for Nationwide Protest Planning

BYLINE: By DAVID M. HERSZENHORN 

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass., March 28

 BODY:
Gone are the phone chains that mobilized antiwar efforts in the 1960's. Campus activism has launched into cyberspace.
Communicating by electronic mail from dormitories, libraries and campus centers across the country, college students have been sending one another a blizzard of messages during the last several weeks, discussing political platforms, possible protest dates and ideas for slogans and petitions. 
 The result: a computer-organized day of demonstrations against the Contract With America, the campaign manifesto from which House Republicans built their legislative agenda.
The planning for the demonstrations, which are set for Wednesday at more than 100 colleges and universities and in communities adjoining them, was spearheaded by a 32-year-old former campus protester and is thought to be the first instance in which campus organizers have taken to the Internet to map a nationwide campaign.
Their unusual origin notwithstanding, the protests are expected to be of the usual sort: a march in Ann Arbor, Mich.; a sit-in in Duluth, Minn.; a fast in Ithaca, N.Y.; leafleting in Cedar Rapids, Iowa, and rallies in New York City, including a mock funeral procession through Battery Park City that was organized by students at Columbia University.
Even on the biggest campuses, organizers are expecting only several hundred participants. But the speed and ease with which the protests were planned, and the broad coalitions of student groups that were reached, are evidence, organizers say, of how fundamentally computer networking has changed the way students communicate.
The Internet, they note, not only links students on one coast with those on the other but also joins collegians of the very same campus who might not have banded together had it not been for common causes discovered through computer interaction.
"Electronic mail is a means to really have a participatory democracy," said Elaine R. Holeton, a senior at the University of Virginia. "Everyone can have their voices heard."
"Electronic mail," said Jeremy Smith, "has totally changed the way students do political activism."
Mr. Smith, 24, a former student protester at the University of Florida, now works at the University Conversion Project/Center for Campus Organizing, which coordinated Internet planning for Wednesday's demonstrations.
The University Conversion Project, run out of the basement of an old stone Baptist church here in Cambridge, was created in 1991 by Richard Cowan, a 1987 graduate of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, to help students protest the Persian Gulf war.
At M.I.T. in the early 1980's, Mr. Cowan protested apartheid in South Africa, tuition increases and President Ronald Reagan's proposal for the "Star Wars" anti-missile defense system. Now 32, he works as a computer consultant at Lesley College here and runs the nonprofit University Conversion Project in his spare time with contributions from liberal foundations and individual donors; its budget last year was $42,000.
Not that campus-based computer interaction is the sole province of the student left. Conservative student groups are also active on the Internet -- there is a particularly active Republican electronic discussion group based at Temple University, for instance -- and young Republicans at Middlebury College in Vermont and Presbyterian College in Clinton, S.C., were not too shy to argue over the network with the organizers of Wednesday's protests.
Plans for those protests began in early February with a discussion in the Conversion Project's cluttered one-room office about a national day of action against the Contract With America, an idea first proposed by a member of the Democratic Socialists of America.
The idea was circulated by computer to several E-mail discussion groups. One, in Berkeley, Calif., had been used by opponents of Proposition 187, the voter-approved California ballot initiative aimed at denying social services to illegal aliens. Another, called Actnow, was based at Brown University in Providence, R.I., and had a widespread undergraduate audience.
The computer conversations quickly moved to the University Conversion Project's own discussion group, which is based at the University of Missouri and, Mr. Cowan says, currently has about 700 subscribers.
"It started out as just a few people talking about the Contract With America," said one subscriber, Camille Leganza, a freshman at Coe College in Cedar Rapids, Iowa. Miss Leganza said she was soon getting 30 messages a day about plans for campus demonstrations.
"It has opened up this whole world for me," she said. "Instead of this small liberal arts school that I am at, I am connected to the whole country."
A draft of a Call to Action was carried in February to the Conversion Project's annual retreat, which attracts campus organizers and members of organizations like the New York Public Interest Research Group and Amnesty International. The retreat took place at the offices of the Fellowship of Reconciliation, an interfaith peace organization in Nyack, N.Y., and among those attending was Mr. Cowan, who distributed the draft electronically on Feb. 23.
In early March, the Conversion Project circulated a packet of advisory materials offering suggestions to campus groups about how to mobilize support and plan protest events.
From that point on, planning for Wednesday's demonstrations became something of a ride on the information interstate, with campus organizers across the country pitching ideas back and forth.
At Wellesley, for instance, students designed an "Unpetition of the Contract With America" that, among other things, states opposition to the House Republican proposals on social services programs, education and the environment. The Unpetition was distributed by computer and will be passed around on paper at Wednesday's demonstrations nationwide.
Protest planning by Internet does have its drawbacks, though.
First, those students whose schools are still not connected to the Internet, especially community colleges, may never learn of a protest organized solely on computer. That, said Nicole Newtown, the Conversion Project's outreach coordinator, is the danger of relying too heavily on electronics.
Another concern is that electronic communication, though fast and effective, is somewhat sterile. "There is no substitute for the face-to-face interactions that build political commitments," said Mark Naison, a onetime leader of Students for a Democratic Society who is now a professor of history at Fordham University and teaches a course on 1960's activism.
"Politicization is a very complex process," he said. "It's not very easy for a student to make the transition from preoccupation with their grades, their careers, their financial problems, their social life, to a passionate political commitment that consumes everything. It's a life experience of new forms of group solidarity. I am not sure there is any shortcut for that."

HEADLINE: Philadelphia Transit Workers Strike After Rejecting Pay Offer

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, March 28

 BODY:
A strike by transit workers today brought the city's subways, buses and trolleys to a halt, forcing 350,000 people to find new ways to travel around the nation's fifth-largest city.
The strike against the Southeastern Pennsylvania Transportation Authority began at 12:01 A.M., after 5,200 members of Local 234 of the Transport Workers Union rejected a 7 percent raise over three years. 
 No talks were held today and none were scheduled. Average pay under the old contract, which expired on March 15, was $16.10 an hour.
Suburban operations -- buses, trolleys and commuter trains into the city -- are covered by contracts with different unions and were not directly affected by the walkout.
Nevertheless, the walkout crippled more than 80 percent of the region's public transportation, causing a big increase in traffic on local highways and city streets. By 8 A.M., many parking lots in the city's business district were filled.
The transportation authority tried to ease the burden by running 12 extra trains on regional rail lines from the suburbs. The number of riders on those lines increased to 47,000, up from the daily average of 31,000, said Michael Burns, the assistant general manager for the railroad division.
But those trains were of no use to most of the people who live within the 136-square-mile city's limits.
For example, 30,000 of the city's 207,000 public school students who rely on public transportation had no ride to school, said a spokesman for the school district, Paul Hansen.
Mr. Hansen said that attendance was down about 6 percent overall, most sharply in high schools, which saw attendance down 21 percent.
Tempers sometimes flared when frustrated commuters encountered striking workers. "You're keeping me from my job," a woman, Emma James, shouted at pickets outside the transportation authority's offices downtown.
A bus driver, Lee King, said: "This is nothing personal against the public. We're sorry for the inconvenience, but they have to understand that this is our livelihood."
Charles Brown, a bus driver for 25 years, said most drivers could not afford to retire until age 65. "Do you really want a bus driver who is 65?" he asked.
The workers walked out today after rejecting a 2 percent raise in each of the first two years of a new contract and a 3 percent increase in the third year. The union is seeking 3 percent each year, as well as greater pension benefits. The transportation authority said the difference between its offer and the union proposal was $37 million.
The president of Local 234, Harry Lombardo, said he had met for less than a minute with a state mediator. The union is planning a rally for 11 A.M. on Wednesday.
A transit authority spokesman, Stephan Rosenfeld, said this afternoon: "There is nothing new to report. Period. Zip. Nothing."
A cab driver, Siamak Padar, predicted that the strike would increase both his fares and anxiety. "It will get crazy," he said. "The traffic will get bad and people will be angry."
The Chamber of Commerce said Philadelphia's last transit strike, a four-day walkout in 1986, caused the local economy to lose about $2.5 million a day.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Philadelphia transit workers went on strike yesterday after rejecting the city's latest contract offer. The walkout halted public transportation for 350,000 people in the nation's fifth-largest city. Outside a bus terminal in northwestern Philadelphia, Walt Toliver, center, handed leaflets to fellow striking workers. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Stocks End Day Mixed, Deflated Somewhat by Bond Prices

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
The stock market closed mixed yesterday, with the Dow Jones industrial average falling, the Standard & Poor's 500 index edging again into record territory and the Nasdaq composite index, which is rich in technology stocks, surging to another high.
The negative factors included the dollar again falling near post-World War II lows against the mark and the yen, and bond prices dropping and long-term interest rates rising. Moreover, some analysts say stock prices may be too high and thus headed for a fall. 
 "The market had every excuse to go way down," said Thomas Gallagher, head of equity trading at Oppenheimer & Company.
Nonetheless, the Dow declined only 5.53 points, to 4,151.81, while the broader S.& P. 500 rose 0.70 point, to a record of 503.90. The smaller-company Nasdaq index rose 3.51 points, to 826.14.
"It was an incredibly good performance, considering," said Edward Laux, senior managing director of equity trading at the Chicago Corporation.
The Federal Reserve, as expected, did not raise interest rates yesterday, which deepened an overnight plunge in the value of the dollar. Currency traders had already bid down the dollar and accelerated their sales after the Fed announced after 2 P.M. that its policy-making meeting had ended.
The dollar's weakness may be helping the stocks of some American companies with multinational interests, as they receive part of their revenue abroad in local currency. When a multinational company is paid in yen or marks, for instance, it receives more dollars than it would otherwise.
"If you see a stronger dollar, you're going to see people easing out of the Coca-Colas, the Gillettes and the Mercks," Mr. Laux said.
Bond prices fell yesterday after two unexpected private-sector reports seemed to indicate the possibility of widening inflation as consumers continued to spend. The yield on the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond rose to 7.39 percent from 7.31 percent on Monday.
The Conference Board's consumer index for March rose to 101 from 99.4 in February, instead of an expected decline. A weekly survey by Johnson Redbook Service projected 1.3 percent higher retail sales for March from February. Analysts had expected a decline.
As bond yields remain relatively high, these fixed-income investments have coaxed money away from stocks. But despite investors' shifting money into bonds in recent months, stock prices continue to rise.
Some analysts note that the price-earnings ratio for the S.& P. 500, a key measure for valuing the stock market as a whole, has steadily risen. Last month, the stocks of the S.& P. 500 sold for $15.84 for each $1 of profit, up from $15.05 in December, the low point for 1994.
High-technology stocks may be especially vulnerable. Top stocks on the Nasdaq composite index yesterday included Intel, the computer chip maker; Microsoft, the giant software company; MCI Communications, the long-distance phone carrier, and Cisco Systems, the maker of computer networks.
Among the biggest gainers yesterday was the Limited, a specialty retailer. It rose 2 3/8, to 22, after it announced plans to split into three companies and sell stock to the public in two of them, a lingerie and toiletries operation that includes Victoria's Secret, and its flagship Limited stores.
The Healthcare Compare Corporation, a hospital-management company, rose 6 7/8, to 38, after a survey indicated that Wall Street analysts expected higher first-quarter profit. Micron Technology, the semiconductor manufacturer, rose 4 1/2, to 84 3/4, after the company doubled its dividend and announced plans for a 2-for-1 stock split.
Among the biggest decliners were Exide, a maker of lead acid batteries, and Tandy Brands Accessories, which makes leather accessories. Exide fell 6 5/8, to 36 1/8, after it announced that a warm winter meant that fewer car batteries had failed, hurting its sales. Tandy Brands fell 4, to 7 1/8, after it said it would close its television-shopping operation.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was close to normal with 320.4 million shares changing hands, about 3 percent below this year's daily average of 329 million shares.
