HEADLINE: Cuts Must Go to Deficit, Senate Says

BYLINE: By JERRY GRAY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The Senate today unanimously approved an amendment that would require $13.5 billion in cuts from spending in the current fiscal year be applied to reducing the Federal budget, not reducing taxes. The move was intended to blunt the opposition of Democrats to the spending-cut plan.
As the Senate began debate today on the midyear budget cuts, known as rescissions, Democrats made it clear that they would oppose the legislation unless they were given assurances that the Republicans would not use the savings to help pay for their proposed tax cuts. 
 "I will not indirectly cast my vote for tax breaks for the wealthy by voting for painful cuts that may be used to finance subsidies for the rich," said Senator Robert C. Byrd of West Virginia, the ranking Democrat on the Appropriations Committee and the sponsor of the amendment that directed the savings to deficit reduction.
Mr. Byrd's amendment was approved 99 to 0.
With barely two weeks to go before the start of a scheduled break, the first major recess since the 104th Congress began on Jan. 4, Republicans and Democrats in committee meetings and on the floor of the Senate today debated budget cuts, tax cuts and the future of a 64-year-old Federal labor law whose repeal Democrats warned would lead to wage cuts for construction workers.
Even as the debate on the rescissions was beginning in the Senate, the Republican-controlled Committee on Labor and Human Resources was voting along strict party lines to repeal the Davis-Bacon Act, the Depression-era law that guarantees that workers on Federal construction jobs will be paid the local prevailing wage. Its critics say the act is outmoded and has raised Federal construction costs by 5 percent to 25 percent.
But vowing that the legislation "will never see the light of day," Democrats said they would filibuster the bill when it reached the Senate floor for debate.
On the House side of the Capitol, an even bigger partisan battle was brewing.
In what amounted to a preview of the debate on tax cuts scheduled for next week, the sharp divisions between Democrats and Republicans and the deep intraparty splits took shape in testimony before the Rules Committee. Republicans argued that their tax proposals would lead to a stronger economy with more jobs, while Democrats countered that the Republicans were pandering to the rich.
Democrats tried to use each of the issues to build a case against the Republicans in the court of public opinion and to turn the image of Republicans pitting the rich against the poor into a campaign theme.
"This is part of a mosaic that has to be at the heart of the whole national debate and discussion," said Senator Edward M. Kennedy, Democrat of Massachusetts. "I know that isn't what the last election was all about, and I think the American people are not going to accept it."
The Senate version of the midyear budget cuts targets nearly $4 billion less than the $17 billion package that passed in the House nearly two weeks ago. The major cuts still would come from social, labor, education and veterans' programs.
Among the major differences is that the Senate excluded House-approved language to cut the entire $1.3 billion from the Low Income Energy Assistance Program. That program provides heating oil in the winter to poor families in the Northeast. Also, the Senate version calls for $1.2 billion more in cuts for transportation than the $700 million that the House approved.
The Senate proposed rescinding $231 million in military construction money, while the House bill contained no such cuts.
But the most significant differences lie in cuts affecting the Federal Government's huge social service agencies.
In the House version, the Veterans Administration-Housing and Urban Development Subcommittee outlined a $9.3 billion cut to agencies and programs under its control and the Labor-Health and Human Services Subcommittee identified $5.9 billion in reductions to programs it oversees. But the Senate legislation recommends a $6.8 billion cut for the V.A. and HUD.and a $3.05 billion cut for Labor and H.S.S.
The committee vote to repeal the Davis-Bacon Act followed a similar move in a House subcommittee earlier this month.
"Davis-Bacon is a good example of legislation that needs to be reviewed," said Senator Nancy Landon Kassebaum, Republican of Kansas, and the chairwoman of the Senate Committee on Labor and Human Resources
The committee members spent just short of an hour debating the bill before the nine Republicans on the 15-member committee defeated a Democratic amendment to save the law and then approved Mrs. Kassebaum's bill to repeal it.
"It may make it out of committee, but I guarantee that it won't make it anywhere on the Senate floor," Senator Tom Harkin, Democrat of Iowa, said before the final vote. "I will do everything I can to stop it. If that means a filibuster, then that's what it'll be. But it will never see the light of day."
Easily, the most passionate debate of the day was over the tax issue.
When the Rules Committee met today to set terms for the debate over the $189 billion package of business and personal tax breaks, the leadership faced unusual opposition. Representative Bill Archer of Texas, chairman of the Ways and Means Committee, touted the leadership's bill, which Speaker Newt Gingrich of Georgia calls the crown jewel of the Contract with America. But a band of Republicans argued that the $500-a-child credit to families should be limited to those with taxable income of $95,000 rather than $200,000, and sought the right to propose such an amendment.
A second band, led by Representatives Fred Upton, a Michigan Republican, and Glen Browder, an Alaska Democrat, contended that the nation cannot afford tax breaks with the Federal deficit untamed. They asked for the right to offer an amendment that would allow the tax reductions to take effect only after progress had been made in lowering the budget deficit.
The House minority leader, Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, proposed a tax cut of his own -- one that would allow deductions for families with annual incomes below $75,000 -- to help offset the cost of college tuition. Mr. Gephardt's proposal is similar to one made by President Clinton.
The Rules Committee, an arm of the Republican leadership, will not announce which amendments will be allowed until next week, after Mr. Gingrich and other leaders make their choices. Mr. Gephardt's will most likely be allowed, as a courtesy; leaders suggested today that no others would survive .
Mr. Gingrich nevertheless opened negotiations with the Republican dissidents, led by Mr. Upton, who want to make tax cuts contingent on deficit cuts. Mr. Upton and Representative Michael N. Castle, a Delaware Republican, say they have enough Republican support to overturn the Rules Committee's decision on the House floor, and thus keep the tax bill from reaching a vote.
The two met with Gingrich emissaries this afternoon and Mr. Castle said the meeting went well. "The idea," he said, "is that maybe we can work something out."

HEADLINE: Students Protest Education Cuts at Rallies Around U.S.

BYLINE: By PETER APPLEBOME 

DATELINE: ATLANTA, March 29

 BODY:
Students at universities around the country held rallies, marches, teach-ins and vigils today to protest both proposed Federal cuts in education and the Republicans' Contract With America, which they said was responsible.
At Emory University here, where about 300 students rallied while President Clinton held a regional economic conference, speakers included the university's president, William M. Chace, and representatives of homosexual student groups. At Cornell University in Ithaca, N.Y., students built a shantytown of cardboard boxes that they called Newtville after Speaker Newt Gingrich and some began a four-day hunger strike. In New York City, students dressed in black held a mock funeral march from Battery Park to City Hall. 
 Still, few of the events, many of them staged by liberal student groups and billed as the first student protest movement organized over the Internet, drew large numbers. And while it was clear that proposed cuts in student aid struck a raw nerve across a broad cross section of students, it was far less clear whether the issue would galvanize a broad movement of student opposition.
Republicans in the House have called for $1.7 billion in cuts in money already appropriated in the $34 billion Department of Education budget for the 1995 fiscal year and have proposed $20 billion in higher education cuts over the next five years, including ones in student aid and loan programs.
At a rally with students later in the day, Mr. Clinton encouraged them to voice their complaints.
"I want you to enter the debate by determining what is best for you," the President said after outlining his differences with Republicans in Congress. "Stand up for education, and stand up for the future."
To be sure, not all students opposed the cuts.
At Georgia State University here, where no protest was planned, David Prigge and Chris Alford, both physical therapy majors, said they had no problems with the proposed education cuts. "I personally believe the deficit is maybe the No. 1 issue that needs to be addressed," Mr. Prigge said, "and everyone has to bite the bullet."
But many others, whether demonstrating or not, said that any cut in financial aid could endanger their ability to get an education.
"I'm an undergraduate on financial aid," said Matt Jordan, a sophomore at Yale University, where about 100 students held a protest today. "I'm going to have to pay $40,000 in loans when I graduate. I want to fight having to pay $60,000."
Organizers said more than 105 campuses in at least 34 states participated in demonstrations today. At Connecticut College, about 150 students marched from the New London campus to City Hall. At the University of Michigan about 150 students marched through Ann Arbor, stopping briefly at a police station to discuss crime issues.
Some events met organizers' expectations.
At Bowling Green State University in Ohio, Annette Wannamaker, a graduate student in literature, was pleased by the turnout of 300 students at a rally and teach-in.
"This is a sleepy little university in the Midwest, and we've just had the best turnout for our rally and teach-in," she said. "I think on a very basic level, students, even those who don't call themselves liberals, are interested because it's going to affect their student loans."
Here at Emory, Dr. Chace, the university president, lent his support to the demonstrators, saying there had been a contract with America from the nation's inception that included investing in people and the nation's future.
"This year, the Federal Government allocated $31 billion as part of a contract with America to student financial aid, and Emory received about $42 million of that money," he said. "It has made this institution stronger. It has made you stronger. It has made the future stronger. It absolutely must be retained."
Still, some Emory students said that while they did not like the education cuts, they approved of other parts of the Republican agenda.
"I could think of a thousand and one things that should be cut before education," said Andrew Halverstam. "Nevertheless, I support the Contract With America as a whole."
Some students said protest was an option that today's hard-pressed students could not afford to take part in the way students of the 1960's could.
"The difference is we are working ourselves to death," said Stacey Green, a senior at Fresno State University in California.
Others said that student protest was just not for them.
"There is a romanticism about protesting," said Stuart Rosen, 21, an Emory student. "Their parents did it while they were in college."

A demonstrator wore a Clinton mask. (Associated Press); At right, near City Hall in Manhattan, foes of the Contract With America mourned the death of social services. Similar protests were held around the nation. (Philip Greenberg for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Clinton Opens Drive on Economic Gains

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL 

DATELINE: ATLANTA, March 29

 BODY:
President Clinton engaged in an all-day public discussion of the Southern economy here today as he began a yearlong campaign to overcome a political conundrum: that his standing with the voters remains mediocre despite the nation's economic health.
The South, a leader both in economic vitality and in antipathy to Mr. Clinton, may be the region where that difficulty is most pronounced. But from early this morning until late this afternoon, in a regional conference at Emory University, the President demonstrated just how relentless he intends to be in trying to subdue it. 
 In opening remarks that set the tone for similar conferences he plans across the country, Mr. Clinton said he understood the reasons for American unease: primarily, he said, stagnant wages and a decline in job stability. But he told dozens of business leaders and other guests invited to the forum by the White House that apprehension should not obscure clear signs of progress.
"If you look at what has been achieved in this state and in this region for the last 10 years," the President declared, "there is a lot more room for hope than for doubt."
The gathering today was modeled on the session that Mr. Clinton convened in Arkansas shortly before taking office, and its trappings were familiar as he presided over one panel discussion after another from the center of a horseshoe-shaped table.
But in contrast to that December 1992 conference, which was in part a celebration of the victory he had achieved after a campaign in which he pledged to make the economy his top priority, today's gathering was motivated more by concern about how little credit he had received in the months since.
More than 40 percent of the jobs created in the United States in the last two years have been in the South. With a regional unemployment rate of 5.2 percent, the 13 states that were the focus of today's conference are now faring half a percentage point better than the nation as a whole. Donald Ratajczak, an economist who is an expert on the region and has sometimes been critical of Mr. Clinton, used an opening presentation today to paint a picture of the South as "a dynamic region of the country."
But after winning the electoral votes of 5 of the 13 Southern states in 1992, Mr. Clinton has seen a plunge in his popularity that has been more precipitous in the South than in any other region. In choosing the South as his setting to kick off the new drive, Presidential aides said, he is calculating that early and intensive attention may restore his fortunes.
Mr. Clinton, who traveled on to Florida tonight for a three-city tour, began his visit here with a speech on Tuesday evening before organizers of the 1996 Olympic Games, saying he expected them to "stun the world" with their performance. The Atlanta Olympics will fall between the Republican and Democratic National Conventions next summer, and Mr. Clinton made clear that he intended to play a prominent role in them; a President who did not do so, he said, was bound to be "a one-termer."
In an interview published today in The Atlanta Constitution, Mr. Clinton said he saw no political purpose in the economic conference here. And during today's proceedings, he refrained from all but the most general criticism of Congressional Republicans.
But among the business leaders, economists, community leaders, students and others assembled for the discussions, more than a few took up the attack, including Elena Hanggi, director of the Institute for Social Justice, a liberal advocacy group in Little Rock, Ark. None of the participants, who also included such prominent executives as Ronald W. Allen, chairman of Delta Air Lines, and Elizabeth Coleman, chairwoman of Maidenform, criticized Mr. Clinton.
The White House also circulated fact sheets listing "what President Clinton's accomplishments have achieved" in each of the 13 states. And with 29 Cabinet and sub-Cabinet officials roaming the South this week to underscore those accomplishments, it was plain that the Administration regarded the event as an opportunity of considerable moment.
Mr. Clinton allowed himself to pose the question that has been a preoccupation of his political advisers. Referring to gauges that have shown sustained economic growth and declining unemployment since he took office, he said, "You may wonder with these numbers, which are better than we've had in decades, why poll after poll after poll says that people think the country is not going in the right direction."
The explanations offered by Mr. Clinton focused on stagnant wages and increased job instability, factors that he said had left many Americans insecure even in a thriving economy.

HEADLINE: F.C.C. WILL ORDER PHONE COMPANIES TO CUT ACCESS FEE

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The Federal Communications Commission will order the nation's local telephone companies on Thursday to reduce the access charges they impose on long-distance carriers by about $1 billion a year for the next few years, agency officials said today.
The rate cuts could translate into one of the biggest single reductions in long-distance telephone costs since the breakup of the Bell System a decade ago. 
 Consumers are likely to see somewhat lower long-distance prices, though long-distance carriers like AT&T, MCI and Sprint will try to keep as much of the reduction for themselves as the market will allow. This comes at a time of intense competition for consumer business by these big carriers, marked by barrages of television and print advertising.
The access charges the long-distance carriers pay to connect to local telephone networks constitute about 40 percent of the cost of a long-distance call and total about $27 billion a year nationwide. Access charges are also the biggest source of profit for local telephone companies, and the rates have come down very gradually over the last decade.
A typical long-distance call from New York to Los Angeles can cost 10 to 27 cents a minute, depending on the time of day and a customer's discounts. But long-distance carriers must pay about 4 cents a minute to each local phone company on either end of the conversation -- 8 cents a minute on average.
Consumer-advocacy groups and the long-distance carriers have been lobbying the Government for months to push these prices down sharply, charging that the Bell companies have been able to enrich themselves unfairly.
Government officials, speaking on the condition of anonymity, said today that the new rules would reduce these local charges about 4 percent a year for the next few years and begin to take effect this summer. That reduction would roughly double the average decline in access charges in recent years, they said.
While the amounts may be unnoticeably small for individual calls, they add up to very large amounts of money when multiplied by the billions of phone calls made every year.
The F.C.C action is intended as part of a grand bargain in which the local phone companies, in exchange for lowering access charges, would be free to keep more of the profits they can make from long-distance access services by cutting their own expenses. Until now, the F.C.C. has put a limit on the profit the local companies can make from these access services.
In addition, the agency plans to develop rules over the next few months that would give the local companies much greater flexibility in the way they allocate costs in pricing various services.
"The goal here is to reinvigorate competition among long-distance companies by reducing the cost of access," said Reed E. Hundt, chairman of the commission, who refused to discuss any precise details of the plan.
Executives at the regional Bell companies were hesitant to comment on the new rules before they were made public officially. But they were clearly unhappy, in part because they contend that the F.C.C. was taking only modest initial steps to let them retain more of the profit they generate through cost-cutting.
"It's unfair," said one executive, complaining that the Bell companies had already reduced their access charges significantly over the last four years. Frank Gumper, vice president for Federal regulatory planning at the Nynex Corporation, added, "This will obviously cause us to reduce some of our rates faster than we had planned."
The new rules also fall well short of the reductions sought by consumer groups and long-distance carriers.
"This is chicken feed," said Brian Moir, Washington counsel for the International Communications Association, a group that represents large business customers. The association, in an alliance with the Consumer Federation of America and long-distance carriers, had argued for rate cuts that would total about $2 billion a year.
The debate over access charges raises many of the most difficult issues swirling through the communications industry, particularly at a time when local and long-distance carriers are gearing up to invade each other's markets.
Local telephone companies, dominated by the regional Bells, concede that many of their prices are artificially high -- in part because of regulatory policy and in part because they still have a near-monopoly over much of the local phone market.
Commission officials, prodded by consumer groups and long-distance companies, have wanted to force those prices down. At the same time, however, they have been at least partly receptive to the idea that local telephone companies should be given more freedom to reap the benefits from their cost-cutting.
The new rules will modify a regulatory system of price limits that were adopted four years ago. Under such limits, regulators impose a ceiling on the prices a telephone company is allowed to charge and they permit the local phone company to retain more of the profit it can realize. But under those rules, phone companies that earn a rate of return higher than 12.25 percent above their costs have to start giving back the excess profit in the form of subsequent rate reductions.
Commission officials said that they were still haggling over precise details, but had reached agreement on several basic points.
First, the F.C.C. will essentially reduce access charges by 2.8 percent this year -- which the agency estimates will yield rate reductions to the long-distance carriers of about $500 million for the rest of 1995.
But the commission will change the formula under which telephone companies are allowed to adjust the price ceilings for inflation. At the moment, these ceilings can be adjusted up by the rate of inflation, but also down by an amount that reflects the improvements in productivity. The theory here has been that telephone companies should pass along some of the benefits handed to them by the soaring advances in technology.

HEADLINE: Officials Say U.S. Is Unlikely to Meet Clean-Air Goal for 2000

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
In a move that seems likely to reduce their leverage at a global conference, Administration officials conceded today that the United States was unlikely to meet President Clinton's goal of limiting emissions of heat-trapping gases to 1990 levels by the year 2000.
The admission, though not a surprise, is expected to make it far harder for American officials at a global climate conference now under way in Berlin to persuade other countries to reduce output of gases like carbon dioxide, which trap heat in the atmosphere much the way glass traps heat in a greenhouse. 
 Convinced that emission of the so-called greenhouse gases is causing global warming, the Clinton Administration is trying to take the lead to get countries to make new and firmer pledges to cut such emissions well into the 21st century.
In the 1992 presidential campaign and again in April 1993, Mr. Clinton pledged that the United States would meet the 2000 goal. In the campaign, he criticized President George Bush for rejecting that goal, set at the 1992 Earth Summit conference in Rio de Janeiro. At the time, Mr. Bush and others in his Administration said that limiting the gases, which result primarily from the burning of fossil fuels, would damage the American economy.
Today, however, several Administration officials acknowledged that the United States was unlikely to meet Mr. Clinton's goal.
"It's going to be very hard," said one White House official. "You could say the way things look now we may be a little short."
At a recent news briefing, Timothy E. Wirth, the Under Secretary of State for Global Affairs and head of the American delegation to Berlin, said the United States would probably miss the goal by a wide margin.
In an interview today, he was somewhat less pessimistic. Still, in previously discussing the most important heat-trapping gas, carbon dioxide, Mr. Wirth estimated that the United States would emit 30 million more tons of carbon in 2000 than it did in 1990.
In 1990, the United States emitted 1.3 billion tons of carbon into the atmosphere. That level would rise to more than 1.4 billion tons by the year 2000 without energy conservation measures, Mr. Wirth said, but he predicted that energy conservation steps the Administration will put forward would limit the emissions to about 1.33 billion tons.
The Administration has relied primarily on voluntary and cooperative measures, like providing technical assistance to companies to install more efficient lighting and heating systems. Energy efficiency is vital to reducing emissions because carbon dioxide is produced by burning coal, oil and natural gas, the fuels that make modern economies run.
Environmental officials in the Administration say that emissions of heat-trapping gases have increased more rapidly than expected over the last three years and are almost 5 percent above 1990 levels. They cite several reasons: economic growth has been strong, fuel prices have been relatively low and the Administration was unable to win Congressional approval of an energy tax that was to be a cornerstone of the its efforts to reduce the use of fossil fuels. Administration officials say that tax would have cut carbon emissions by 25 million tons annually by the year 2000.
Many environmentalists cite another reason for rising emissions -- that the Administration has not sought stricter fuel efficiency levels for automobiles for fear of alienating companies and consumers.
Mr. Wirth said today that Vice President Al Gore was overseeing development of further steps that would be announced in October to fulfill Mr. Clinton's commitment.

HEADLINE: House Subcommittee Initiates a Bill to Establish a Board to Manage Washington's Finances

BYLINE: By KAREN DE WITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
A House subcommittee today voted unanimously to establish a board that would essentially take over the financial management of the District of Columbia. The city, which is on the verge of bankruptcy, faces a deficit of about $400 million.
The legislation would create a five-member board, which is modeled on the financial bailout agencies used for New York and Philadelphia. It was described by staff members who worked on the bill as "the strongest ever created" for any American city. The board, which will be selected by the President and Congress, will control the city's spending and will set up an annual balanced budget, as well as a four-year financial plan for the city. 
 The bill retains some degree of home rule for the mayor and the 11-member Council. Mayor Marion S. Barry Jr. will be able to select two new officials provided for in the bill, the chief financial officer and the inspector general. But his choices must be approved by the board, and only the board can dismiss them.
The board will control the finances until the city balances four budgets in a row and repays any money borrowed with the board's cooperation. In addition, any money the city borrows in private markets would need approval by the board.
The bill is expected to be taken up by the full House next week, where few difficulties are expected. To go into effect it would have to be approved by the Senate and President Clinton, who has said he supports such an authority.
House Republicans said their goal was to have the board in operation overseeing the city's finances by the end of April.
After years of what some have called chronic overspending, muddled accounting practices and ignored signs of trouble, the city was found to be essentially bankrupt after Mr. Barry took office. Many in the city, however, say the situation is mainly a result of its being the nation's capital and having so much Government property that it cannot tax.
"Measured by the desperate condition of the city and what I have been able to get included in this bill, I concluded a few hours ago at the end of the final negotiating session that I could become a co-sponsor," said Eleanor Holmes Norton, the District of Columbia's nonvoting delegate in Congress.
The bill approved by the Oversight of Government Management subcommittee avoided efforts by some House members to include nonfinancial matters, including lifting the city's ban on guns and imposing an anti-abortion measure.
This afternoon, members of the subcommittee emphasized that the bill won bipartisan support, playing down the tough compromises that went into it.
"This is a major bipartisan effort," said Representative Thomas M. Davis 3d, the Virginia Republican who heads the subcommittee. "This bill does not abolish home rule. It is not designed to micromanage."
Mr. Clinton, in consultation with Congress, will appoint the board members. They will serve with no compensation for an initial three-year period and must be residents of the District or pay taxes to the city, including business fees or wage taxes.
In Atlanta, Mr. Clinton made clear that he would support a financial oversight authority for the District. He said his Administration had closely worked with Congress in drafting the bill introduced today and was ready to work further with it to determine what help might be appropriate.
"The financial crisis in the District is serious and demands immediate attention," Mr. Clinton said. "Although other cities have suffered similar problems, Washington D.C. plays a uniquely important role in the nation's life. It is the nation's capital and is important not just to the people who live there but to all citizens of the United States."
"I care deeply about the District and its residents," he said. "They deserve a government that delivers municipal services efficiently and effectively."
Mr. Davis said today that the bill's path in the House would be smoothed because of the interest Speaker Newt Gingrich has in it.
Representative Gil Gutknecht, the Minnesota Republican who is vice chairman of the subcommittee, said Mr. Gingrich had been at some negotiating sessions on the bill.
Speaking this afternoon at the Acton Institute for the Study of Religion and Liberty, Mr. Gingrich talked about the negative effects of welfare citing the Anacostia section of Washington as an example of the "destructive undermining of the poor".
"You are in the national capital of the greatest country in the world," Mr. Gingrich said. "And you have citizens in your capital who we have abandoned as though they were not Americans and were not endowed by God."
But David Clarke, chairman of the City Council was not appeased.
"We wanted to be able to review contracts over a million dollars among other things," said Mr. Clark. "Instead we will be talked to. We will be notified. It's a disappointment."

HEADLINE: Pentagon Picks Miami as Base for Latin America Command

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
In a decision that could bring 1,500 new jobs and thousands of additional visitors to Florida, the Pentagon today selected Miami as the next headquarters for United States military activities in Latin America.
The announcement was timed to coincide with President Clinton's arrival in Tallahassee, Florida's capital, this evening. "The President was going to be in Florida and has good news to share with them," said a senior White House official. 
 At the airport in Tallahassee, where he arrived this evening after a daylong economic conference in Atlanta, Mr. Clinton told a welcoming crowd that the regional military command was "working to promote democracy," and added, "The center of that effort will be in your state."
Florida, which will have 25 electoral votes in the 1996 Presidential election, had powerful Congressional support for two of the six sites the Defense Department was considering: Miami and Tampa.
Military officials said they concluded that Miami was the best choice after considering the cost, quality of life for military personnel and the city's standing as a cultural, business and transportation hub of the Americas.
Miami civic leaders have estimated that the new headquarters will provide 1,500 new jobs, 10,000 additional visitors a year and a five-year infusion of $100 million in the local economy.
With United States forces scheduled to withdraw from the current headquarters of the Southern Command in Panama by 1999 under terms of the Panama Canal Treaties, the Pentagon's search for a new home for the command had touched off a mad scramble among six communities. Besides Miami and Tampa, the Defense Department was also considering New Orleans, Atlanta, Washington and Puerto Rico.
At a time when the Pentagon is closing scores of military installations nationwide, the new headquarters offered a rare opportunity to win a major headquarters with 700 military and civilian employees and a $27 million annual payroll.
The Southern Command is responsible for United States military activities in 19 countries in Central and South America, excluding the Caribbean and Mexico. The command, which now has 8,500 troops in Panama, provides humanitarian aid, wages an aggressive anti-drug campaign in the region and protects the Panama Canal.
Some losers said they would demand an explanation from the Defense Department. "We hear it's very, very close and so we are now asking for them to show us their cost analysis," said Representative Robert L. Livingston, a Louisiana Republican who heads the House Appropriations Committee and was pushing hard for New Orleans.
Indeed, Pentagon officials said if cost -- including moving expenses and cost of living -- were the only criterion, New Orleans might have been the cheapest site. But senior military officials said cost was only one factor that the Pentagon weighed. Others included quality of life for military personnel, good schools for their children, good airline connections and strong cultural ties to Latin America.
Of four sites in the Miami area, the Pentagon said today it would seek to develop a 140,000-square-foot headquarters at the United States Coast Guard base at Richmond Heights, in southern Dade Country.
The building's planning and design will continue through next year, as will environmental impact reviews, with construction to begin in 1997 or 1998. The military wants to move in by late summer 1998. Pentagon officials said the total cost of the project would be about $60 million.
"Obviously, we are thrilled that the President has made Miami the choice for the Southern Command headquarters," said Carlos Palomares, the chief executive of Citibank Florida, who is chairman of the Beacon Council, an economic development organization in South Florida.

HEADLINE: Orange County to Vote on Tax

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: SANTA ANA, Calif., March 29

 BODY:
Although they showed no enthusiasm for the prospect of a half-cent increase in the sales tax, Orange County's supervisors decided early today to let the voters decide whether to impose the increase for the next 10 years to help pay for investment losses.
For years Orange County has prided itself on avoiding tax increases, but three months after risky investments plunged the county into bankruptcy, the mood here has changed. That paved the way for the board's 5-to-0 vote at 1:20 this morning in favor of putting the tax increase, which will be known as Measure R, on the June 27 ballot. 
 The sales tax increase of 0.5 percent would be added to the current rate of 7.75 percent to help the county make up for $1.7 billion in investment losses.
One Supervisor, Marian Bergeson, said it was not her intention to raise taxes when she was sworn in, but "we have all taken a dose of realism."
A second Supervisor, Jim Silva, said he was unhappy with the prospect of a tax increase but believed the voters should decide.
Without a sales tax increase, Mr. Silva said, the county will be unable to meet its financial obligations.
Although most of the 50 or so speakers who addressed the supervisors before their vote opposed the tax increase, there was powerful support for letting the voters decide from speakers representing the League of Women Voters, labor unions and others.
Angela J. Keefe, president of Local 681 of the Hotel Employees and Restaurant Employees Union, said the 5,000 workers she represented were on the county's lowest economic rungs and stood to be hurt the most by a tax increase. Nevertheless, Ms. Keefe said, the workers would be willing to support Measure R.

HEADLINE: U.S. Proposes Opening Power Cost and Supply To Competition

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The Federal agency that oversees the nation's utilities approved a sweeping proposal today to force utilities to open their transmission systems to competitors.
The proposed rule, which would greatly increase competition, would eventually bring down wholesale rates, if the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission decides to put it into effect later this year, as expected. However, the changes could be modified after all sides have the chance to comment. 
 Strictly speaking, the proposal affects only the transmission of power to wholesale customers like big industrial plants, not to retail customers like homeowners. But opening wholesale competition is expected to have a ripple effect, eventually lowering the cost of electricity for all customers.
Robert Glynn, executive vice president of the Pacific Gas and Electric Company in San Francisco, the nation's largest investor-owned utility, said, "This is one more step toward the competitive electric industry of the future." He added, "Most people think that greater competition will lower prices."
The proposal would require 137 investor-owned utilities to allow outside access to their transmission grids on the same basis that they use the grids.
Charles E. Bayless, chief executive of the Tucson Electric Power Company, said: "This will make it easier for wholesale buyers like smaller utilities and independent power producers to get access and sell their power. This will also help insure that the lowest-cost producers are the ones that will tend to deliver the power."
Utilities would have to file prices for transmission service with the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission and to disclose their transmission costs.
Elizabeth A. Moler, who heads the commission, an independent body within the Department of Energy, said at a meeting today: "Clearly, this commission is not introducing competition to the electricity industry. Competition has introduced itself. Marketplace economics have changed. Customers are simply demanding access to lower-cost supplies from the other regions and access to newer, lower-cost generation resources."
The pressure for the changes has come from independent power producers, which have rapidly grown and have many plants that produce cheap surplus power. The independents want to sell that surplus over the big investor-owned utilities' transmission grids.
Even though they are required by law to transmit outside suppliers' power, the big utilities, afraid of losing customers, have often stymied the independents by making them go through a long process of getting orders from the commission on a case-by-case basis. These utilities have also used the information filed for such orders by their smaller rivals to go after the same customers, often beating them out.
The aim of today's order is to equalize opportunities so that outsiders will have the same information and the same cost of using the grid as the utility owning the transmission system.
Opening the system is expected to make it increasingly possible for industrial customers to build their own power plants and to sell the surplus or to buy power directly from independents, rather then from their local utilities.
Because in many cases, utilities have had to build power plants and transmission lines specifically to serve big industrial sites, this could leave them with what are called stranded costs -- assets that are underused and that would become an economic burden. That, in turn, could lead to overall rate increases and declines in the utilities' stock prices. To avoid such results, the commission proposed a way for utilities to recover their costs.
This recovery provision would allow utilities to file requests with the commission to recapture costs, either by making a customer who leaves the system pay an exit fee or by allowing an extra charge for power transmission.
Mr. Glynn of Pacific Gas and Electric said the industry was pleased that the commission had recognized the possible costs and had provided a means of recovery.
Mary Kenkel, a spokeswoman for the Edison Electric Institute, a trade group that represents many investor-owned utilities, said that thanks to the recovery provision, the proposal today would not undermine investor confidence.
The five commissioners who met in Washington all said that they could not just throw open a transmission system without allowing utilities to recover from the losses that could result. But they were unanimous, as well, that it was time to end inequities in power transmission.
"Now is the time to eliminate the transmission-market power of the utilities over which we have jurisdiction," said one of the commissioners, William L. Massey. "How can there be truly robust competition if buyer and seller can't reach each other?"

HEADLINE: Single-Family Home Sales Lowest in Nearly 3 Years

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
Sales of new single-family homes fell a larger-than-expected 14.0 percent in February to the lowest level in almost three years, even as mortgage rates dipped below 9 percent for the first time in four months.
All regions of the country reported declines. The largest came in the West, where flooding devastated communities in California and sales made their sharpest drop in 13 years. 
 "Builders have been telling us that higher interest rates are weakening demand, hurting sales and stamping out the housing recovery," said Jim Irvine, a home builder from Portland, Ore., who is president of the National Association of Home Builders.
Total sales decreased to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 551,000 -- the lowest since April 1992 -- after rising a revised 2.6 percent in January, the Commerce Department said. A month ago, the Government estimated that January sales increased 3.8 percent.
The outlook for the rest of the year is not promising, builders said.
"In 1995 we'll experience less business -- how much less, we don't know," said Tim Eller, president and chief executive of the housing subsidiary of the Centex Corporation of Dallas.
The drop in housing has also led to declines in the appliance industry. The Association of Home Appliance Manufacturers said shipments in February fell in all six major categories -- ranges, washers, dryers, dishwashers, refrigerators and freezers. Before today's report, economists had expected a 3.7 percent decline in February new home sales.
The drops are seen as a result of the Federal Reserve's board fight to hold down inflation by pushing up interest rates, which makes housing more expensive for buyers by raising monthly mortgage payments.
Robert Dederick, an economic consultant at the Northern Trust Company in Chicago, said the question now was whether the economy will grow at a more modest pace in 1995 and 1996 -- or slide into recession.
"The economy is trying for a landing," Mr. Dederick said. However, the sharp decline in home sales "doesn't tell us whether it's soft, hard or in-between," he said.
Though borrowing costs are higher than a year ago, mortgage rates have been moving lower since December. After peaking at 9.25 percent in December, mortgage rates fell to 8.73 percent by the end of February and to 8.40 percent last week. New home sales should decline by 6 to 7 percent this year, while starts of new construction are likely to drop even more, by 12 to 13 percent, said David Seiders, chief economist of the National Association of Home Builders.
In today's report, the Commerce Department said new home sales dropped by 28.5 percent in the West in February to an annual rate of 118,000; fell 10.7 percent in the South to 266,000, declined 8.8 percent in the Midwest to 104,000, and decreased 1.6 percent in the Northeast to 63,000.
The supply of homes for sale nationwide increased by 1.7 percent in February to an annual rate of 350,000 -- the largest since June 1990, just before the last recession started -- while the inventory of vacant homes climbed to a 7.7-month supply.
The Commerce Department also reported today that the average price of a new home increased by 5.6 percent, to $155,700 in February from $147,400 in January.
"People simply don't feel good enough about job prospects or job security to buy a home," said Edmund Woods, president of the National Association of Realtors, regardless of where mortgage rates stand.

HEADLINE: End of Urban Agency Draws Fears of Neglect

BYLINE: By BRETT PULLEY 

 BODY:
Seventeen floors above 125th Street in Harlem, Donald J. Cogsville was reluctantly preparing to move out of his corner office, with its expansive windows and sweeping views of Central Park and the Hudson River.
"We've been cut out," said Mr. Cogsville, the chairman and chief executive of the Harlem Urban Development Corporation, as he sat surrounded by architects' renderings and scale models of grand development projects, many of which never came to be. 
 Although he acknowledged that creating jobs and growth in the ailing economy of Harlem had been difficult, Mr. Cogsville said he still could not understand why, after 23 years, the Pataki administration had decided to shut down the corporation and fold its activities into the New York State Urban Development Corporation.
That move "leaves the future of this community to outsiders," he said.
"You can't develop Harlem from downtown," he said. "You have to be here, constantly pushing, out there all the time."
Charles Gargano, Commissioner of the State Department of Economic Development, said that eliminating the Harlem organization as a separate entity would promote efficiency and save $3.5 million a year. He pointed out that financing for another economic development group in western New York was also eliminated in Gov. George E. Pataki's final proposed budget.
The elimination of the corporation will not become official until the Legislature passes a final budget, but no one expects the agency to win a reprieve, and the office could shut down as early as this week, when the fiscal year ends.
The demise of the Harlem Urban Development Corporation comes as Congress has approved plans for the creation of a Federal empowerment zone, using investment and tax breaks to lure businesses to Harlem and parts of the South Bronx. But the empowerment-zone effort faces a threat from the Pataki administration, since the Governor's budget plan includes only one-tenth the amount the state had originally planned to contribute to its creation this year.
State legislators have said they hope to persuade Mr. Pataki to increase the empowerment zone allocation in the budget.
The Harlem Urban Development Corporation, set up in 1971 by Gov. Nelson A. Rockefeller, was intended to spur housing and commercial development by attracting private and public investment in blighted areas of Harlem.
"We fought so desperately hard to get the Harlem Urban Development Corporation," said Representative Charles B. Rangel, a founder of the agency. "This seems like the end of development in Harlem as we have known it."
Another co-founder, the Rev. William M. James, said the state's action ignores the organization's purpose. "It came about so that we could have a say in our own destiny," said Mr. James, the agency's board chairman.
But like many urban economic development groups that emerged during that era, the Harlem agency had difficulty in attracting investment. By the late 1970's, after modest success building low-income housing, the organization's leaders decided that the only way to attract commercial development would be to keep middle-income residents from leaving.
With Mr. Cogsville at the helm, the organization sought to develop housing on neglected city-owned property, which in 1980 accounted for about 65 percent of the land in Harlem. It teamed with local developers and housing groups to help plan and negotiate financing for more than 6,500 mixed-income housing units in the area. Other housing groups also developed projects, and since then the land in Harlem owned by the city has decreased to about 35 percent, according to the agency.
But the group's success in creating jobs and expansion has been far more limited. It did play a major role in revitalizing 125th Street, helping to renovate the legendary Apollo Theater and negotiating to bring many retailers onto the strip. Still, by 1990, the unemployment rate in Harlem had reached 13.4 percent, compared with 11.1 percent a decade earlier, and 30 percent of the population was living in poverty, according to Federal census figures.
The development corporation has also attracted criticism from community residents and state officials. William Stern, who between 1983 and 1985 was the chief executive officer of the State Urban Development Corporation, said many people viewed the organization as ineffective, and he criticized it for wasteful spending. "Not much that they did ever worked or had anything to do with the economic development of Harlem," said Mr. Stern, a Harlem native who is now a private investor in New York City. The agency and other local urban development groups "are something better done without," he said.
Mr. Gargano said $500,000 had been kept in the state budget to pursue capital projects in the Harlem area, but many of the agency's 42 employees, including Mr. Cogsville, will lose their jobs at the end of this week. Those who remain will move to state offices in lower Manhattan.
Mr. Gargano said Mr. Cogsville's office on 125th Street might be taken over by the State Division of Housing and Community Renewal. That department is headed by Commissioner Joseph Holland, a Pataki appointee and businessman from Harlem who has been a critic of the organization. After all, "the space is going to be available, and we have a lease there," Mr. Gargano said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Donald J. Cogsville in his office overlooking Harlem. The Harlem Urban Development Corporation, which he heads, is being shut down. (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Suddenly Put on the Defensive, Pataki Takes to the Road to Do Battle for His Budget

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: NEW CITY, March 28

 BODY:
Gov. George E. Pataki hunched forward in the metal chair and stared intensely at the student with the nose ring, someone else upset about his budget: It would increase state college tuition, close campuses, hurt poor people, hurt single mothers, hurt her, she said.
As she spoke, the Republican Governor fidgeted like an excited student waiting to answer. "By far the most important part of this budget for students, for you, for everybody, and you might disagree, is that we are going to start creating jobs in New York State," he finally blurted out, karate-chopping his hands together. "We want you to get a decent, good, affordable higher education in New York, and get a job." 
 No chance meeting, the encounter between the Governor and the student was arranged for reporters as part of an aggressive campaign to sell Mr. Pataki's first budget, using commercials, letter-writing drives and personal appearances by the Governor across the state. [In an unusual move, Mr. Pataki also plans to deliver a five-minute statewide television address Thursday night calling on voters to support his budget.]
Mr. Pataki's campaign, which hit full stride just this week, comes as he has been thrown on the defensive by a multimillion-dollar media and lobbying blitz by opponents of his plan to cut taxes, cut spending and require able-bodied welfare recipients to work.
Mr. Pataki is also struggling to overcome a series of public-relations setbacks this week, which included his retreat from a threat to close down state agencies if the budget is late and efforts by some Republican allies to get him to delay future phases of his four-year tax-cut plan.
Now, in the most critical battle of his young administration, he finds himself being outspent by opponents and having to fight for votes in the divided Legislature. Given that backdrop, even supporters say his budget road show, which has taken him to eight cities in seven days, is belated.
"Certainly coming out earlier would have helped," said Rick Zimmerman of the Farm Bureau, an agricultural industry group that is financing radio commercials supporting Mr. Pataki's budget. "But I think the Governor still can help his case by articulating his vision."
Mr. Pataki and his allies, chiefly the state Republican Party and business groups, have sought to build pressure on Democratic and Republican lawmakers, mainly from upstate, by flooding their districts with letters, phone calls and television and radio commercials endorsing the Pataki budget.
But at the heart of the campaign is Mr. Pataki himself, whose amiable personality and regular-guy style, his advisers believe, make him an effective pitchman for a budget that opponents have attacked as heartless to the poor and generous to the rich.
It is a difficult task, given that Mr. Pataki's promises are for unspecified future economic growth, while his opponents' warnings are of sharp and immediate pain. "The other side needs only to say this threatens the foundations of civilization, details to follow," said David Shaffer, secretary of the Business Council, a lobbying group that supports Mr. Pataki. "His job is tougher."
Mr. Pataki has attempted to sell his policies by emphasizing the notion that, above all, they are reasonable. In Rochester last week, he told 800 Rotarians that the State University "will still be the most affordable in the Northeast" even after he cuts its aid by 31 percent.
At a Chamber of Commerce meeting outside Buffalo the same day, he said New York will still have the largest college tuition assistance program and still spend more per Medicaid recipient than any other state even if his budget passes unchanged.
Two days later in Poughkeepsie, he told 600 business leaders that cutting welfare will allow "people to develop a sense of self-worth and confidence and dignity." He also said that a welfare mother who works a minimum-wage job two hours a week will take home more money per month under his plan than under current policies.
And here in New City, he said that New York would still have higher income tax rates than its neighboring states at the end of his four-year plan, despite critics' claims that it cuts taxes too quickly, too deeply.
Everywhere he goes, he cajoles his audiences to lobby their legislators to support his plans. "I welcome your support and your willingness to get out there and tell your legislators and tell the people of this state that this budget is what we need to restore people's confidence in the future of New York State," he said in Hamburg, near Buffalo.
Everywhere he goes, Mr. Pataki also encounters protesters, usually state workers, state university students and advocates for poor people. At times, he wades into their ranks to debate his policies. But in Rochester, he entered a Rotary Club luncheon through a basement entrance to avoid demonstrators, only to have a woman hand him a bouquet of dead flowers as he stepped onto the podium.
Like an accomplished miler, Mr. Pataki has the ability to appear as if he is loping even as he runs hard. In New City, he slouched nonchalantly in a chair while fielding questions from about two dozen business representatives in the office of the Rockland County Executive, C. Scott Vanderhoef. But his answers were crisp and well-received, particularly when he pledged to reduce red tape, cut business taxes and lower utility costs.
"It was kind of inspirational," said Barry Hersh, a project manager with Reynolds Metal Corporation. "I got the feeling he really means it."
After his session with the business leaders, Mr. Pataki's aides ushered into the room five college students who had been demonstrating against his budget outside. But though he talked to the group for nearly 20 minutes, offering assurances that poor people would not be denied college educations under his budget, he won no converts in the group.
"He just met with us because he knew it would look good for the press," said Bobta Kim, 19, who carried a cardboard gravestone that read, "R.I.P. CUNY."

HEADLINE: Private Research Unit Sees Big Shortfall for Whitman

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON 

DATELINE: TRENTON, March 29

 BODY:
A respected nonpartisan legislative research group predicted today that the state would take in $680 million less in revenue than Gov. Christine Whitman has anticipated, raising serious questions about her ability to deliver another round of tax cuts.
The prediction by the research group, the Office of Legislative Services, represents the widest forecasting discrepancy with a Governor's budget in at least a decade. Last year, the projections by the group and Governor Whitman were nearly identical. 
 Administration officials said they had used a different method to make their forecast this year, one that anticipated a larger surge in tax revenues when people start paying their taxes next month than the research group predicted. They also said the group's forecast about the state's business outlook was too pessimistic.
"We are not trying to palm off a rosy scenario to your committees," Brian W. Clymer, the State Treasurer, said in defense of the Governor at the opening of the annual budget hearings today. "But rather, to make as reasonable and as informative a forecast as we can."
If the usually reliable legislative research group is correct, however, the $680 million discrepancy is a serious blow. The Governor was counting on having to find only $300 million of cuts plus additional revenues, in a $16 billion budget.
Democrats pounced on the gap in forecasting, accusing the Whitman administration today of cooking its revenue outlook. They said the administration was trying to quiet growing concerns among even some Republicans over the wisdom of enacting Mrs. Whitman's full 15 percent tax cut this spring, a year ahead of her original schedule and months before the impact of Federal cuts in state aid are known.
"We have been saying that tax cuts are great if you explain how you're paying for them, and the Governor has failed to do so," said Tom Byrne, the state Democratic Party chairman, when the legislative figures were released this morning. "It is becoming abundantly clear that a billion in gimmicks and shifts to property taxes wil not only hurt her budget but every New Jerseyan's."
Senator Bernard F. Kenny, the ranking Democrat on the joint Assembly-Senate budget committee that conducted today's hearing, said: "Let's be clear. Governor Whitman is not lifting the tax burden from hard-working New Jerseyans, she is only shifting it from one pocket to another."
Last year, the difference between the administration and the legislative forecasts amounted to less than a hundredth of one percent, but the contending forecasts, on which all budgeting is based, differed by 3.3 percent this year.
Revenue forecasts predict how much money the state will collect in taxes and fees during a budget year, and they are often the subject of dispute. Over the years, however, the legislative forecasts have usually proved to be more accurate than those of the administration in office.
The problems for the Whitman administration are compounded by forecasts from Rutgers University predicting that that the state economy this year is not likely to match last year's level of job creation and tax receipts, which made the Governor's last round of tax cuts possible.
Mr. Clymer, the treasurer, said the administration used a different method to forecast revenues for the budget, which takes effect on June 30.
Besides charging the legislative group with misreading minor parts of the Whitman budget proposal, Mr. Clymer said that the legislative agency's forecast failed to anticipate a surge in income tax receipts, as taxes are collected next month, because of 1994's robust economy.
Because it is a neutral arm of the Legislature, which is controlled by Republicans, the office did not reply to Mr. Clymer's charges. In his presentation to the joint budget committees, however, David J. Rosen, the office's chief forecaster, said that New Jersey's state receipts might, if anything, turn out to be lower still.
The legislative group assumes a gradual slowing of economic growth, Mr. Rosen said, but some economists think that the Federal Reserve Bank's interest rate increases "may precipitate a more drastic slowdown in economic activity."
"If this alternative view proves correct, our revenue estimates are too high."
In addition, Mr. Rosen reminded the committee that the Governor's revenue forecasts depend on the enactment of many small taxes and fees, like a tax on the savings from store discount coupons, that many members of the Legislature oppose. If all these small money-makers are not enacted on schedule, he said, revenues will fall still more.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Slumps as Dealers Expect No Rate Change to Ease Pressure

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar slumped against leading currencies yesterday as dealers increasingly saw little chance that interest rate changes would provide any relief for the currency.
Dealers sold dollars yesterday in anticipation that the Bundesbank would not lower interest rates at its policy meeting today . A similar selloff occurred on Tuesday, when dealers, anticipating that the Federal Reserve would not raise rates at its policy meeting, pushed the dollar lower. When the Fed left interest rates unchanged on Tuesday, the dollar fell further. 
 Higher interest rates help a currency by making bank deposits denominated in it more attractive.
"A combination of the Fed's inactivity and rising expectations of no Bundesbank activity have taken a bite out of the buck," said Kevin Lawrie, manager of foreign exchange at the Mellon Bank in Pittsburgh.
Statements today by Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin and Lawrence H. Summers, Treasury Under Secretary for International Affairs, calling for a strong dollar helped the currency pare its losses.
The dollar dropped as low as 88.04 Japanese yen before settling in New York trading at 88.43 yen, down from 88.90 yen on Tuesday. The dollar also touched a low of 1.3737 German marks, its lowest since March 8, when it reached a post-World War II low of 1.3455 marks in intraday trading. The dollar settled at 1.3825 marks, down from 1.3882 marks.
Relatively strong economic growth will probably keep the Bundesbank from cutting rates today, analysts said. The economy grew 2.5 percent in western Germany last year.
"With wage settlements not satisfactory enough, I think the Bundesbank will hold back" from a rate cut, said Ken Nixon, currency adviser at the Harris Trust and Savings Bank in New York.
Some unions in Germany have reached labor settlements that included annual wage increases of nearly 4 percent. Bundesbank officials have expressed concern about large wage increases.
While interest-rate concerns weighed on the currency today, "Rubin's and Summers's comments have helped the dollar" rebound from its lows, said Stephen Flanagan, currency dealer at Credit Agricole in New York.
Mr. Rubin said on CNBC television that the Clinton Administration believed a strong dollar was good for the economy. And Mr. Summers told a Washington trade conference that a "strong dollar is in America's national interest."

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
A gritty campaign from DDB Needham seems to be the medicine Digital Equipment needed.

BYLINE: By Anthony Ramirez 

 BODY:
FOR DDB Needham Worldwide, it was one of the toughest assignments possible for an advertising agency: persuade people to buy big, expensive products from a company when everyone thinks the company is dead or dying.
The solution, since last fall, has been chin-up bravado and television commercials so irritating that no one could mistake the company for dead. The campaign moves into a new phase next month. 
 Last summer, the Digital Equipment Corporation, a big computer manufacturer, certainly looked like a goner. From a 1987 peak of almost $200 a share, its stock price had plummeted to barely $20. Cumulative losses since 1991 totaled $5 billion, or close to the gross domestic product of Iceland.
Even the company's top marketing officer, Charles B. Holleran, has adopted a kind of gallows humor. "You're calling me?" Mr. Holleran said at the beginning of a recent interview. "You're going to pay for the phone call? Good! We could really use the money."
New management, under Robert Palmer, the chief executive, had been furiously cutting costs and reorganizing the business for months, but by last summer the company realized it needed to get its advertising and marketing house in order, too.
For years, Digital, with a multitude of products, had used a multitude of ad agencies, as many as 77, with Ketchum Advertising in Pittsburgh and Young & Rubicam in New York being the largest of the American agencies. When the company was making money, it didn't matter that Digital's message was fractured.
But when it was hemorrhaging money, Digital had to reassure customers, suppliers, and perhaps even its own staff and executives, that the company was still in business. Last summer, Digital executives asked five ad agencies, including DDB Needham Worldwide, to pitch the account.
Surprisingly, DDB Needham not only had a basic strategy but also specific creative ideas for a campaign. "It had stopping power," recalled Mr. Holleran about the DDB Needham pitch. "It was gritty as opposed to pretty."
DDB Needham executives knew they had their work cut out for them, even before they won the $90 million account in August. When potential customers on a test panel were presented with identical ads for personal computer products, but each with a different Hewlett-Packard, I.B.M., or Digital logo, they rated the Digital ad as much as two-thirds lower in believability.
The company's financial problems created "an ugly algebra of fear and doubt," said Mike Rogers, executive creative director at DDB Needham. To twist around the negative perceptions of the company, Mr. Rogers, DDB Needham's executive creative director -- along with Tony Romeo, Giff Crosby, David Nathanson and John Staffen -- brainstormed ideas for days before the pitch.
They hit upon the idea of a series of stark, quick-cut TV commercials tied together with a signature coda of take-no-prisoners rock music as well as a series of print ads related to the TV campaign.
And they pursued the unusual media strategy of buying commercials mostly on Sunday. The commercials would boom out of the television set and the fast editing would light up a quiet Sunday morning living room like a strobe light.
Typical of the approach was a commercial that began with a stinging guitar chord, followed by quick cuts of scenes of tornadoes, flood and fire.
"When billions of dollars' worth of mission-critical computing is threatened," booms the urgent voice-over, "who do people call?" Then, the Digital logo appears, then lettering like that punched out of plastic labeling tape appears. "Hell Has Our Phone Number," it reads.
Perhaps the boldest ad was one that ran only twice on Thanksgiving. "Some of the contracts we've won lately," reads the same style of labeling tape, as the same heavy metal music blares. Names like Navistar, Rockwell and Blockbuster Entertainment Group appear. Then a shot of what looks like a medieval engraving of Death with a scythe.
"Today's Score," the label continues. "Digital 15, Grim Reaper 0."
While the effectiveness of corporate awareness campaigns is often hard to measure, Mr. Holleran of Digital said that consumer awareness had risen 13 percent and that Digital had received as many as 3,000 calls on a Sunday to its "800" number for more information.
Perhaps more importantly, Digital has reported a profitable second quarter, and Wall Street analysts project a narrow loss for the year as well as a profit for the 1996 year ending June 30.
Now, the campaign is entering a more settled phase, too, with advertising returning once again to specific products and services, but with the same bat-out-of-hell approach.
A recent ad, playing off recent computer hacker cases but conceived before the arrest of Kevin Mitnick, contends that Digital has some of the toughest computer security services in the business.
"For more information on how tough Digital security is," reads the labeling tape, as the picture of a prisoner comes on the screen, "Contact Inmate Number 23-38AA3872, Leavenworth, Kansas."

LENGTH: 1578 words

HEADLINE: Heavy Angst When It's 'Ivy or Else'

BYLINE: By ANDREE BROOKS 

DATELINE: NORWALK, Conn., March 26

 BODY:
Within easy reach in the green canvas backpack that Karen Sumberg carries to high school is a pocket-size daily planner that helps her track all the assignments, volunteer activities, projects and club meetings that are crammed into every free hour.
Ever since sixth grade, Karen's daily life has been circumscribed by all she must do to reach her self-imposed goal of getting into a top-tier college. "You are always asking yourself, 'Are they going to like this or that?' " said Karen, a sophomore, as she strode across the marble foyer of Temple Beth El, in Norwalk, where she spends Sunday mornings as a religious-school aide. 
 By 1 P.M. she was ladling fruit salad at a soup kitchen. Later she rushed home to change for a swim club awards dinner, at which she was honored as co-captain of the senior team (sportsmanship wins points on a college application, too). Many evenings she practices her vocabulary skills on a computer in the family room. Last summer, she completed an S.A.T. prep course at Wellesley College. She has joined a Model United Nations debating club and has even taken up rowing.
"I want to go where I want to go," Karen said. "I don't want to settle for where I can get in. And the schools look for these things. Grades alone aren't going to do it."
Determined? Yes. Unusual? No. Getting into an increasingly rarefied list of the nation's most prestigious colleges, once a status symbol, is now perceived as a survival issue by families who see a future dogged by diminished opportunities, a stagnant economy and heightened global competition. And more students from places and circumstances where the Ivy League once seemed too remote are competing for all they're worth.
"You have to do more than be a good student," said Jennifer Hong, 15, of Dunwoody, Ga., who has joined the Future Business Leaders of America even though she intends to be a pediatrician.
The Ivy-or-bust mentality is evolving as a defining issue from earliest childhood. It haunts the games students play, the vacations plans they make, relationships with parents and friends and even the sports they choose.
Harvard University was deluged with a record 18,000 applications for the 1,600 places for men and women entering in 1995, up 50 percent from 12,000 applications in 1990. The University of Pennsylvania was similarly swamped with 15,000 applications this year, compared with 10,600 five years ago. Applications to Princeton University have also climbed, from 12,600 in 1990 to 14,200 for 1995. All three universities say the odds have never been tougher.
The amount of down time in these youngsters' lives is disappearing rapidly. The two largest national tutoring services that focus on S.A.T. preparation, Kaplan Educational Centers and the Princeton Review, each enrolled more than 30,000 student in their after-school classes in 1994, almost double the number of five years earlier. In the last five years, Kaplan has added 1,000 satellite classrooms to its 150 learning centers as demand has grown from Tullahoma, Tenn., to Minot, N.D., said Jonathan Grayer, the president of Kaplan.
And the demand for their services is coming at an ever-earlier age. Three years ago, the Princeton Review introduced a junior level, for grades 7 through 10. More than 5,000 now attend.
"Parents are asking us for S.A.T. prep even for our eighth and ninth graders," said Marcia Evans, president of Academic Study Associates in Armonk, N.Y., a company that provides summer enrichment programs at five universities. Duke University, a pioneer in summer programs for the mathematically gifted, today serves about 6,000 children a year.
College expectations are even redefining the hot sports. "You see kids giving up baseball for lacrosse," said an 18-year-old from Wilton High School in Wilton, Conn., who asked that her name not be used. Lacrosse, she says, often leads to big Ivy League scholarships. Crew is another popular choice, because it is seen as an easy way to stand out.
The pressure to succeed is also touching the lives of the very youngest children. Educational toys have become the fastest-growing category in the industry, according to Playthings magazine, a monthly trade journal. Stores like Egghead Software have entire shelves of products for toddlers.
The emphasis on beating the competition is so profound even in kindergarten and first grade that children think they must win all the prizes "just to feel 'good enough,' " said Maria Alba-Fisch, a child psychologist in Mount Kisco, N.Y.
The regular rankings of colleges by U.S. News and World Report and Money magazine, plus "The Bell Curve," the recently best-selling book by Richard J. Herrnstein and Charles Murray (Free Press, 1994) about America's intellectual elite, have contributed to the angst. So has the latest boomlet in the birthrate, called the baby boom echo, plus the rising cost of an undergraduate degree, which can now top $100,000.
Educational counselors have found that parents are reluctant to spend a huge sum on a college without a prestigious name. But the pressure can "drive a wedge" between parents and their children when it starts too soon, because the children can't always see the point, warned John Katzman, president of the Princeton Review.
Antoinette Saunders, a clinical psychologist working with children in Wilmette, Ill., warned of increased rebellion and resentment and even drug or alcohol abuse among less able children.
Many parents say it is a no-win situation. "It's the way we give our children the very best chance for their future," said Toni Peebler, a Manhattan mother of a 14-year-old son. "The child might be more relaxed if the pressure were off. But try being the only boy in a class of 20 who didn't get into the Harvards of the world. It's not only their future but their self-worth that's in jeopardy."
Others argue that a busy schedule is healthy. "Being involved in a multitude of activities has helped Karen find her strengths," said Ilene Sumberg, in Norwalk. But what about the stress? "When we think she's going into overload, we talk about where she can pull back," Mrs. Sumberg said. "It's a way to learn priorities." Karen says she is learning to become an efficient time manager, adding that it helps her enjoy her tight schedule rather than resent it.
Anwar Bennett, 15, of Decatur, Ga., who has set his sights on either Cornell or Vanderbilt, lets out the tension by playing soccer or golf. Yet, he sounded melancholy as he recalled the fun of being the "class clown," a role he relished until ninth grade, when his parents sent him to parochial school and private after-school tutoring. Things had to change, he said, because "as a black youth, you're afraid you'll get even less opportunities if you don't get into a good school."
The cutthroat college competition can warp friendships. "If you get a really good grade in math, then everyone else in the class gets mad at you," a student observed at a focus group in Wilton on college pressures. Betsy Kahan, a clinical psychologist in Wilton and a former consultant to the town's public schools, said it was particularly tough for a successful girl to develop relationships with boys who viewed her as a competitor.
To keep friends and appearances, "kids lie to each other about their grades and their scores," said Amanda Rose, executive director of Stanford Coaching, a Manhattan service that provides private tutors. Typically, she said, a child will call a friend to find out how much time has been spent studying for a test. The friend, who may have been working for six hours, may say, "Only half an hour," to prevent the other from doing as well. Lisa Jacobson, president of the company, knows youngsters who do "all nighters" with their books and coffee.
The top colleges insist that they're not to blame and that the panic emanates from rising applications and wider economic concerns. "We're hearing it's statistically much harder to get into the right kindergarten in some cities," said William Fitzsimmons, Harvard's dean of admissions.
Yet, the pressure to excel may also be coming from a change in attitude toward financial aid by the colleges. At a conference on higher education held last month at Spelman College in Atlanta, one paper reported a nationwide shift toward basing financial aid on merit. "So extra-curricular activities have to be even more impressive," said Alison R. Bernstein, director of the educational and cultural program at the Ford Foundation and a former associate dean of the faculty at Princeton. And this, she added, will further separate the rich and poor. "You will lose a lot of kids whose parents can't give them that extra leg up."
Claire Gaudiani, president of Connecticut College, says that ivy has crowded people's vision too much. "Ninety-eight percent of our 1993 graduates have gone on to graduate school or are employed," she said. "The Fortune 500 companies actively recruit on campus, and we see starting salaries in the $40,000-to-50,000 range."
But Ms. Jacobson said, "You can say to a child, 'Don't worry, it doesn't matter where you go,' but with all the peer pressure and hype, the kids won't believe you."
She urges parents to remove themselves from the "me too" trap and to show a child, through specific examples, that many roads lead to success in life. "Otherwise," Ms. Jacobson said, "those children may never know the sheer joy of just being a kid."
