HEADLINE: House Proud

BYLINE: By Tracie Rozhon 

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass.

 BODY:
ONE side of the two-family house has a new kitchen with a neon banana hanging over a grand piano. The other side also has a new kitchen -- with a sedate collection of Staffordshire plates over an oak table.
David B. Waters and Don Sturdy live side by side. They met 13 years ago, were housemates for five years and then drifted apart -- until one needed a partner to buy a house. Now, they live together again -- in mirror-image half houses with completely different decor. 
 With the aid of an architect friend, Mr. Sturdy and Mr. Waters enlarged what one of them called a "dark and dreary doll's house" and created two distinct maisonettes that can either be closed off -- they once had two dinner parties simmering on the same night -- or combined into a single drawing room suitable for a raucous party for 40.
Their plan called for a new addition off the back that created twin living rooms with back-to-back corner fireplaces. They also built a cantilevered, double-story bay window on each side. They remodeled extensively, creating the two kitchens and two new bathrooms with skylights, and they redid the second-story bedrooms and installed new plumbing and wiring -- all for $80,000.
They were able to pull off this financial sleight of hand because their friend, Adam Schoenhardt, became their architect, drawing up plans with the skill of a budget-minded Houdini. They also did all the scouting for salvaged building materials, appliances and furnishings. Mr. Sturdy -- the one with the neon banana -- dug up an Art Deco bar in a Boston warehouse, sunk a sink into it and put it in his bathroom. Some of their best bargains came from discount catalogues and hardware chain stores.
Mr. Waters, who runs the Hasty Pudding Club's restaurant at Harvard, was tired of paying $1,000 in rent every month. But as his search for a reasonably priced house propelled him deeper into the suburbs, he realized he couldn't afford the house he wanted -- where he wanted it -- unless he found a partner.
He thought of Mr. Sturdy almost immediately. Mr. Sturdy, a singer and music manager who is an assistant treasurer for the Wang Center for the Performing Arts, was amenable to buying a place with Mr. Waters but insisted on some conditions he thought would be hard to fulfill. "I would only do it if we got a side-by-side duplex," he said. "I like symmetry, and how would we ever decide who would get the upstairs and who would get the downstairs if we did it the other way?"
As it turns out, side-by-side houses are indeed rare; Mr. Waters estimates that there may be only 20 in all of Boston. He was living on Chilton Street when he saw a FOR SALE sign go up on a dilapidated two-family home a block away. It was in a pocket of six identical houses built in the 1920's for workers at a nearby brick factory.
"Stuff in the neighborhood was all $300,000 to $400,000," Mr. Waters said. When he learned that the asking price for the house on Concord Avenue was $230,000, he said he almost fell over. But the reason for the price soon became obvious.
"It never had any work done to it," Mr. Sturdy said.
"The house had a certain amount of charm, but it was ridiculously small and humble," Mr. Waters added. The two men bargained and later signed a contract for $204,000.
"By dumb luck, we hit the very bottom of the mortgage market," Mr. Waters said. They got a mortgage fixed at 6 percent for 10 years, with no points and 10 percent down. Closing costs were held to $1,500.
Before the bank gave the two men a definite commitment, it demanded a signed contract with a building contractor; to pick a contractor, the two men had to have plans to show. Mr. Schoenhardt saw only half the house for 10 minutes, discussed the concepts briefly with the new owners and delivered the plans the next day.
"We went, 'Uh, huh,' and that was that -- he got it exactly right," Mr. Waters recalled.
By "pinching the envelope," as Mr. Schoenhardt said, and opening up every possible space to new windows, he was able to reconstruct a 1,900-square-foot house into one of 2,500 square feet that feels as spacious as one of 3,500 square feet.
Besides adding the new 28-by-15- foot space at the back of the house and the 9-by-2.5 foot, double-story bay windows, the architect also opened up the kitchens, which lead to the new sunken living rooms. Instead of spending $8,000 for twin masonry fireplaces, the owners opted for metal fireplaces, which cost $2,000.
The design of the two halves is identical; the decor is not. Mr. Waters categorizes his taste as "more like a room in an Edwardian club." Mr. Sturdy gives new meaning to the word "eclectic": "My artwork is 80's and 90's and my furniture is 20's and 30's," he said.
The two kitchens are an interesting case study in tastes -- and pocketbooks. Mr. Sturdy, 39, who is also a part-time chef, chose a $2,000 Viking restaurant stove and pickled maple cabinetwork ($3,800). To economize, Mr. Waters chose to cover his counters with laminate and trim them with cherry wood. He did without upper cabinets, saving $1,200.
"I'm an old New Englander," the 35-year-old restaurateur said. "If I paid full price, I paid too much. I found a church podium at a junkyard in Maine -- and turned it into a dictionary stand."

HEADLINE: International Briefs;
Profit at Deutsche Bank Declined 39% in 1994

 BODY:
Deutsche Bank A.G., Germany's largest bank, said yesterday that bond trading losses slashed its profit by 39 percent, to 1.36 billion marks ($980 million), in 1994. The managing board chairman, Hilmar Kopper, said profit would rebound amid confidence that 1995 would be a better year for financial markets.
Operating profit, which is before taxes but after provisions for bad loans, fell 23.1 percent, to 4.05 billion marks ($2.92 billion). 
 In a prelude to grim trading reports expected from other German banks, Mr. Kopper said the profit slump was essentially a product of a global bond-market collapse that started after the Federal Reserve of the United States raised interest rates in February 1994. (Bloomberg Business News)

HEADLINE: Tale of Evasion Of Ban on Aid For Guatemala

BYLINE: By TIM WEINER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The Bush Administration, in a show of public anger over the killing of an American, cut off military aid to Guatemala in 1990 but secretly allowed the Central Intelligence Agency to send millions of dollars to the military Government to make up for the loss, members of the Bush and Clinton Administrations said today.
The payments apparently continued into the Clinton Administration but it could not be learned if they are still being made. 
 About $5 million to $7 million flowed annually from the C.I.A. through the agency's Guatemala station to the military, the officials said.
The delivery of funds started after the White House formally halted military aid approved by Congress, a public display of frustration at the Guatemalan military's failure to assist in the investigation of the killing of the American, Michael DeVine, the officials said. Mr. DeVine, an innkeeper in the northern forests, was killed by Guatemalan soldiers in June 1990.
Two Bush Administration officials familiar with the payments, who insisted on not being identified, said the money did not buy weapons but went to mollify the Guatemalan military and to pay C.I.A. sources.
"The agency ran this program on the basis that it was the key to keeping U.S. policymakers informed about what was going on within the Guatemalan Government and the Guatemalan military," one official said. But he noted that it did not buy crucial information about the death of Mr. DeVine.
The apparent contradiction between the public policy and the approval of secret funds raises the question of how deeply committed the White House and the C.I.A. were to investigating Mr. DeVine's death.
Representative Robert G. Torricelli, a New Jersey Democrat who is a member of the House Intelligence Committee, has linked a C.I.A. agent, Col. Julio Roberto Alpirez of the Guatemalan Army, to the killing of Mr. DeVine and to the death of a guerrilla leader who was married to an American lawyer.
The White House, acting for the first time in the matter, has begun an inquiry into the killings, and said late tonight that the National Security Agency, the electronic-eavesdropping branch of United States intelligence, had secured an area where "document shredding is allegedly occurring." The White House gave no further details.
The White House also said it had ordered all relevant agencies to preserve documents and had instructed the Justice Department to begin a formal investigation.
President Clinton also plans to ask the Intelligence Oversight Board, senior officials charged with reviewing United States intelligence activities, to review the case of Colonel Alpirez.
Within the Bush Administration, some officials said, there were arguments over whether the Administration was being hypocritical by sending contradictory messages to the Guatemalan military.
"Guatemalan military officers told us: 'You guys are crazy. You shut us down in public, and then you give us stuff through the back door, and everyone in the military knows it,' " one Bush Administration official said.
The C.I.A.'s payments to the Guatemalan military apparently fell under the veil of a "liaison" relationship with a foreign intelligence service, the Bush Administration official said. The details of such relationships are rarely shared with Congressional oversight committees, though it is not known whether Congress was informed in this instance.
The Bush Administration's public decision to withhold military aid to Guatemala in 1990 came as its Ambassador, Thomas F. Stroock, was protesting increasing rights abuses in Guatemala and the inability or unwillingness of political leaders there to assert control the army.
Washington cut economic aid to Guatemala to $113.4 million in 1990, from $141.3 million in 1989; military aid fell to $3.3 million, from $7 million. In 1990 the State Department also placed Guatemala on its list of major drug-trafficking countries.
Mr. DeVine was kidnapped and nearly beheaded by members of the Guatemalan military in June 1990, and left by the roadside near his inn in Poptun, a village in the Guatemalan jungle. His body was discovered near a Guatemalan Army Special Forces training camp run by Colonel Alpirez, the officer accused by Representative Torricelli of complicity in the murder. At the time, the colonel was on the payroll of the C.I.A., Mr. Torricelli says.
Ambassador Stroock said in an interview that he dispatched his military attache, Col. Allen C. Cornell, to interview Colonel Alpirez at his jungle base but that the attache was rebuffed.
Mr. Stroock said he went to see the C.I.A. station chief after Colonel Cornell reported the incident.
"I looked him in the eye and I said, 'Are any of your 'assets' involved in this?' " the former Ambassador said. "And he said no. And I believed him. I had absolutely no idea that Alpirez was a C.I.A. asset."
But Mr. Stroock said that after Colonel Alpirez refused to cooperate, he told senior officials at the American Embassy that they were not to help the colonel again.
Colonel Alpirez received at least $60,000 from the C.I.A. between the time of the killing and the end of his financial relationship with the C.I.A. in the summer of 1992, Administration officials have said.
In December, after Ambassador Stroock had tried and failed to persuade the Guatemalan Government and military to find the officers responsible for the death of Mr. DeVine, the Bush Administration halted military aid to Guatemala.
In addition to $3.3 million appropriated that year, millions more in the pipeline were withdrawn, said a third former high-ranking State Department official who served in the Bush and Clinton Administrations.
"The DeVine case was a big issue with us," he said. "It was the central issue between the United States and Guatemala, and we were breaking their arms and legs over DeVine. We were very angry."
This official said he was certain the C.I.A. did not inform the State Department and the National Security Council that Colonel Alpirez was its paid agent, even after the State Department received intelligence reports in 1991 that the colonel was implicated in the DeVine case.
"This raises a very serious question," he said. "The C.I.A. absolutely knew that the U.S. Government at the highest levels was confronting the Guatemalan Government and insisting they investigate. They knew it was a central issue in U.S.-Guatemalan relations. For them not to have disclosed that to State and to N.S.C. is a serious issue."

CORRECTION-DATE: March 31, 1995, Friday

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, an article yesterday about money transferred secretly by the C.I.A. to Guatemala in the early 1990's referred imprecisely to the recipients. They were military intelligence officials, not a military government.

HEADLINE: Electricity Rates Are Frozen For Con Ed and Lilco for Year

 BODY:
Electricity rates will be frozen this year for customers of Consolidated Edison and the Long Island Lighting Company under rate plans approved yesterday by the State Public Service Commission.
The commission approved a three-year rate plan for Con Edison that involves no increase for the first year, beginning April 1. 
 The plan, on which the utility and the commission's staff had reached agreement in January, allows the company to raise rates in the second and third years to cover rises in some of its costs, including contracts with independent power producers, property taxes and interest rates.
Con Edison had initially sought a 3.6 percent rate increase. The commission's staff had proposed a rate cut.
In contrast, Lilco had proposed no increase in its base rates for two years and a 4.3 percent increase in 1997.
The commission acted only on the first year of the Lilco plan, approving the rate freeze with some modifications. But it said that if Lilco could match the cost-cutting programs already adopted by other companies, it would not need rate increases for years.
A Lilco spokeswoman, Elaine Davis, said the rate decision was "just what we expected." She said the company had already cut costs and reduced its work force of about 600,000 by one-tenth in the last several years.
But the commission chairman, Harold A. Jerry Jr., said the company had not done enough. "I think they can do more and do better," he said.
Many on Long Island had hoped the commission would impose a rate cut. Long Island officials and business leaders have long said that high electricity rates have hindered economic growth and forced businesses to leave.
"The decision is a disappointment," said Richard M. Kessel, the chairman of the Long Island Power Authority. "Basically, the rates Lilco wanted were approved. If people were looking for rate relief, they are going to be disappointed. I would call today's decision a nonevent."
The authority, a Lilco watchdog created by a 1986 state law, had called for a 2.6 percent rate decrease.
The decisions yesterday were the first by the commission since Gov. George E. Pataki took office. Mr. Pataki named Mr. Jerry as chairman to succeed Peter A. Bradford, who resigned. He also appointed John O'Mara to a commission seat.
Commission officials said that Lilco's rate freeze for 1995 was attained by reducing the amounts Lilco will be required to spend for conservation programs, research and development and customer service.
Lilco spent more than $30 million for conservation programs in 1994. It will be required to spend about $12 million under the rate plan approved yesterday.
Mr. Jerry and other commissioners said the cost reduction strengthened the company, but they expressed concern about Lilco's high debt and any ill effects the rate decision might have on the company's bond rating.
Richard W. Cortright Jr., a utility credit analyst at Standard & Poor's Rating Group in Manhattan, said he expected no immediate impact on the company's bond rating.

CORRECTION-DATE: March 31, 1995, Friday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about a plan to freeze rates charged by the Long Island Lighting Company misstated the number of people it employs. It is 6,000, not 600,000.

HEADLINE: Maj. Gen. John M. Dunn, 69, Military Aide to Vice Presidents

BYLINE: By J. MICHAEL ELLIOTT 

 BODY:
John Michael Dunn, a veteran of three wars who rose to become a major general and a military aide to two Vice Presidents, died on Friday at his home in Arlington, Va. He was 69.
The cause was complications from pulmonary fibrosis, said his son Alan. 
 At the time of his death, General Dunn was president of the Can Manufacturers Institute, a national trade association, a position he had held since 1976.
He was on the staff of Vice President Spiro T. Agnew in the second term of President Richard M. Nixon. After Mr. Agnew's resignation in the fall of 1973, General Dunn stayed on to serve his successor, Gerald R. Ford.
He began his stint on the Vice-Presidential staff as a military assistant and rose to become an assistant in charge of foreign and economic affairs and finally chief of staff. He handled relations with the National Security Council, the Office of Management and Budget, the Council of Economic Advisers and the Office of Science and Technology.
He stayed on the Vice-Presidential staff until early 1974, when he retired from the military and became the principal assistant Secretary of Commerce for domestic and international business. Later that year, he became executive director of the Council on International Economic Policy, a Presidential advisory group. He was also a liaison between the White House and Congress on economic affairs.
Born in Los Angeles and reared in Natick, Mass., he needed his mother's permission to enlist in the Army at age 17. After serving in World War II, he entered Harvard University, graduating in 1949. He returned to the Army as an officer and fought in the Korean War.
During his career, he received the Distinguished Service Medal, the Legion of Merit, Silver and Bronze Stars, the Purple Heart with two oak-leaf clusters and the Commendation Medal with oak-leaf cluster, among others.
From 1963 until 1965, he was personal assistant to Ambassador Henry Cabot Lodge in South Vietnam and executive secretary at the American Embassy in Saigon, now Ho Chi Minh City.
He earned master's degrees in public affairs and international affairs and a doctorate in political science, all from Princeton University. He was also a graduate of the Command and General Staff College and the National War College.
Besides his son Alan, of Alexandria, Va., he is survived by his wife of 44 years, Mary Frances Hobbs Dunn; another son, Neal, of Panama City, Fla., and three grandchildren.

TYPE: Obituary (Obit); Biography

HEADLINE: New Jobless Claims Declined Last Week

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
The number of workers filing new claims for unemployment benefits fell last week for the first time in a month, the Labor Department said today. Analysts said the decline suggested that moderate job growth was continuing despite a slower economy.
First-time applications for unemployment insurance totaled a seasonally adjusted 338,000, down 10,000 from the week that ended March 18. 
 It was the first decline since claims fell by 12,000 in the week that ended Feb. 25. Claims rose during the next three weeks, the first three-week string of increases since last April.
In fact, the report said claims during the week that ended March 18 were higher than the initial Government estimate of 346,000.
Despite the decline last week, the closely watched four-week moving average of weekly jobless claims continued to rise. The average was 341,750, up from 340,250 in the period that ended March 18 and the highest since 347,750 in the period that ended last July 30.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

U.S. Stock, Bond Markets Sluggish After Rate Cut
 Although the dollar surged after the Bundesbank unexpectedly cut German interest rates, the move was shrugged off by stock and bond markets in the United States. 
 Stocks closed mixed, with the Dow industrials rising 11.76 points, to 4,172.56, but the S.& P. 500 fell almost a point. Prices of 30-year Treasury bonds also declined. D6.
 By shaving its discount rate, the Bundesbank has improved the prospects for equity markets across Europe during the rest of this year. Market Place. D6.
 
Trump Files to Sell Casino Company Stock
 Donald J. Trump filed an initial public offering with the S.E.C. to sell 10 million shares of Trump Hotels and Casino Resorts to the public for $14 to $16 a share. Mr. Trump hopes the stock offering and a separate offering of bonds will raise $245 million to $295 million. D2.
 
Insurer Settles Bias Complaint
 American Family Mutual has agreed to pay $14.5 million to settle a lawsuit accusing it of discrimination against black people. It is the most costly insurance company settlement ever for charges of discrimination. D16.
 
Wellpoint Near Health Systems Deal
 Wellpoint Health Networks, after rejecting an enhanced bid by Blue Shield of California, prepared to sign an agreement to acquire Health Systems International in a $1.8 billion stock swap deal. D6.
 
Luxottica Aims at U.S. Shoe Board
 Luxottica Group of Italy stepped up its efforts to acquire U.S. Shoe, urging the retailer's shareholders to set up a special meeting to replace the board with a slate of its own. D2.
 
Lobbyists Having More Say
 With the Republican majority in Congress, industry lobbyists have greater access to the legislative process, helping lawmakers in drafting bills. Republicans are unapologetic. A G.O.P. lawmaker said, "Of course we should reach out to those with real-life experiences who have to put their money where our mandates are." A1.
 
Circa Stock Soars on Merger
 Watson Pharmaceuticals has agreed to acquire Circa Pharmaceuticals in a $600 million deal. Circa shares surged 34 percent. D6.
 
Phone Companies Protest Fee Cut
 The F.C.C. drew protests from regional and local phone companies, ordering them, as expected, to cut about $1 billion from the fees they charge the likes of AT&T, MCI and Sprint to connect with local customers. Bell Atlantic said it would challenge the order in court. D3.
 
Pension Gap Is Narrowing
 The Pension Benefit Guaranty Corporation said the worst is past in the gap between promised benefits and the money set aside to pay them at companies with inadequate pension plans. The Federal agency added that the shortage, now $71 billion, should shrink 70 percent over the next 15 years. D2.
 
Hachette Buys Mirabella Magazine
 Mirabella magazine has been acquired by Hachette Filipacchi Magazines and will be published six times a year. D5.
 
Lowe Lands Sony Account
 Sony Electronics named Lowe & Partners/ SMS as agency of record on its account, with billings estimated at $50 million. Most of the account had been handled since May 1991 by Leo Burnett. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. D5.
Move to Block Mexican Aid Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato and nine other Republican Senators set off a furor by proposing an amendment that would block the United States loan package to Mexico. A25.

HEADLINE: Stocks Generally Shrug Off The Bundesbank's Action

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
Germany's surprise interest-rate cut electrified the international currency markets yesterday and sent the dollar higher, but stock traders appeared to take little notice.
Indeed, against most expectations, the stock market closed mixed but mostly down yesterday. 
 After the Bundesbank, Germany's central bank, announced its rate cut, the dollar surged more than 2 percent against the German mark to 1.4083 marks in New York, theoretically giving foreign investors an incentive to buy American stocks and bonds. It did not work out that way.
True, the Dow Jones industrial average rose 11.76 points, to a record 4,172.56, setting its 10th record closing high this month. But the broader Standard & Poor's index of 500 companies fell 0.90 point, to 502.22, and the smaller-company Nasdaq composite index fell 2.30 points, to 816.86.
Prices for bonds also dropped. The yield, which moves in the opposite direction of the price, on the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond rose to 7.41 percent from 7.37 percent.
"What the bond market and maybe the broader stock market are saying is that the rise in the dollar is not going to last long," said David De Rosa, a director of foreign exchange at Swiss Bank.
Emotion may be at work as well. "The market is very nervous at these price levels," said Bill Allyn, director of listed equity trading at Jeffries & Company, referring to a Dow nearing the 4,200-point level.
Because today is the end of the first quarter, both stock and bond markets may experience more volatility than usual, as portfolio managers use their last day to buy and sell assets to meet performance targets.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was heavier than normal with nearly 363 million shares changing hands, about 10 percent more than this year's daily average of 330 million shares.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index fell 0.01 point, to 271.83, with advancing stocks outpacing decliners 1,310 to 825. The American Stock Exchange market value index rose 2.66 points, to 465.01.
Among the biggest gainers were shares of Circa Pharmaceuticals Inc., a drug maker, which rose 6, to 23 3/4, after Watson Pharmaceuticals Inc. agreed to buy the company.
Shares of the James River Corporation rose 3, to 26 3/8, after the forest products company said it would spin off a subsidiary. Caterpillar Inc., the heavy equipment maker and favorite of institutional investors, rose 2 1/2, to 56 3/8 a share.
Among the biggest decliners were Exx Inc., a toy maker. Its class A shares fell 5 1/8, to 19 3/4, on the American Stock Exchange and its class B shares fell 5 1/4, to 18 3/4, despite a large rise in fourth-quarter profits.
The Chipcom Corporation, a computer-equipment maker, fell 4 1/4, to 38 a share. As with other high-technology stocks, some traders believe Chipcom's share price is too high.
The most active stock, at 10.2 million shares, was that of the Intel Corporation, which fell 111/16 , to 853/8 .
One reason that some expected yesterday's sharp rise by the dollar to be temporary is that it was accelerated by short covering. Traders who had expected the dollar's value to fall had shorted it -- that is, borrowed dollars, then sold them hoping to buy replacement dollars at a lower price and make a profit on the difference. When the dollar rose instead, such traders had to buy dollars to stem potential losses.

HEADLINE: Insurer to Settle Milwaukee Bias Lawsuit

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT 

 BODY:
The American Family Mutual Insurance Company of Madison, Wis., has agreed to pay $14.5 million to settle a lawsuit accusing it of discrimination against black people in Milwaukee.
The settlement, announced yesterday by the company, the National Association for the Advancement of Colored People, the American Civil Liberties Union and the United States Justice Department, was the most costly insurance-company settlement ever for discrimination charges. It comes as the Department of Housing and Urban Development is preparing rules to make clear that the Federal Housing Act prohibits discrimination in the sale of homeowners insurance. 
 As consumer groups have accused insurers of discriminating in recent years, insurance companies have variously denied that discrimination is a problem and have asserted that higher prices and less attractive products offered in low-income areas reflect the higher risks of doing business there.
In the American Family case, the Justice Department said it had written and tape-recorded evidence of company managers who told agents to quit writing policies for blacks. But it commended the company yesterday for turning "a new page in its history and agreeing to take steps to provide insurance to the African American community of Milwaukee."
Attorney General Janet Reno said in a statement: "Risk discrimination is permitted. Race discrimination is not."
A study issued late last year by the National Association of Insurance Commissioners found that "property and liability insurance is more expensive and difficult to obtain" in inner-city areas. While insurers may not be intentionally discriminating, the study said, "the fact that underserved areas tend to have high minority populations leads to charges of racial as well as geographic discrimination."
The case against American Family, which was not proved in court, was based on records of policies and internal documents that A.C.L.U. lawyers also said made clear that agents were discouraged from selling to blacks.
As part of the settlement, American Family, the country's 11th-largest property and liability insurer, recently began offering policies that promise to pay the cost of replacing a damaged building, even if its market value is as little as 40 percent of the insured replacement cost. Many companies do not make replacement-cost policies available in cities where those costs can be far higher than a building's market value.
Consequently, homeowners typically buy less attractive policies that promise to pay the cost of repairs, reduced by depreciation. Because those policies often fail to cover the full cost of repairs, they can result in abandonment of damaged buildings.
The $14.5 million is to be divided among black homeowners in Milwaukee's inner city. Uses of the money will include interest-rate subsidies on mortgages and home repair loans, emergency home repairs, a counseling program for homeowners and cash payments to people who were denied insurance or deterred from seeking it.
Rick Fetherston, a spokesman for American Family, noted that the allegations were based on events in the 1980's, and said the company had recently built a strong record for selling insurance in the seven ZIP codes in Milwaukee with large black populations. In those areas, he said, the company is the leading insurer, with 37 percent of the market.

HEADLINE: Hillary Clinton Talks to Poor Working Women in India

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: AHMADABAD, India, March 31

 BODY:
In a sweltering tent, Hillary Rodham Clinton spoke today with vegetable vendors, embroidery workers and other poor women who said they were no longer afraid to organize for better treatment in India's marketplace and society.
Mrs. Clinton's visit to the Self-Employed Women's Association could well be the high point of her 12-day tour of South Asia, a trip centered on promoting better health, education and opportunity for women and girls throughout the world. 
 "I will never forget what I have seen and heard and learned here today," Mrs. Clinton told a group of 200 women. "I know how this kind of solidarity and effort can change lives."
The association is a union of 143,000 women formed in 1972 to help those in the largely invisible "informal" economy of agricultural workers, vendors and other self-employed women. It was organized by Ela Bhatt, 62, a follower of Mohandas K. Gandhi who wanted to ensure better treatment for the often oppressed, illiterate women who struggle to provide for their families.
From a modest beginning, the association has grown into the country's leading women's organization. It set up a bank that offers credit to women who otherwise could not get loans, organizes cooperatives to build up business and create jobs, offers skills training and health insurance and provides legal aid.
Mrs. Clinton, who first met Mrs. Bhatt at the White House last year, said the United States could learn from the association's example on expanding access to credit and other matters, and praised the group's efforts to give women "a better life without fear."
Earlier today, Mrs. Clinton laid a wreath at a monument to Gandhi and toured the Ahmadabad retreat where he lived from 1918 to 1930. She also toured the Taj Mahal with her 15-year-old daughter, Chelsea. The 17th-century tomb was built by the Mogul Emperor Shah Jahan in memory of his wife, Mumtaz Mahal, who died giving birth to their 14th child.
On Friday Mrs. Clinton is to fly to Nepal. She has already visited Pakistan and is to visit Bangladesh and Sri Lanka.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Surges Following Move By Germany to Lower Its Rates

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar surged against leading currencies yesterday, registering its biggest one-day gain against the mark in seven months, after the Bundesbank unexpectedly cut German interest rates.
The move by the German central bank was seen as an effort to slow the recent rapid ascent of the mark, which had surged to record highs recently against many other European currencies. Many dealers sold dollars on Wednesday in anticipation that the Bundesbank would leave interest rates unchanged because of relatively strong economic growth in the country and concerns over wage increases in recent labor settlements. Lower German interest rates would make mark-denominated deposits and assets less attractive to investors and the dollar more attractive, in comparison. 
 "The Bundesbank caught the market off guard, and we had an immediate bounce by the dollar," said Varick Martin, manager of foreign exchange at the Manufacturers and Traders Trust Company.
The dollar rose as high as 1.4205 marks after the increase by the Bundesbank, before settling in New York at 1.4083 marks, up from 1.3825 marks on Wednesday. The dollar also rose to 1.1680 Swiss francs from 1.1425 francs. The British pound fell to $1.6035 from $1.6118.
The Bundesbank's move "smacks very much as being related to foreign exchange concerns," said Tom Hoge, a currency trader at the Bank of New York. "They were playing it for a maximum surprise and foreign exchange effect."
While the Federal Reserve disappointed the foreign exchange markets by leaving interest rates unchanged on Tuesday, dealers yesterday were looking next to relief from Japan. They said it was increasingly likely that the Bank of Japan would follow the Bundesbank's lead and cut its discount rate to try to halt the soaring yen.
In Tokyo early today, the Bank of Japan said it was encouraging declines in some short-term money market rates, although it did not cut its discount rate.
The dollar rose a 13-day high of 90.14 yen before settling in New York at 89.55 yen, up from 88.43 yen.
Though the dollar rose yesterday, many currency dealers said they expected it to resume the decline that took it to post-World War II lows against the mark, yen and Swiss franc this month. Indeed, the dollar is still down 9 percent against the mark since the beginning of 1995.
"It's crucial for the dollar to hold these gains," said Karl Halligan, vice president for strategic trading at CIC Bank New York. "If it doesn't, the dollar's got bigger problems than people thought. The dollar needs to go a lot higher to convince people that it has bottomed."

HEADLINE: Market Place;
The German rate cut gives a lift to European stock markets.

BYLINE: By Paul Lewis 

 BODY:
BY shaving its key interest rates yesterday at a time many feared it might do the reverse, the German Bundesbank has given a lift to German stocks and other European equity markets.
Earlier this week, the German mark's renewed strength against the dollar and leading European currencies had pushed the Frankfurt stock market's 30-stock DAX index down to an 18-month low of 1,910.96. This meant that in March alone it had lost some 9 percent of its value, wiping out much of the spectacular gain it posted during 1992 and 1993 when the smell of Germany's coming recovery sent it soaring from 1,450 to a high of 2,271. 
 The Bundesbank announced its rate decision only after official trading was over in Frankfurt. But in after-hours trading the index rose 30.91 points to end at 1,949.76, after briefly touching 1,957.50. Other European stock markets rallied by 1 percent or more.
"We consider Frankfurt a buy opportunity," said Catherine Ayers-Rigby, an expert on Germany at Ceros Portfolio Management in Fairfax, Va.
While she had not expected the Bundesbank to cut interest rates until later this year, she said that yesterday's rate cut and the weaker mark now "mean company earnings are less endangered than they seemed when the mark was overshooting." She said she favored the German software company S.A.P. and the auto maker BMW.
The German rate reduction will also be especially welcome in France, where the Paris stock market rose more than 2 percent on the news. Lower German rates make it easier for Prime Minister Edouard Balladur to maintain his strong franc policy -- "le franc fort" -- in the run up to the May presidential election at a time when he is trailing fellow conservative and rival, Jacques Chirac, in the polls.
The German central bank also lifted some of the gloom settling in over Europe's economic prospects after eight countries, including Britain, France, Italy and Spain, raised interest rates to protect their currencies against speculative storms unleashed by the rise of Germany's mark.
The German cut, which generally caught analysts by surprise, was widely seen as a signal that the Bundesbank now feared that the strong mark, combined with five consecutive monthly declines in money supply growth, could seriously hurt the country's export-based economy in the second half of this year. The damage would come at a time when German companies are already struggling to absorb the recent round of high wage settlements for German workers.
In the past, the Bundesbank has usually welcomed an appreciating mark as an inflation depressant. And some analysts had even expected the central bank to raise rates this year to offset the inflationary impact of the latest pay round.
"Last year's mark appreciation did not damage German export competitiveness as companies offset the effects of a stronger currency with corporate restructuring," said Kathleen Stephansen, an economist with Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette.
"But this year's 10.7 percent appreciation against the dollar was too strong for corporations to sustain," she added. "The recent decline in the German stock index is a telling sign."
Other analysts speculated that the Bundesbank move was also intended to allay growing fears in Europe that the mark's new-found strength and the tensions this has created in currency markets were threatening the Continent's recovery prospects this year and next.
"In the last few days the mark has been rising against all the main European currencies, creating pressure for higher interest rates," said Lisa S. Kaess of Geoffrey Bell & Company, a New York investment advisory firm. "This is the Bundesbank doing its bit for Europe."
In a report to clients earlier this month, Salomon Brothers' chief European analyst, Kermit L. Schoenholtz, pointed out that recent foreign exchange turbulence had forced Italy, Britain, Sweden, France, Belgium, Denmark, Spain and Ireland to raise interest rate in recent months to defend their currencies. "If these increases last beyond midyear we will get an economic slowdown," he warned.
The Bundesbank move may also relieve a bit of the tension in the world's financial markets that has followed the bungled currency devaluation by Mexico at the end of last year.
The immediate impact of that devaluation was to destabilize the industrial world's emerging stock markets. But a secondary impact was the flight into "safe haven" currencies, like the mark, which helped to send the American dollar into a worrisome downward spiral.

HEADLINE: G.O.P. Halts Senate Budget Bill by Move on Mexico Aid

BYLINE: By JERRY GRAY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato and a band of fellow Republicans stunned the Clinton Administration today and stalled a vote on a Senate budget-cutting bill by introducing an amendment aimed at blocking the American loan package to Mexico.
The surprise amendment left both Senate Democrats and the Administration momentarily shocked and fearful that even a discussion of cutting off aid could imperil the Mexico rescue plan. And it sent the Republican leadership scrambling to avoid a threatened Democratic filibuster that would delay if not kill any chance of swift passage of the bill, which would cut $13.5 billion out of spending in the current fiscal year. 
 Late in the afternoon, about three hours after he set off the furor, Senator D'Amato, a New York Republican, agreed to delay consideration of his amendment until the end of the debate on the budget adjustments, known as rescissions, or to introduce it again -- as early as next Monday -- as a separate bill.
The amendment offered by Mr. D'Amato and nine other Republican Senators would require Congressional approval of annual assistance to any foreign country exceeding $5 billion from the exchange stabilization fund. The Administration tapped that fund for $20 billion to help Mexico after failing to win support from Congress for an aid package.
The amendment is retroactive to Jan. 1, and thus would scuttle the loan guarantee package that Mr. Clinton presented on Jan. 31 with the political blessings of the Senate majority leader, Bob Dole, and the House Speaker, Newt Gingrich.
"We're dealing with economic dynamite here," said Senator Paul Simon, an Illinois Democrat. "The very discussion has to be disquieting to a lot of people in the financial markets around the world."
Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin gave just that warning to Mr. D'Amato today.
"I am deeply concerned that the actions you are taking will have the potential to undermine market confidence in international support for Mexico and thereby reduce the likelihood of success," Mr. Rubin wrote in a letter to the Senator. "By limiting the American response to the Mexican crisis, your amendment could threaten the credibility of the stabilization program and undermine the confidence Mexico is trying to restore among investors. These consequences could, in turn, have a negative impact on jobs, wages and prospects of American workers here at home."
But Senator D'Amato was initially unmoved. "We're talking about rescissions of $14 billion and here's $20 billion going to Mexico -- $5 billion has already gone down and another $5 billion plus will go down in the next two or three weeks," he said. "You want to talk about aid? I want to aid the communities that need it. Orange County -- I'd rather give them a loan guarantee with this money. How about the District of Columbia?"
Mr. D'Amato was joined in his effort by nine Republican co-sponsors, including Pete V. Domenici of New Mexico, Jesse Helms of North Carolina and Larry Pressler of South Dakota.
"This may not pass; I think it probably will be defeated," Mr. D'Amato said on the Senate floor. "I understand that there are lots of my colleagues who would just as soon stay out of this situation. They are happy to let the President do it, and if Mexico deteriorates we didn't do anything. But I think it is a question of our constitutional responsibility."
The D'Amato amendment set off a furious round of inside politics.
Fearing that the measure would have the votes to pass, Senate Democrats, led by Senator Christopher J. Dodd of Connecticut, quickly began what the Republicans immediately criticized as a filibuster.
For Mr. Dodd, who is co-chairman of the Democratic National Committee, the fight was as much to protect Mr. Clinton on the Mexico deal as it was a legislative battle.
Senator D'Amato's political maneuvering appeared to fly in the face of the public stance that Mr. Dole had taken on the Mexico deal. Senator Mark O. Hatfield of Oregon, the chairman of the Appropriations Committee who was managing the budget bill on the floor, was flabbergasted and clearly angered by the D'Amato amendment. It suggested that senior Republican leaders were just as surprised as the Democrats by Senator D'Amato's move.
But the Senator, who is leading Mr. Dole's Presidential campaign effort in New York State, said in an interview that he had advised Mr. Dole on Wednesday of his intentions, and that his amendment was completely in line with the majority leader's views.
Only weeks after giving his public support to the Administration's Mexico plan, Mr. Dole said in a speech on the Senate floor on Feb. 24 that after analyzing the agreement, he found it "somewhat surprising and, at its core, disappointing."
Senator Dole put even greater distance between himself and the plan in a letter on March 10 to Secretary Rubin. While Mr. Dole supported the aid package to avoid Mexico's "complete financial collapse," he said in the letter -- a copy of which was provided by Mr. D'Amato -- the majority leader added that "this expression was not intended and should not be construed to convey my blanket support for the underlying policies of the Administration or for the economic and legal agreements implementing these policies."
Senator Dole concluded that he fully supported the "ongoing examination of the important issues raised by the Mexico situation" by the Senate Banking Committee, of which Mr. D'Amato is chairman.
In a telephone interview, Mr. D'Amato said he would be willing to withdraw his amendment to the budget bill but would bring it up again on "another piece of legislation that the President wants."
In further explaining the timing of his legislation, Mr. D'Amato said that he and other members of the Banking Committee and the members of the Energy Committee had met with Administration and intelligence officials on Wednesday for a confidential briefing on Mexico. The Senator said the meeting had left him "deeply concerned about the manner in which this is being administered."
Mr. D'Amato declined to elaborate on what he and his Senate colleagues were told.
In the House, Republican dissidents continued to vex the leadership with demands on the Republicans' five-year, $189 billion tax cut bill, despite assertions by Speaker Gingrich to the contrary.
With a floor vote on the legislation scheduled for next week, each of at least four groups of Republicans has its own gripe about some aspect of the bill. And to varying degrees, each has the potential to block the floor vote by joining with the near-unanimous bloc of 204 House Democrats who oppose the bill.
The leadership is somewhat whipsawed between the groups, because changing the legislation to suit one faction has the potential to upset another.
The central parts of the tax package would lower the capital gains tax rate, wipe out the alternative minimum corporate tax and bestow a $500-per-child tax credit on taxpaying families earning less than $200,000 a year.
One dissident Republican group of uncertain size, led by Representative Pat Roberts of Kansas, seeks to lower the earnings ceiling for the family tax credit from $200,000 to $95,000. But a second group of 37 Republicans, led by Representative John T. Doolittle of California, has sent the leadership a letter that all but threatens to block a vote on the bill if the leaders consent to Mr. Roberts's request.
A third rebel group threatens to oppose a vote because it dislikes a provision that would trim retirement benefits for members of Congress and their staff, making pensions more like those that other Federal workers receive.
And the fourth, most muscular group does not oppose the tax cuts themselves, but wants the bill changed to make them contingent on cutting the Federal budget deficit.
Members of this last group, Representatives Fred Upton of Michigan, Bill Martini of New Jersey and Michael N. Castle of Delaware, met with House leaders Wednesday night and today, and Mr. Upton said later that Mr. Gingrich seemed generally amenable to their notion. But the Speaker and other leaders are balking at the three lawmakers' proposal to set deficit-reduction goals each year and to suspend the tax cuts if the goal is not being met.
Mr. Castle said today that he believes the three have the edge in the debate. "We haven't backed down on anything," he said.
None of the four rebel groups could singlehandedly defeat the tax bill itself. But most have enough members to block a vote by opposing a procedural motion, called the rule, that must be approved by the full House before a final vote can take place.

HEADLINE: If They Play It, Will Fans Come?

BYLINE: By LYNETTE HOLLOWAY 

 BODY:
Kenneth E. Smith sat in his windowless office at Shea Stadium in Queens this week with a telephone receiver at each ear. He had the mustard guy on one end and the nacho guy on the other.
"When can you get that product in?" Mr. Smith asked the mustard guy. "Monday is good. In three weeks, we'll make the conversion from half gallons to gallons. All I want you to do is send the pumps directly to me. Attention: Kenny Smith." 
 Mr. Smith, assistant general manager for the Aramark Concession Company, hung up with the mustard guy and started talking nachos: "We need the warmers, the chips, the cheese and other supplies on Saturday."
Players' strike or not, with the first Mets home game since early August scheduled for next Tuesday, preparations are proceeding for peanut, popcorn, hot dog and pretzel vendors, who count on sports events for their livelihood.
But even as they place their orders for mustard and ketchup and other ball park fare as if it were business as usual, no one knows for sure what kind of opening day it might be, if in fact there is an opening day for the Mets on Tuesday.
The specter of replacement players means lower ticket sales, which means fewer hot dogs. So how much mustard and ketchup do you order?
As Thomas Soloweyko, the souvenir manager at Shea Stadium, put it: "We're in a dither here. We're preparing for opening day with replacement players. If the regular players come back, then we'll have the regular goods come in at a later date. Everybody wishes things were back to normal."
What is normal, for the next few weeks at least, may be determined today. Judge Sonia Sotomayor of United States District Court in Manhattan is scheduled to hold a hearing this morning on the National Labor Relations Board's request for an injunction that would force team owners to restore salary arbitration and competitive bidding for free agents.
If the judge grants the injunction, the players will end the strike; the owners would then have to decide whether to allow them back or lock them out. If the judge doesn't grant the injunction, owners have said they will start the season with replacements.
The season is to open on Sunday with the Mets playing in Florida. Their first home game is scheduled against the Colorado Rockies on Tuesday, with the Yankees and the Minnesota Twins in the Bronx on April 10.
Amid all the possibilities, though, one thing seems sure: there will be no regular players on a New York diamond next week, since they would need several weeks of training first. In the meantime, the two sides continued to negotiate last night in Manhattan.
Advance ticket sales seem to reflect the confusion. Bill Ianniciello, vice president of ticket sales and services for the Mets, said 42,467 fans filled the stadium on opening day last year. Though Mr. Ianniciello would not release sales figures for this year, workers at Shea said they were told to prepare for 10,000 to 12,000 fans on opening day.
To match last year, "we'd have to do a good walk-up sale," Mr. Ianniciello said. Officials at Yankee Stadium said 56,706 tickets were sold for opening day last year. For this year's advance sales, they would say only that tickets are available.
The baseball strike -- it costs the local economy $2 million a game, the City Comptroller's office said -- has been especially painful for small merchants whose businesses stand in the shadow of Yankee Stadium, in the Highbridge section of the Bronx. The uncertainty has been compounded by doubts about whether the Yankees will remain in the embattled South Bronx.
Usually a bustling commercial strip, River Avenue, which runs alongside the stadium, seemed dark and deserted this week, even on a sunny day. Blue steel gates were drawn across the fronts of most of the businesses along the strip, which runs beneath elevated train tracks.
One of the few businesses open on River Avenue this week was the Ball Park Lanes, a bowling alley with a small souvenir stand in front. Some $20,000 worth of merchandise sat in boxes, left over from Aug. 12, when the strike began. John Gianos, the building owner, said the remnants would be put out on opening day, about half the usual stock.
His bowling business, he said, lost $150,000 as a result of the strike, with the souvenir stand down $8,000.
The Wendy's restaurant that had been at 161st Street and River Avenue since 1980 closed in February, said Gary Monie, vice president of Fast Food Systems, a Queens company that owned the franchise. A Popeye's restaurant sharing the space also closed that month.
Mr. Monie estimated that the strike cost him at least 10 percent of his business and helped contribute to its demise.
Stan's Sports Bar and Restaurant -- "Home of the Diehard Yankee Fan" -- the Discount Dugout, the Yankees Eatery and Stadium Souvenir are usually open a few days a week during the winter, according to neighbors, but this year they have been shuttered since August.
Nonetheless, the strip is expected to spring to life on April 10.
A fresh coat of blue paint has been applied to the outside of Yankee Tavern, at 161st Street and Gerard Avenue. Joe Bastone, the owner, said he was planning on five employees for April 10, half his usual opening day staff.
Donna Sheehan, in her second year working at the tavern, said she had lost a quarter of her income because of the strike. "My son doesn't understand that Christmas was smaller than usual because of the strike," said Ms. Sheehan, who has a 10-year-old boy. She doesn't expect a substantial raise. "A lot of baseball fans told me they would never go to another game," she said.
But the Kinney System, which runs the parking garages around Yankee Stadium, was hiring its usual number of attendants for the season, 55 to 65, this week.
"I must be prepared for what will be," said Alex Vincent, Kinney's regional vice president.
At a cavernous, dimly lighted garage at 156th Street and River Avenue, about 60 people lined up to turn in job applications. For them, the return of the baseball season could not have come soon enough.
"I need employment, so I don't care who plays," said Terry Felton, 33, who said he had been unemployed for two years. Raymond Mowatt, 19, agreed. "I haven't had a job in three months," he said.
Inside both Yankee and Shea Stadiums this week, workers bustled as they slapped on new coats of paint, replaced awnings at concession stands, watered the plush lawns and smoothed the infield clay.
Workers at Yankee Stadium unloaded a 16-wheel truck full of concession supplies. Hundreds of silver canisters emblazoned with RC Cola and Sea Breeze punch were stacked on the dock, four deep. A dozen 10-gallon containers filled with "Yankee blue" paint sat in the walkway.
At Shea, the concessionaire prepared for new offerings as well as old. This year new stands include Sbarro's pizza, Carvel ice cream, popcorn, lemonade, buffalo wings and nachos.
Workers completed orders and tallied the ones they had already received, including 1,680 pounds of jumbo hot dogs, 840 pounds of franks, 750 pounds of kosher hot dogs. They will receive cups on Monday because a new design is still being printed. Hot dog rolls will arrive on Monday or Tuesday morning so they remain fresh, said Tim Zorn, a commissary steward.
"The beer is here," Mr. Zorn said. "It started arriving two weeks ago."
The stadiums, in fact, have been careful to stock up on refreshments after the International Brotherhood of Teamsters announced that the union would refuse to cross a baseball players' picket line to make beer deliveries.
"We wanted to start the beer up early," Mr. Zorn said, "because we wanted to avoid picket lines in case there are going to be any."

HEADLINE: Essex County's Bond Rating Is Downgraded

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY 

DATELINE: NEWARK, March 30

 BODY:
The financial crisis in Essex County deepened today when its bond rating was downgraded for the second time in four months, in part because of concern that an austerity plan for layoffs and service cuts would do little to address the county's sustained economic decline.
In taking the action, Moody's Investors Service, a major Wall Street bond-rating concern, said that the county's fiscal health was so poor that its leaders had little room to maneuver. It also questioned whether all the reductions being considered to balance the budget were possible. 
 The cut in the bond rating, from A (average) to Baa1 (below average), came a little more than a week after the new Essex County Executive, James W. Treffinger, presented a $529 million budget that trimmed $66.2 million in annual spending and did not seek an increase in property taxes.
He proposed eliminating 450 jobs, furloughing some workers one day a week and making millions of dollars in other reductions. He said later that he might reconsider the furloughs if other ways can be found to save a similar amount of money.
Mr. Treffinger, a Republican, has blamed the Democrats who have long ruled this county for the budget situation, maintaining that his predecessors had often put off paying their bills. The county's debt has soared during the last 20 years, from $88 per resident in 1979 to an estimated $958 per resident by the end of 1995, according to figures provided by Moody's.
Mr. Treffinger said today that he was not surprised by Moody's decision, which will force the county to pay higher rates of interest when it sells its bonds. He said Moody's was merely confirming what he has said since he came into office: that the Democrats had mismanaged the county.
"No one wants to see a downgrade," he said. "But the way I interpret this is that Moody's has recognized that we were left a mess and that they are hopeful that some of the reforms will be implemented. They see the problem being fixed, but only if very large spending cuts are implemented."
The Democrats who control the Board of Freeholders, the county's governing body, have often criticized Mr. Treffinger over the budget, saying he has manipulated figures to make the Democrats look bad. This afternoon, Joseph DiVincenzo, the Freeholder President, declined to comment on the Moody's action, saying he was still reviewing it.
In December, Moody's lowered Essex County's rating from A1 (above average) to A (average), pointing out that property values in the county were declining while poverty rates were growing. As the county becomes poorer, it said, its bonds are a riskier investment.
Today, Moody's said that Mr. Treffinger had taken appropriate steps so far, but it emphasized that he was restricted in his moves because he did not control all of the county's budget. About 60 percent of the county budget is for items required by the Federal or state governments, including jails, law-enforcement and courts.
That means that if Mr. Treffinger does not raise property taxes, the cuts he can make come out of a smaller pool of money in the county budget, about $225 million.
"The budget as presented by the executive is a very satisfying one from our standpoint," said Paul Devine, a vice president at Moody's. "There are no smoke or mirrors there. On the other hand, we also perceive that the severity of those actions is reflective of a lack of flexibility."
With the downgrading today, Essex County's rating continues a slide that began in the early 1980's, when its rating was AA (high grade). With its new rating, it has the lowest of any county in the state except for Hudson County, which also has Baa1. Hudson, which has more working-class and poor people than Essex, was particularly hard hit by the recession in the early 1990's.

HEADLINE: Back From the Brink, the Legal Services Corporation Discovers It's in Danger Again

BYLINE: By STEPHEN LABATON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
It was a heady reception at the White House last September when old and young civil rights lawyers and judges gathered in the East Room to celebrate the survival of the Legal Services Corporation.
After more than a decade of fighting Republican Administrations that had tried to shutter its doors, the corporation had finally found itself with many supporters sprinkled throughout the highest ranks of power in Washington. Its two strongest allies were in the White House -- a President who had long supported its mission of providing legal services to the poor and downtrodden, and his wife, who had once served as the corporation's leader in the late 1970's, its period of expansion. 
 The agency also enjoyed strong support in Congress, and at the White House ceremony there was talk about dramatically increasing its size.
Now, a mere five months after celebrating its triumph, the agency is again honing its survival skills.
With little notice, the Legal Services Corporation has joined other federally supported programs on the list for drastic cuts or possible elimination by the new Republican Congressional leadership. But unlike the Corporation for Public Broadcasting and the National Endowment for the Arts, which are also targets of cutbacks or cutoffs, Legal Services is being rent by three political forces: fiscal austerity, welfare reform and a powerful new hostility to lawyers in Washington that has inspired conservative lawmakers to promote an agenda aimed at limiting lawsuits brought by the poor or injured.
Prominent Republicans, including Representative John R. Kasich of Ohio, the chairman of the House Budget Committee, and Senator Phil Gramm of Texas, the Republican Presidential aspirant, have begun a loosely organized campaign to eliminate or dramatically curtail the budget and mission of the Legal Services Corporation.
"There is little question they are in danger of not surviving," said Representative Harold Rogers, the Kentucky Republican who heads the House Appropriations subcommittee that oversees the agency's budget and will soon be holding hearings on it. Mr. Rogers, one of the corporation's Republican supporters, said it was likely that the agency's budget will be significantly reduced this year.
Other lawmakers say the reductions will only be the first step.
One of the most powerful Republican House leaders, Mr. Kasich has proposed phasing out all financing for Legal Services over the next five years, part of his plan to cut $100 billion in discretionary spending from the Federal budget.
Across the Capitol, the fate of Legal Services rests largely in the hands of Senator Gramm, a longtime critic. In an interview this week, Mr. Gramm said that he viewed Legal Services as a "major impediment to meaningful welfare reform" and that it could expect significant cuts and possibly elimination.
What irks Mr. Gramm and other conservative lawmakers is Legal Services' lawsuits to gain the rights to welfare for large groups of people in class actions as well as suits that have stopped states from imposing restrictions on welfare benefits. Such precedent-setting cases represent a relatively small amount of the legal work financed by the corporation. But they have had far-reaching consequences, making it harder for states to adopt restrictions that the Democrats had been reluctant to impose. As such, the Legal Services Corporation has emerged as a new symbol to the Republicans of what is wrong with welfare.
"They're being advocates for the existing welfare bureaucracy, and while they may have a right to do it, they don't have a right to do it with taxpayers' money," Mr. Gramm said.
Other critics, such as Ralph Reed, the executive director of the Christian Coalition, argue that the corporation should be abolished because it is undermining American values. In an interview earlier this week, Mr. Reed asserted that Legal Services "subsidizes divorce and illegitimacy" by paying for the representation of indigents in domestic disputes.
In its defense, the lawyers at Legal Services maintain that fewer than one-tenth of 1 percent of all its cases are class actions, and that Congress has already established procedures to insure that class actions are filed only when appropriate. They assert that in welfare cases they do not make the law, but argue on behalf of clients as zealously as they can. The same is true in domestic disputes, they say.
The Legal Services Corporation budget this year is $415 million, which is distributed among some 320 organizations around the nation that supply 4,000 lawyers for a wide array of civil legal matters. (Criminal cases are typically handled by other public defender's organizations.) Its lawyers assist poor people in landlord disputes and domestic strife; help the indigent and elderly obtain Social Security and other entitlements; involve themselves in legal issues affecting the homeless and mentally handicapped, and aid migrant workers and American Indians.
Alexander D. Forger, the president of Legal Services, said that even though the agency had been strongly supported by the American Bar Association and lawyers' groups, the opposition had been formidable. "These people who are fighting us have large contributors and the middle class is angry at us because they don't get free legal services," Mr. Forger said. "We have no contributors. Where is our power base?"
The corporation, an offspring of the Johnson Administration's Great Society programs, was founded in 1974 in one of the final pieces of legislation to be signed by President Richard M. Nixon. It flourished in the Carter Administration, when Mrs. Clinton became its chairwoman, but steadily declined in size in the Reagan and Bush Administrations, as the White House installed Legal Services officers who were openly hostile to its mission.
Although it has made significant inroads in providing lawyers for the poor, its supporters say it has never come close to providing the representation that the poor need. On average, the corporation's officials say it provides one lawyer for every 6,000 to 7,000 indigent clients. By comparison, for the rest of the population, there is an average of one lawyer for every 300 people. Legal Services lawyers earn starting salaries of about $25,000 a year and generally can expect annual pay increases of about $1,500, far below the national average for lawyers.
And although the corporation's budget has been increasing, many of its largest offices report that they are about half the size they once were because Federal contributions have failed to keep pace with inflation. Legal Services provides an average of only about two-thirds of the local legal services' budgets; the rest is donated by the states, law firms and charitable groups.
The decline in Federal support has forced Legal Services to perform the equivalent of legal triage in deciding which matters to take and when. "We used to be able to get involved in the earliest stages of the process -- such as when a client received notice of a foreclosure," said Louis S. Rulli, the head of Community Legal Services Inc. of Philadelphia. "Now we often can only get involved at the very end of the process, when an emergency exists."
Other local units of Legal Services report similiar problems. "We get about 85 calls a day asking for new help, but we can only offer services for about 16 or 17 of them," said Ashley T. Wiltshire Jr. of Legal Services of Middle Tennessee.
Congressional Republicans argue that any Federal money for Legal Services should go in block grants to the states to decide how they want to spend it, as they propose to do with welfare and health care.
But Mr. Forger said that many states in the South and the Rocky Mountain region have never provided money for Legal Services, while others, like Pennsylvania, have recently cut all such financing.
Opponents of Legal Services say the job of representing the poor should be left to various bar associations. But many law firms have been reluctant to provide more free services and many of the nation's poor live where there are few lawyers.
"In North Carolina, for instance, there are broad areas of the eastern plains and western mountains where there is great poverty and very few lawyers," said Richard M. Taylor of Legal services of North Carolina. "Poverty is not distributed where the lawyers are."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Alexander D. Forger, president of the Legal Services, said that even though the agency had been supported by the American Bar Association and lawyers' groups, "where is our power base?" (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times)
 
Chronology: "A 'Perils of Pauline' Tale"
 
1974 -- Legal Services Corporation Act signed by President Richard M. Nixon creasting a federally-funded, nonprofit corporation that would award grants to programs serving the civil lega needs of the poor.
 
1975 -- The first Legal Services Corporation board of directors is confirmed by the Senate.
 
1978 -- Memphis Light, Gas & Water v. Craft, ruled that customers are entitled to due process before their power can be disconnected for nonpayment of disputed bills.
 
1981 -- "Minimum access" goal is briefly reached; Legal Services provides two lawyers for every 10,000 poor people nationally and covers every county in the United States and its territories.
 
1981 -- Congress resists President Ronald Reagan's initiative to eliminate Legal Services, but cuts its 1982 budgeet by 25 percent. The number of Legal Services offices across the country drops to 1,121 from 1,406 in 1980 while the number of lawyers and paralegals drops to 4,766 from 9,416 in 1980.
 
1981 -- The Legal Services board of directors requires grant recipients to work in partnership with the private bar; programs receiving grants must spend a percentage of their grant funds engaging private lawyers (efective 1982) to work on behalf of the poor.
 
1990 -- Sullivan v. Zebley invalidated an improper administrative decision that denied disabled children Supplemental Security Income benefits.
 
1994 -- White House Observance of the 20th anniversary signing of the Legal Services Corporation Act.
 
1995 -- House Budget Committee proposes phasing out funding for the Legal Services Corporation.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
Official Israeli Advice

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

 BODY:
Why has terrorism against Israelis exploded since the signing of peace principles between Israel and the Palestine Liberation Organization on the White House lawn on Sept. 13, 1993?
Is the P.L.O., a collection of groups united by a detestation of Israel and a determination for Palestinian independence, capable of carrying out its commitment to combat anti-Israeli terrorism? 
 Why does its leader, Yasir Arafat, not try to stop it -- to pursue and arrest Palestinians after they kill Israelis, even disarm some before they add to their score of at least 120 Jews since that fine September day?
Will more Israeli concessions of land persuade him to follow the approach of Shimon Peres, Israel's Foreign Minister? It was so neat and simple -- "Why should we chase the terrorists? Let the P.L.O. do it." Why doesn't Mr. Arafat get it?
In the Mideast, there are few more important questions than those. The Israeli Government wants to move to the long-delayed next step in the process -- pulling its army out of some Arab towns, followed by Palestinian elections.
But the Rabin Government fears it will fall if it orders the pullout before it calms Israeli fears by presenting evidence that the P.L.O. will prevent or punish the murder of Israelis.
P.L.O. compliance with its anti-terrorist promises is watched day by day, by those Israelis and foreigners who favor the peace talks and those who think they are a trap for Israel.
So, on March 1, at a Jerusalem meeting with presidents of American Jewish organizations, a top Israeli official was asked a skeptical question about the P.L.O. The gathering got a prompt, straightforward reply: Mind your own business.
Only Israel, said Deputy Foreign Minister Yossi Beilin, would decide whether Mr. Arafat was in compliance with those commitments, not Americans or anybody else.
Mr. Beilin's comments are still ricocheting around American Jewish organizations. The Rabin Government has been consistently and curtly clear that it does not relish advice by non-Israeli Jews.
But still -- some of the organization heads did assume that since Israel had asked the U.S. to lead international economic support for the P.L.O., urged it to get ever more involved in the P.L.O.-Israel talks, and also wanted U.S. troops to be stationed on the Golan Heights if Syria ever deigned to accept it back, why, Americans had some interest in Arab anti-terrorist performance and might be forgiven by Labor if they opened their mouths now and then as they did in the old Likud days.
In fact, Congress has made antiterrorism a condition for aid to the P.L.O., and the Administration must report this spring.
That's a rub, there. Israeli officials want no fuss about compliance lest it mire the talks deeper. And Washington, after taking a major role in the talks and funding of the P.L.O., considers Clintonian prestige committed to momentum between Israel and the P.L.O.
The U.S. has issued an interim report to Congress, which blamed any lack of P.L.O. compliance on inefficiency. The full assessment is due by June 30 under legislation introduced by Senators Richard C. Shelby of Alabama and Arlen Specter of Pennsylvania and Representative Nita M. Lowey of New York.
But the American-Israeli Public Affairs Committee recently published a document that raised questions about P.L.O. compliance. That piece of impudence contributed to the furious Beilin response to the P.L.O. question, which was put at the meeting by Morton Klein, head of the Zionist Organization of America.
Mr. Beilin reminds me that Israel itself must be assessed on its compliance to peace principles. True; since it gave the P.L.O. political parity the Israeli Government bought into moral equivalency.
He says that only his Government can assess P.L.O. compliance to antiterrorism because nobody outside it is privy to secret contacts and understandings it has with the P.L.O. That may not calm Israelis who remember that the recognition of the P.L.O. also resulted from secret Labor-Arafat talks.
Mr. Beilin is highly intelligent but cannot understand why, when he says just trust me, everybody does not sit down and shut up. It's a problem that strikes the best of us sometimes.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH
