HEADLINE: What Became of My Democrats?

BYLINE: By Felix G. Rohatyn; Felix G. Rohatyn is a senior partner at Lazard Freres, investment bankers.

 BODY:
When I landed in this country in 1942, I was 14 and very lucky. I was in America. I could as easily have wound up in Auschwitz. To me, America meant freedom, opportunity and F.D.R. Even though I later made my career on Wall Street -- not exactly a bastion of liberalism in the 50's and 60's -- there was never the slightest question in my mind about my political identifications. I was a Democrat, a liberal, a believer in the New Deal and in government's ability and duty to improve the well-being of its people.
As chairman of New York's Municipal Assistance Corporation from 1975 to 1993 and an adviser to two governors, I began to see the world somewhat differently. Irving Kristol once said that a conservative is a liberal who's been mugged. I was mugged by deficits. I did not change parties or become a social conservative. But I became a fiscal conservative -- a balanced-budget liberal. 
 All during the 1980's, while a Democratic Congress and Republican Presidents ran up $3 trillion in deficits and collapsed the value of the dollar, I continued to believe in the need for fiscal restraint coupled with active government.
When Bill Clinton came along, his philosophy seemed very much along those lines: deficit cuts, investment in the public infrastructure, greater emphasis on education, active involvement in trade and support for conversion of defense industry.
While the commitment to the infrastructure and any serious effort to reshape defense industry has gone by the boards, in its first two years the Administration delivered on some of the main issues that concerned me. I felt, then and now, that it had compiled an important record of economic achievement that is largely unappreciated by the voters. And then the roof caved in last November -- and my political home caved in with it.
As a result of the Republican landslide, both parties (if you can call what is left of the Democrats a party) seem to have adopted the opposite of "balanced-budget liberalism." What they have adopted is "deficit-financed conservatism" -- abdicating Government's responsibilities in order to cut taxes while pretending to balance the budget. To me this is the worst of all combinations: economically untenable in the long run and, in the near future, socially destructive.
I do not believe that "conservatism" or "liberalism" are relevant ideologies now. Given the financial constraints of an advanced industrial democracy in the age of global economics, neither the hard-edged conservatism in fashion here nor European-type welfare states can provide the policies required by the competitive nature of "advanced capitalism." An appropriate mix of fiscal conservatism, a strong private sector and active and supportive government will have to be brought about.
The Administration had the opportunity, after the election, to take the high road economically and the courageous road politically, which would have been consistent with its previous policies. It could have pointed out that cutting taxes while the economy is growing and the Fed is increasing interest rates makes no sense; that reduced interest rates and a strong currency are far more beneficial to Americans than the middle-class tax cut myth; that until the deficit is brought much lower by continued reduction in expenditures and economic growth, it would veto any tax cut, and that it would join with Republicans in a meaningful tax cut when the deficit was nearer to zero and a slowdown in the economy was apparent.
I had hoped the Administration would, at long last, face the reality of the unsustainable growth in entitlement programs such as Medicare and Social Security. None of this has happened. On the contrary. The Administration and the new Republican majority seem to have adopted variations on the same theme: tax cuts financed by unspecified reductions in expenses; more or less punitive forms of welfare reform; more or less drastic reductions in the size of the Government; continued increase in the Federal debt.
The Republican variations are harsher, more drastic and internationally more isolationist in concept. But behind both variations lies an underlying rejection of the need or the capacity of the Government to play a major role in the well-being of most of our citizens. Indeed, wherever possible the responsibilities for more and more of these roles are to be passed backed to the states.
As a result of these approaches, we are about to see the most profound attempt to change the structure of our Government since the Confederacy. The push to send programs such as welfare and Medicaid back to the states is likely to be the start of the dismantling of a social safety net that had evolved since the Depression.
There is no question that many of these programs need major reforms and restructuring. The states should act as laboratories to determine the best approach. But they should not set national policies.
Nor can we expect that the states will carry out Federal responsibilities. The states will cut taxes to attract business, by reducing support to the cities and to the social programs the cities must provide. The cities are at the bottom of the pyramid, and as Washington and the state governments cut taxes, the cities will be left with their problems undiminished and resources shrinking -- a recipe for social disaster.
It is up to the Democratic Party to do something about this, but regrettably it has become a kinder, gentler version of the Republican Party. It no longer stands for a clear set of principles. The Democrats will oppose some of the more extreme Republican proposals on orphanages or child nutrition, but that is not the underpinning of a major political party.
The Democrats were once the party of ideas -- a role now pre-empted by the Republicans. I disagree with many of the Republicans' ideas and much of the ideology. But political power will go to the party with ideas. When one party is bereft of ideas, it does not bode well for the two-party system -- or for the country.
As the most powerful and productive nation in the world, we should be approaching the 21st century with the confidence that it, indeed, will be the American Century. Our policies, however, are going the other way.
By pretending that a Constitutional amendment to balance the budget is fiscal prudence, we are fostering an illusion. By abdicating to the states we are weakening the country. By continuing to overconsume we are starving investment. By refusing to focus more heavily on public education, public investment and public jobs, we are perpetuating an urban underclass that will combine with an educational proletariat, neither of which will have a real place in our society.
The next election should be an argument about those issues, not about affirmative action or abortion. But this debate won't happen unless Democrats recapture their bedrock belief in hope, opportunity, faster growth and higher living standards. It is not too late for Mr. Clinton to provide the leadership to bring this about. Otherwise, the party will become irrelevant and I will, once again, become a political refugee.

GRAPHIC: Drawing.

HEADLINE: BOOKS OF THE TIMES;
The Self and Those Who Tend It

BYLINE: By MICHIKO KAKUTANI 

 BODY:

CONSTRUCTING THE SELF, CONSTRUCTING AMERICA
 A Cultural History of Psychotherapy
 By Philip Cushman
 430 pages. Addison-Wesley. $27.50.
Given the ascendancy of the therapeutic ethos in America today, the expressed aim of Philip Cushman's new book is certainly a useful one: to trace evolving notions of the self in America and to provide a cultural history of psychotherapy as it has developed in this country in recent years. But while "Constructing the Self, Constructing America" is peppered with fascinating insights, the volume is also filled with dogmatic assertions that ultimately color all of the author's arguments. It should not be read as an objective or fair-minded cultural history, but as a highly ideological assessment of American psychotherapy and its social and political implications. 
 A faculty member of the California School of Professional Psychology, Alameda/Berkeley, Mr. Cushman relies heavily upon the theories and methodology of the French philosopher Michel Foucault, and to a somewhat lesser degree upon Marxist-inspired analysis. He is not only intent on situating his subject in a social and economic matrix, but is also eager to interpret any and all developments in terms of shifting vectors of money, power and control.
As Mr. Cushman sees it, "each era has a predominant configuration of the self, a particular foundational set of beliefs about what it means to be human," and the current configuration of the self in contemporary America is "the empty self," characterized "by a pervasive sense of personal emptiness" and committed "to the values of self-liberation through consumption."
"The empty self," he adds, "is the perfect complement to an economy that must stave off economic stagnation by arranging for the continual purchase and consumption of surplus goods. Psychotherapy is the profession responsible for treating the unfortunate personal effects of the empty self without disrupting the economic arrangements of consumerism." By medicalizing such phenomena as "emotional isolation, selfishness, drug addiction and the nihilistic use of others," he argues, psychotherapy effectively depoliticizes them, disguising "the exercise of power, the impact of economic forms of production, and the injustice caused by oppression owing to race, gender and class."
Such assessments, of course, are predicated on the belief that there are no universal illnesses, that there is no shared human condition. And they tend to reduce all human problems to time-bound, socio-economic byproducts, as though selfishness, greed, loneliness and alienation were unknown feelings in Shakespeare's England or Chekhov's Russia, as though such emotions were simply modern, capitalistic afflictions.
When Mr. Cushman stops trying to hammer home his impassioned anti-consumerist message, the results are considerably more convincing. He gives the reader a succinct and relatively readable account of how Freud's theories were adapted from a European context to an American one, evolving from a vaguely threatening theory of sexuality and aggression into a more optimistic and pragmatic course of self-improvement. There are several long, angry digressions about American advertisers' use of Freudian theory to sell products like cigarettes and soft drinks, and repeated asides about the fruitless efforts of Americans to fill up their emotional and spiritual emptiness with food, drugs and an assortment of unnecessary consumer products. Still, the patient reader is also treated to a fascinating account of the different sorts of popular therapies (including mesmerism, the mind cure and positive thinking) that have captured the American imagination at different times.
As for Mr. Cushman's narrative of how America evolved from a small group of colonies, devoted to God and community, into a sprawling, culturally diverse nation filled with therapy-seekers, it is familiar, to say the least, drawing heavily at times on the work of such disparate writers as Christopher Lasch, T. J. Lears and Philip Rieff.
Once again, we are treated to a discussion of the dislocating effects of urbanization, industrialization and secularization; and the moral conflicts engendered by the Indian wars, slavery and the Civil War. Mr. Cushman tells us how the idea of character came to be replaced by personality, how heroes gave way to celebrities, how manual labor was increasingly supplanted by sales. As the country grew more and more secular, he says, as entrepreneurial zeal increased, the search for transcendence was removed from the communal realm and relocated within the individual; a sense of fulfillment became an end in itself instead of a byproduct of communal participation.
Needless to say, Mr. Cushman's discussion of these matters is heavily embroidered with complaints about the empty self and its pivotal role in America's consumer-based economy, complaints that escalate by the end of the book into an out-and-out rant against consumerism.
"Consumerism," he writes, "brought members of the middle class an affluence and psychological-mindedness previously unheard of, but it also caused a commodification of human relating that has been degrading and self-defeating. Finally, it caused the United States to squander precious environmental, financial and human resources in service of wasteful and ultimately destructive campaigns of paranoia, acquisition and consumption."
With such passages, Mr. Cushman signals that he has abandoned all pretense of scholarly detachment, that he has stepped from the realm of history writing into the realm of proselytization.

GRAPHIC: Photo of Philip Cushman. (Addison-Wesley)

HEADLINE: A Surprise German Rate Cut Propels the Dollar Up by 2%

BYLINE: By NATHANIEL C. NASH 

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, March 30

 BODY:
Moving against a powerful surge by the German mark and a punishing fall in the American dollar, Germany's central bank cut key interest rates today by up to half a percentage point.
The rate cut immediately propelled the dollar up more than 2 percent against the mark to 1.4083 marks in New York; it traded as low as 1.3758 in Europe in the early morning. 
 The Bundesbank's move, the first since May, stunned European markets. Traders generally believed that because of fears of inflation the bank would not try to halt the mark's persistent, monthlong climb.
But analysts said the central bank feared that an overvalued mark would make German exports too expensive to compete in world markets and would risk aborting the country's economic recovery that began last year.
"You've had a drumbeat of chief executives recently getting up at their shareholders meetings and saying that a high mark threatens their exports," said Ralf Conen, chief equities analyst at Salomon Brothers in Frankfurt. "We have an export-led recovery, and if you're the Bundesbank you don't want to see this go down the drain because of an overvalued currency."
[Some traders had expected Japan to follow suit. However, the Bank of Japan chose not to cut its discount rate on Friday morning, preferring instead to encourage declines in short-term money market rates, and Japanese stocks fell sharply.]
European stock and bond markets rallied on word of the German cuts. In Germany, the DAX 30 stock index, which had closed down 0.42 at 1,918.46 before the Bundesbank's announcement, only 7.5 points above its 18-month low, surged 30.91 points in after-hours trading to 1,949.76. [Page D6.]
Other currencies also soared, after being beaten down by the mark in the last month, including the British pound, the French franc, the Canadian dollar, the Spanish peseta and the Swedish krona.
Traders warned that the Bundesbank's move alone would not stop the strong anti-dollar sentiment in Europe, where investors are concerned about the effects of the Mexican bailout on the dollar and the inability of Washington to reduce its budget deficit substantially.
"After a while we're headed back down again," said one American trader in Frankfurt. "I think we are seeing a long-term trend here. And a 50-basis-point cut is not going to change that."
A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
The Bundesbank cut its discount rate to 4 percent from 4.5 percent. That is the rate charged on loans used mainly to meet German industry's liquidity needs for up to three months.
The bank cut its rate on repurchase agreements, or repos, to 4.5 percent from 4.85 percent. That is the rate charged commercial banks for short-term borrowing.
The bank left its Lombard rate unchanged at 6 percent. The Lombard rate is roughly equivalent to the Federal Reserve Board's discount rate, which is an overnight rate to banks. The Bundesbank has left this very-short-term rate higher than the other two rates to discourage its use.
Central banks of Switzerland, Austria, Belgium and the Netherlands also cut their lending rates, in line with their policy of mirroring the monetary policies of the Bundesbank to keep their currencies stable against the mark.
While there was speculation that the Bundesbank wanted to help bolster the dollar, German economists said that the action was purely aimed at internal problems.
"The decision has to do with the bank's outlook in domestic terms," said Hermann Remsperger, chief economist at the BHF Bank in Frankfurt. "The domestic scenario gave room for a cut. If there are effects in other countries, that is fine. But the bank is concerned about Germany."
Analysts said today's move was exceptional because it marked one of the few times the Bundesbank had responded to the pressures of the currency markets -- attempting to defend the German economy.
"The danger is that once you start playing this game, the market decides to take you on and see if you will cut rates again if the currency continues to strengthen," said Adrian Cunningham, chief currency economist for the Union Bank of Switzerland in London.
Members of the Bundesbank's council seemed later in the day to try to curb expectations that the rate cut would calm the currency markets and solve the dollar's problems.
"I do not believe our decision can significantly change currency markets," said Hans Tietmeyer, president of the bank. "The currency markets are determined primarily by a judgment of the situation in weak currency countries."
But Finance Minister Theo Waigel was more optimistic. "The measures taken by the Bundesbank will contribute to strengthening the trust in the stability of exchange rates in the markets," he said.
Though most analysts agreed that the bank was reacting to the mark's surging strength, the bank tried to play down its market reaction.
It justified the interest-rate move because its key measure of the German money supply had fallen below the bank's target range. The bank uses a broad money supply measurement called an expanded M-3, which includes cash in circulation, demand and savings deposits at banks and money market accounts. Its yearly range for this M-3 measurement is a growth rate of 4 percent to 6 percent.
It said today that between January 1994 and 1995, it had grown only 2.4 percent, including an uptick in February of seven-tenths of 1 percent. As a result, it said, it could cut interest rates without fearing a surge in inflation.
The central bank also said that because the strong mark made imports cheaper for Germans, inflationary pressures would also be reduced.
"The appreciation of the mark should form a considerable counterweight to the upward pressure on domestic costs and to the price rise for imported raw materials, as well as industrial products," the bank said in its statement.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Securities Prices Retreat After Early Gains

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities slumped yesterday, after a brief bounce from early gains in the dollar.
The dollar surged after the German central bank lowered interest rates and four other European central banks followed suit. A stronger dollar makes securities denominated in it more attractive to foreign investors. 
But the rise in the dollar failed to support Treasury prices. Part of the reason, traders and analysts said, was the concern that lower European interest rates could stimulate global economic growth and accelerate inflation as a result.
"With foreign central banks stimulating their economies, the potential for export growth in the U.S. is keeping the economy moving ahead at a faster-than-sustainable level," said James Hale, a fixed-income analyst with MMS International in San Francisco.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond fell 14/32 , to 10218/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from its price, rose to 7.41 percent, from 7.37 percent on Wednesday. Rates on short-term bills were also higher, up an average of about 4 basis points. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Underscoring investor concern yesterday about economic growth, were two employment reports. The Conference Board said its February index of help-wanted advertising rose to 134 from 129 in January, suggesting there is still demand for workers. And the Government's weekly report on initial jobless claims for the week ended last Saturday fell by 10,000, to 338,000, which was in line with expectations. But it also showed labor strength could accelerate inflation.
Rising prices of precious metals, often viewed as a proxy for inflation expectations, played another factor in undermining the market, Mr. Hale of MMS International said.
Also weighing on the market yesterday, some analysts said, were end-of-the-quarter pressures brought on by managers selling securities to take profits and dress up their portfolios. Traders noted some profit taking in early trading on the bounce in prices on a strong dollar.
The Treasury, meanwhile, sold $42.25 million of short-term debt, with $25 billion of 17-day cash management bills and $17.25 billion of new one-year bills. It brought the total amount of debt sold this week to $69.75 billion, when the regular weekly bill auctions and sales this week of two- and five-year notes are included. Traders said it was a sizable amount of debt for the market to absorb during a week of so much uncertainty and could have also put pressure on prices.
At auction, the new one-year bill was priced at an average rate of 6.02 percent, down from 6.06 percent last month.
The weekly jobless claims data did not draw much notice even as they confirmed the trend of strength in employment.
In the corporate bond market, the Tennessee Valley Authority increased the size of its planned sale of equity-like debt to $600 million from $500 million amid good investor interest.
The securities, which mature in 50 years, are callable in five. The pricing is expected to take place today through underwriters led by Goldman, Sachs & Company.
Following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auctions of a new one-year bill and 17-day cash management bills:

FULTON FIRE: ARSON IS OBVIOUS . . .
Fire Department officials said the blaze that destroyed one of the two main commercial buildings at the Fulton Fish Market on Wednesday had been deliberately set. They said hallway floor had been soaked with a flammable liquid and the sprinklers had been intentionally shut off. A1.

HEADLINE: In Budget War, Pataki and Silver Fight a Battle on Television

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 30

 BODY:
Gov. George E. Pataki and his chief political rival, Assembly Speaker Sheldon Silver, shifted their budget duel out of the back rooms and onto the airwaves tonight, firing bitter partisan salvos at each other in back-to-back, statewide televised addresses.
For Mr. Pataki, a Republican, the air time allowed him to go over the heads of the protesters and lobbyists who have ringed the Capitol in recent weeks and speak directly to the people outside what he calls "the Albany beltway." He argues that most New Yorkers strongly support his proposals to shrink government, reduce welfare and cut taxes. 
 "The Assembly leadership opposes our changes even in the face of a budget deficit of a staggering $5 billion, more than the total budget of 31 other states," Mr. Pataki said in his five-minute address. "This deficit has been caused by endless special interest spending of your tax dollars. It has to stop. It will stop. The special interest groups are going to discover that there is no free lunch for them anymore in Albany."
For Mr. Silver, a Manhattan Democrat, the five-minute rebuttal provided his first opportunity before a statewide audience to counter Mr. Pataki's arguments.
The Governor's budget, he said, finances tax cuts for the rich by cutting health care, transportation and education programs for the middle class. "The difference between George Pataki and his Newt Gingrich approach to governing, and this Democratic Party, is that we are not going to sit idly by and allow the destruction of the middle class so George Pataki and his colleagues can continue their policies of helping the rich get richer," Mr. Silver said.
The speeches gave both men a chance to blame the other for stalling negotiations and virtually assuring that, for the 11th consecutive year, the budget will be passed after the April 1 legal deadline. Both men implored viewers to voice support for their positions in letters and phone calls to the other side.
The dueling addresses cranked up what has already been one of the most intense budget battles in state history. Both sides have spent millions of dollars in television and radio advertising, direct mail campaigns and phone bank operations. Mr. Pataki, whose aides first requested the free air time from television news directors on Wednesday, spoke for five minutes starting at 6:05 P.M., making him among the lead items on newscasts on about 30 stations across the state.
Mr. Silver's rebuttal followed immediately, and most but not all of the television stations carried it. In New York City, for example, Channels 2 and 4 carried both addresses, but Channel 7 carried only the Governor's address.
The last New York State Governor to receive donated air time to make a statewide address was Gov. Hugh L. Carey in 1975, when he implored President Gerald Ford to help New York City avoid bankruptcy.
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani has taken to the airwaves three times in the past 15 months to make direct appeals to viewers, twice to brace New Yorkers for budget cuts and once to endorse former Gov. Mario M. Cuomo. His predecessor, David N. Dinkins, also made three television addresses at times of pressing concerns for the city, two in 1991 dealing with the recession and one in 1992 to calm continuing tensions from the Crown Heights disturbances a year before.
But the addresses tonight were clearly sharper edged, more partisan. Both men have already traveled back and forth across the state attacking the other and seeking support for their positions.
Lee M. Miringoff, director of the Marist Institute for Public Opinion in Poughkeepsie, said he believed the partisan quality of both speeches may have turned voters off to both men's messages.
"Score one for gridlock," he said. "That's what people see when they watch the Governor and Speaker lock horns for 10 minutes."
Aides to Mr. Pataki said the Governor wrote the speech himself, with some polishing from Zenia Mucha, his director for communications, and Robert J. Bellafiore, his press secretary. The live address was broadcast from a Capitol conference room that was set up with a makeshift office complete with a desk and photographs of the Governor's children.
Mr. Pataki, wearing a gray suit and red tie, spoke while seated behind the desk, clasping his hands before him except to make occasional pointing gestures for emphasis.
At its heart, Mr. Pataki's message was that the state's economy will continue to falter unless government spending shrinks and taxes are cut. He said the state has missed a national recovery that has produced 7 million new jobs nationwide since 1991. New York, meanwhile, lost another 125,000, he said.
He argued that his plan to require able-bodied welfare recipients to work would help them become less dependent on government, and he said his proposal to offer managed care for Medicaid recipients would improve their health care. And he accused Mr. Silver of wanting to spend more money on failed welfare policies.
"Speaker Silver wants to increase state spending and keep your taxes the highest in the nation," he said. "That's wrong. I'm asking you to contact Speaker Silver and urge him to pass our budget."
Mr. Silver's counsel, Fred Jacobs, wrote his speech, along with his press secretary, Patricia Lynch. Also dressed in a gray suit and red tie, Mr. Silver spoke from a state television studio situated near the Capitol.
Mr. Silver argued that Mr. Pataki, who won with 49 percent of the vote, did not have a mandate to make such deep spending cuts. He said those cuts would make college too expensive for many middle-class families, cause local property taxes to rise and force elderly people into nursing homes.
"Middle-class New Yorkers are terrified, and they should be," he said.
After the speech, Mr. Silver told reporters that he is willing to pass a budget that spends less than the current year's budget -- something the state has not done in a half-century. This has been one of Mr. Pataki's major goals. Even with that, however, the Legislature would still be able to restore up to $1.1 billion that Mr. Pataki proposes to cut.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Owners Vote to Use Replacements as Talks Go On

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Club owners formally anointed replacement baseball yesterday, hours before the union made a new proposal. But a hearing before a Federal judge today could put the striking players back on the field in the replacements' place.
By a conference-call vote of 26 to 2, the owners authorized the use of replacement players beginning Sunday night, when the regular season is scheduled to open. Only the Baltimore Orioles and Toronto Blue Jays opposed the plan. 
 George Steinbrenner of the Yankees initially voted against the use of replacements, saying it would be a tragic mistake, but subsequently changed his vote, saying he was doing it to support Bud Selig, the acting commissioner and owner of the Milwaukee Brewers.
Negotiators for both sides met for an hour and a half last night, with the union issuing a new offer on a luxury tax. Selig said the owners had accepted the players' offer to return to work under previous terms and conditions of employment, assuming that they return under the owners' definition of previous terms and conditions of employment.
It is the difference of opinion over what those terms and conditions should be that will place the two sides and the National Labor Relations Board before Judge Sonia Sotomayor of United States District Court in Manhattan at 10 A.M. today.
The Mets and Florida Marlins are scheduled to play the first game of the season at 8 P.M. Sunday in Miami. The replacement players will take effect officially at 6 P.M. Sunday, when general managers must submit their 32-man rosters. The owners chose to wait until the last possible minute, wanting to leave as much time as possible to get the dispute resolved.
They have an economic reason to wait, too. Once the season begins with replacements, the clubs will be committed to paying an additional $21.6 million in bonuses to the 864 players the union has called strikebreakers.
While they want an agreement, the owners aren't rooting for the striking players to return to work under an injunction the N.L.R.B. will seek today. The injunction would force the owners to restore salary arbitration, competitive bidding for free agents and the anti-collusion provision of the free-agency rules.
Those weren't the terms Selig had in mind in his letter sent to the office of Donald Fehr, the union leader, while Selig and Fehr were meeting 10 blocks away in the New York law offices of the owners' lawyers.
In what almost seemed like a tongue-in-cheek reply, Selig wrote, "I am delighted that the Union has decided to end its strike, and I hope that we can come to terms on a new long-term Basic Agreement in the near future."
The owners' labor committee, Selig concluded, "will work with you and your staff to arrange for an orderly return to work."
While that letter was being faxed to Fehr's office, Fehr was presenting the union's new proposal to Selig. It lowered the tax-triggering payroll threshold from $54 million in the previous proposal to $50 million but held the tax rate at 25 percent. Had the tax been in effect last year, six clubs would have paid taxes.
Under the owners' latest proposal, 11 clubs would have paid taxes at a rate of 50 percent on portions of payrolls over $44 million. In that proposal Monday night, the owners raised the threshold from $40.7 million but stayed at the rate of 50 percent.
"I said Monday the clubs strained to make that proposal," Selig said at a news conference. "But we now have been given a counterproposal and we'll digest that proposal."
In another part of the tax plan separating the sides, the owners want the tax to be in effect for the last five years of a six-year agreement. The players proposed no tax the first and fifth years of a five-year agreement.
N.L.R.B. lawyers have urged Sotomayor to rule before the season opens, but the owners' lawyers have called the Sunday deadline arbitrary, pointless and legally irrelevant. There is no way of telling when the judge might rule.
Selig's letter aside, if the judge grants the injunction, the owners would have to decide whether to allow the players to return or lock them out.
With increasing frequency, people familiar with the thinking of the owners and their lawyers have said this week that the owners don't have the 21 votes needed to approve a lockout and the lawyers are advising against it because of the legal risks.
The owners have not indicated if they would play replacement games for three weeks while the returning players got in shape if that scenario developed.
During the owners' conference call yesterday afternoon, said a person who listened in, the owners did not discuss the players' offer to end their strike or a possible lockout. "The word lockout was never used," the person said. Only slight mention was made of the court hearing, the person added.
The Orioles, who do not intend to form a replacement team, tried to have the vote on replacements tabled until Saturday, but the motion was defeated. Then the vote on their use was taken, and the outcome was 25-3.
Steinbrenner, in voting no, said he wanted to be on record that it was a tragic mistake in the long run to go ahead with the plan. He said, however, that if it were adopted, he would make every effort to make it work.
Toward the end of the call, which lasted about an hour, Steinbrenner said to Selig that if it would benefit him to have the vote unanimous, he would be happy to change his vote. But the Blue Jays and the Orioles stuck to their positions. Peter Angelos, the Orioles' owner, was not on the call but was represented by club officials.
Steinbrenner then told Selig to do what he wanted with his vote. Selig responded that Steinbrenner would have to make that call, and the Yankees' owner said, "O.K., I'll go with you."

SECTION: Section D; Page 6; Column 4; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 380 words

HEADLINE: No Japan Move; Stocks Off

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Friday, March 31

 BODY:
Despite widespread anticipation that it would follow the lead of Germany's central bank, the Bank of Japan chose not to cut its official discount rate today, saying instead that it would nudge short-term money market rates lower. The decision caused prices to fall sharply on the Tokyo stock market, where traders had been expecting the discount rate to be cut.
The central bank said in a statement that partly because of the yen's sharp appreciation against the dollar, the gradual recovery of Japan's weak economy now "appears to have somewhat slowed down." 
 Analysts said this negative sentiment was paving the way for an eventual cut in the discount rate, which is already at a record low of 1.75 percent.
"The currency market move has darkened the economic prospects rather seriously," said Robert Alan Feldman, director of economic research for Salomon Brothers here. "When there is evidence to show that conclusively, then the B.O.J. will move."
Stocks in Tokyo initially surged in reaction to the German rate cut and on anticipation that the Bank of Japan might follow suit. But stocks soon reversed course, with the Nikkei average of 225 issues falling sharply in the afternoon and closing at at 16,139.95, down 372.27 points, or 2.25 percent.
The currency markets did not react as strongly. In Tokyo this afternoon, the dollar was quoted at 89.09 yen, down from 89.55 in New York late Thursday.
The Bank of Japan has consistently said its monetary policy is guided by concern for Japan's economy and is not conducted to influence currency exchange rates. Thus, today's move dampened speculation that the United States, Germany and Japan would make coordinated interest rate moves to help bolster the dollar.
Today's announcement will affect the overnight call rate at which banks make loans to one another. The overnight rate fell today to 1.75 percent, the same as the discount rate, compared with about 2.20 percent in the last few days.
By guiding those rates lower, the central bank is telling the market that it is monitoring the situation but not yet ready to lower the discount rate. Tod Wood, economist at Baring Securities here, said the change in the overnight call rates was "not a large enough change to affect the yen or the economy."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
SUNBEAM-OSTER STOCK FALLS ON EARNINGS UNEASE

BYLINE: Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The stock of the Sunbeam-Oster Company fell yesterday after the company, which makes blenders and other consumer goods, said it expected first-quarter earnings to fall below analysts' estimates of 39 cents a share. The stock closed down $1, to $23, on the New York Stock Exchange. Sunbeam-Oster, of Fort Lauderdale, Fla., said it expected first-quarter net income of 35 cents to 37 cents a share, up from 34 cents a year earlier. Revenue is expected to increase by 6 percent to 8 percent. The company did not specifically say why results would fall below expectations, but said Mexican sales will be lower this quarter than a year ago because of poor economic conditions.

SECTION: Section A; Page 30; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 575 words

HEADLINE: Budget Brinkmanship in Albany

 BODY:
It was no mystery why Gov. George Pataki went on television last night to try to rally support for his sweeping budget cutbacks. Opinion polls show that New Yorkers are alarmed by the pain his budget would inflict. Led by the Assembly Speaker, Sheldon Silver, Democrats would rather delay passage of a budget than accede to one that does so much damage. If Mr. Pataki wants a timely budget, he will have to compromise.
It is an old Albany axiom that the party that cares less about getting a rapid agreement ends up getting more out of the eventual deal. Mr. Pataki needs a budget agreement right now to establish his credentials as an effective Governor. An impasse does not redound to his credit. 
 The Governor has already compromised by wisely retreating from his earlier threat to close down the state government tomorrow if the Legislature fails to enact a budget by then. He apparently recognized that he would have taken the blame for punishing innocent state workers, creating legal and logistical chaos throughout the state and wrecking the atmosphere for negotiations on the budget. The same concern for the well-being of the state that led him to that compromise can guide him again now.
In his television address, Mr. Pataki effectively stated the broad conservative themes that helped get him elected last fall. He is right to insist that the cost of government be controlled and that the state needs long-term tax relief to help restore its economy. But the speech failed to allay concerns that his budget tries to do too much too quickly. The $5 billion in budget cuts introduced in January illustrate the political perils of moving from the campaign slogans repeated last night about painlessly reducing the size of government to programmatic specifics. Even the Senate Republicans with whom he is allied favor relief for hospitals threatened with being shut down and college students faced with steep tuition hikes.
Mr. Silver, responding to the Governor's speech with an appeal of his own, argued that the Governor's budget is an assault on the poor and middle class, and that the proposed tax cuts would help the rich. He embraces the restorations accepted in the State Senate and would go further, restoring cuts in Medicaid, welfare, mental health and programs for children. The Governor is understandably frustrated that Speaker Silver has avoided specifying exactly what he wants restored and where he would get the money to pay for it. The most obvious place to look for the revenues is in a scaled-back or deferred tax cut for next year.
After only three months in office, Mr. Pataki can already claim credit for changing the terms of debate in New York politics. He has forced everyone to discuss not whether but how much to cut spending. Few doubt that his extremely harsh cutbacks were intended as an opening proposal and that compromise will come sooner or later. Mr. Pataki can therefore compromise while still claiming a victory of principle and then turn to the job of privatizing state services and finding other ways to cut costs.
The fiscal year ends at midnight tonight, and the state cannot possibly enact a budget by then. Hopes are gone for approving the first budget on time in more than a decade. But there is still time for everyone to reach a broad agreement on principles signaling that the state is prepared to live within its means and still protect the sick, the elderly and the young.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial
